FLOVRES FOR LATIN SPEAKING SELECTED AND gathered out of Terence, and the
same translated in to English, together with the exposition and setting
forth as well of such latyne words, as were thought needful to be annoted,
as also of diverse grammatical rules, very profitable and necessary for the
expedite knowledge in the latin tongue: Compiled by Nicolas Vdall.

Nicolai Vdalli carmen end casyllabum ad libellum suum.
SI certum est tibi pertinax libelle
In multas hominum manus uenire,
Doctorumque libet subire nasum,
Sannas, uerbera, iurgium, cachinnos,
Perme sit tibi liberum uagari.
I quocunque uoles tuo periclo.
Securus poteras domi latere,
Et mecum poteras manner tutus.
Nunc es publicus, haud meus libellus.
Nec possum tibi iam patrocinari,
Nec suffragi, gratiamque uulgi,
Aut uitam, geniumque polliceri.
Orbis sed tibi, multitudinis que
Standum iudicio, uel est cadendum.
Ioannis Lelandi Londinensis decastichon.
Ca^didus exacta^ monstrare Tere^tius arte^
Eloquij nouit Roma diserta tui.
Illius ex horto flores selegit amoenos
Vdallus cupidae sedulus instar apis.
Quoque labor pueris studiosis gratior esset,
Transtulit in patrios uerba latina sonos.
Insuper et scholion, facu^dae munera linguae,
Addidit, aeterna uiuere digna cedro.
Vos igitur iuuenes Vdallu^ ornate Britanni.
Sic fluat e uestro comicus ore lepos.
Edmundi Ionsoni carmen in laudem operis.
Barbary lingua^ pulsa qui scire latina^,
Quique breui fieri te^pore doctus aues,
Hu^c lege Romani flores sermonis habente^
Non alius quales hortulus edit opes.
Hunc lege, qui lepidi uerissima sensa Tere^ti
Promit, et obscuros explicat art locos.
Hu^c lege, quam nitidus, limatus, dulcis amoenus
Verba loqui ueteru^ more latina docet.
Vdallo meritas tali pro munere grates.
Aeternum digno uiuere, lector age.
Nicolaus Vdallus suauissimo discipulorum suorum gre^gi salutem plurimam dicit.
QVANQVAM initio quidem, ut ingenue fatear, non mea uoluntate ad hanc docendi
prouinciam capessenda^ inductus, sed partim assiduis, eisdemque importunissimis
amicorum quorunda^ solicitationihus efflagitationihus que compulsus, partim
nescio quo meo fato protrusus uideri poteram, nunc tamen, postquam egregia
quorundam uestrum indoles luculentam sane minime que duhitatam spem ostendit,
fore aliquando, ut ad insignem aliquam eruditionem euadatis, tantum abest, ut
me instituti, coeptique poeniteat, ut nihil profecto hodie prius habeam, aut
antiquius. Quapropter quam sort nactus sum spartam, ornare, prout Graecorum
prouerbiu^ admonet, mihi proposui, planeque constitutum habeo, superis been
iuuantibus, in hanc gnauiter curam incumbere, insigniterque in eo elaborare, ut
uos ex immanissimis barbariei faucibus quamprimum eripiam, atque ex tenebrosa
obstrusaque inscitiae caligine uindicem, ad politioris literaturae puritatem,
lucem, claritudine^. Nec dici me hercule potest, qua^to desyderio flagrem,
quantaque quum omnium qui literis sunt dediti, tum uestris, duntaxat honestis
et rectis, studijs proficiendi cupiditate ardeam. Iam inde enim ab eo tempore,
quo in meam uos scholam, et disciplinam recaepi, parentis erga uos animum
induisse me fateor, semperque existimasse in eorundem me locum succedere, a
quibus traditi mihi ad instituendum estis. Quod cum ita sit, ut est, quam mihi
rem gratam, et iucundam, quam porro laetabilem, et gloriosam putatis fore, si
uos aliquando ad eas in disciplina uires accrescere, et peruenire uidero, ut
exuperatis euictisque istis grammaticarum praeceptionum, et rudimentorum
difficultatibus, ac uelut salebris, ad iucundissimam, ea^demque multo uberrimi
fructus latinorum authorum lectionem studium transfer ualeatis. Ad hanc autem
maturitatem cum animaduerterem non alia demum ratione perueniri posse, nisi si
quis in promptu iam ante, et ad manum habeat bonam, atque adeo diuitem latini
sermonis supellectilem, sedulo equidem mihi faciundum putaui, ne uobis deesset
unde ea potissime facultas parari possit, et latini sermonis copia, puritas
nitor, elegantia, nullo, aut certe quamminimo negotio perdisci. Scripsi itaque
uobis suauissimi tyrunculi quasdam latin loque^di formulas, ad cotidiani
sermonis usum et copiam sane quamaccommodatissimas. Eas uero ex Publii Terentij
potissimum comoedijs delegimus, quod is scriptor ad informandam, instruendam
que linguam puerilem maxim omniu^ idoneus, citraque controuersiam facile
princeps uideatur, utpote per quem ipsum Tullium Ciceronem, summum alioqui
latinitatis magistrum, ad tantu^ eloquentiae fastigium profecisse constet, id
quod quum ipsemet de se fatetur alicubi, imo potius gloriatur, tum scripta eius
ubique tantum non clamant. Porro latina ipse anglice interpretatus sum, quo uos
quoque latina uernacule, aut e contrario latin uernacula absque molestia uel
negotio, et cum aliqua ratione ac gratia, nec interim ineptis prorsus atque
absurdis, quod plaerique faciunt, sed appositis et accommodatis uerbis reddere
addiscatis. Neque uero putetis uelim nullum esse operae precium, si quis latina
apt in maternum uertere sermonem calleat. Nam si Cicero quaedam ex Graecis tam
poetis, quam oratoribus, ac philosophis, latin interpreta^do, quae in suos
referret libros operae precium existimare se fecisse non nusquam gloriatur, si
Terentius plus sibi laudis statuit, maioremque gloriam posuit in uertendis
Graecorum antiquis fabulis, quam inueniendis suis nouis, si ide^ Terentius rem
nihilo minus ingeniosam arbitratus est ex bonis graecis bona latina facere,
quam si ipse de suo noua excogitasset, quae scriberet, et posteris legenda
traderet, si denique ingenij argumentum, et non postremae laudis opus
existimatur, bona uernacula sic uerbis latinis mutare, ut interim seruetur
utriusque linguae idiotismus, et gratia, qui minus id quoque e diuerso maximae
laudi dari par sit, et uel summi artificis opus haberi conueniat, ex bonis
latinis scilicet reddere bona uernacula? Verum, ut redeam qui coepi tendere,
nec iusto diutius in hijs haeream, imo consilij uobis factique mei rationem
compendio expediam, addidi, sicubi opus id esse uidebatur quaedam uelut
scholia, quibus tum sensus poetae explicetur, tum uerba ipsa non paulo
declarentur apertius. Si qua insignis aut elegans incidit metaphora, indicaui.
Si qua figura occurrit, admonui. Si qua fabula interuenit, non sum grauatus
altiuscule repetitam narrare. Si quid quod ad latinitatem egregie faceret, sese
obtulit, non commisi, ut praeteriretur silentio. Si quid ad rationem
grammaticam pertinere uisum est, non piguit enucleare. Si quid prouerbij
interspersum est, exposui. Si quod uocabulu^ obscurius iudicatu^ est,
illustraui. Si qua formula a communi, uulgarique, et usitata latin loque^di
ratione paulo alienior apparuit, rationem, reddidi, citatis, ubi res posceret,
atque adhibitis ex optimis quibusque et probatissimis authorihus exemplis ac
testimonijs. Denique ut finem tandem faciam, quicquid usquam obiectum est, quod
puerile ingenium iudiciumue retardare in legendo posse uidererur, quantumuis id
humile foret aut leave, sedulo adnotaui, uestris scilicet studijs quamoptime
consultum cupiens, omnibusque omnium uestrum commodis nusquam non libentissime
deseruiens. Proinde haec quidem, quae commemoraui, omnia, pingui, quod dicitur,
crassaque ac rudi Minerua tradidi, et uereor, ne quorundam opinione, iudicio,
sententiaque, nimis etiamnu^ anxie, nimis scrupulose, nimis denique, ut it a
loquar, frustulatim persecutus sim, utique dum singula ad iudicij uestri
immaturitatem, et captus teneritudinem attempero. Sed nimisquam mihi, in
hocduntaxat negotio, placuit illud, quod dici solet, indoctius modo apertius,
praesertim, cum scirem quam maxim opus esset, nil nisi uelut praemansum uobis
in os inseri. Porro ipsum opusculu^ FLORES TERENTII libuit inscribere, quoniam
hae formulae, quas uobis iam nuncupamus, ex eius poetae lepidissimis iuxta
atque elegantissimis comoedijs, quasi horto quodam fragrantissimo, et ad
miraculum uario, amoenoque topiario, ordine omnes selectae sunt, et excerptae.
Hiis igitur laborum nostrorum quasi primitijs fruimini, ta^quam arrabone, ac
pignore tum nostri erga uos summi amoris, tum officij ac fidei. Quodsi prodesse
haec conducereque uestris studijs intellexerimus, alia, fauente Christo,
dabimus propediem altiora, at que maiora. Vestrae autem parts erunt suauissimi
tyrones, omni, quod aiunt, pede stare, omnibus ingenij neruis contendere, ac
modis omnibus curare, ne nostros sudores laboresque frustremini, ne ue nostrum
hoc tantum oleum et operam eludatis, sed potius, ut conceptae de uobis tum
spei, tum expectationi possitis per omnia respondere. Valete.

FINIS INDICIS.
Out of Andria in the Prologue.
ANimum ad scribendum appulit, He applied his mind to wrytinge.

Id solum negotij credidit sibi dari: He thought he should have had no further
busynes but that.

Multo aliter euenire intelligit. He perceyueth hit much otherwise to chance
or to comme to pass.

Animum aduortite. Take heed, or set your minds hereto and harken.

In the first act and first Scene of the same.
Istaec intro auferte. Have in this gear.

Adesdum. Come hither.

Paucis te uolo. I would speak a word or two with you.

Curenter recte haec. Let these things well done.

Expecto quid uelis. I would fain know what your will, or pleasure is, Or I
long, or desire to know your mind, will, or pleasure.

In memoria habeo. I remember it well, or I bear it well in mind.

Habeo gratiam. I thank you.

Mihi hoc molestum est. This greueth me.

Quin tu uno uerbo dic. But say on at ones or quyckely.

Quid est quod me uelis? What is hit that you would with me?

Rem omnem a principio audies, You shall here all the matter even from the
beginning.

Excessit ex ephaebis. He is paste childehode, Or, he wexeth a man, or he
grows to man's state.

Qui scire posses? How might a man know?

Bona uerba queso. Speak fair I pray you, or prouerbially, You woll not do as

Out of the Second Act
In the first Scene.
Quid ais? what say thou?

Illa datur hodie Pamphilo nuptum. She shall be married unto Pamphilus this day.

Sic est. It is even so, or, ye forsothe.

Apud forum modo e Dauo audiui. I herd it abroad right now of Dauus. Forum is

gether, as in London, Lumbardestrete, or westmynster halle: Forum is not latin
for a marie or markette, except sum word of such signification be added

carnarium, where flesh was sold, Forum piscarium, where fysshe was sold.

frendshyppe begone of little ones has grown and increased together with our
years and age.

Per unica^ gnata^ tua^, et gnatu^ meu^. I pray you as you love your only
daughter and mi son. Seruandi mei summa tibi potestas datur. It lyethe very
much in your hondes to save my life.

Me adiuues in hac re. Help me in this matter. Ah ne me tantopere obsecra.
Tusshe pray not me so instantly.

Alium esse censes nunc me atque olim fui? Do you think me an other manner
manne now thanne I have been in time paste? or heretofore.

Si in rem est utrique. If it be for the profit of us both.

Si ex ea re plus mali est quam commodi utrique. If there shall come more hurt
or displesure unto us both than profit thereby.

Accersi iube. Comma^de him to be gone for or bid one go call him.

Te oro, in come ut co^sulas. I pray you look on ye matter indiffere^tli for us
both, or I prai you do indifferently for both parts.

Ita postulo ut fiat. I desire you that hit may so be.

Non postulem abs te, nisi ipsa res moneat. I would not desire it of you, but
that the thing self does bid me and give occasion.

Quid est? what is the matier?

Irae sunt inter Glycerium et gnatum. Glycerie and my son be fallen out.

Spero posse auelli. I trust he may be pulled away from it.

Profecto sic est. Undoubtedly it is so.

Anteeamus, dum tempus datur. Let us preuente the mattier, while we have time.

Lachrymae confictae dolis reduc unt animu^ aegrotum ad misericordiam. Tears
deceit fully feyned do bring a love sick mind again to pity and compassion.

Spaero illum dehinc facile ex illis sese emer surum malis. I trust that he
woll from hens forth easily wind him self out of tho myschieffes.

Coniugio liberali deuictus. Bouude in honest matrimony, or wedlock.

Tibi ita uidetur. So thinks you.

Non arbitror me posse perpeti. I think I shall never be able to endure it.

Qui scis istuc nisi periculum feceris? How know you that, except, or until
you have proved hit.

Incommoditas denique huc omnis redit. All the displeasure or the worst of the
matter at the vttermoste comes but unto this point.

Dij prohibeant. God forbede.

Quid istuc? what is that?

Si istuc animum in duxisti esse utile. If you think in your mind that this
is expedient.

Nolo tibi ullum co^modum in me claudier. I woll not that any commodity or
pleasure be debarred or stopped from you by me.

Merito te semper maximi feci. I have not without cause always set very
much by you.

Qui scis eos nunc discordare inter se? How know you, that they are now
fallen at debate?

Intimus eorum consilijs. Chief of their counsel, or, of their priuey and
secret counsel.

Is mihi persuadet, quantum queam, ut maturam. He cousaileth me in any wise,
that I make all the speed I can.

Number censes faceret, nisi sciret filium haec eadem uelle? would he have so done
think you, but he knew that my son desyrethe the same?

Tute adeo iam eius uerba audies. Your self shall here him speak by and by.

Heus euocare huc Dauum. How syrs call me out Dauus hither.

Eccum, iudeo ipsum for as exire. Lo yo^der I se him self cominge forth of the
doors.

In the fourth Scene.
Ad te ibam. I was cominge to you.

Cur uxor non accercitur? why is not Pamphilus wife sent for?

Ia^ aduesperascit. It is almost night already.

Audin tu illum? Do thou here him?

No^nihil ueritus sum abs te ne dolis me deluderes. I fered greatly on thy
part of behalf, that thou woldeste have deluded or mocked me by some
subtilte, craft, or gyle.

Ego istuc facerem? Should I have done such a deed?

Vos celaui, quod nunc dicam. I have kept from you that that I wol now show
you, or, I have kept priuey from you that, that I wol now disclose or open.

Propemodum habeo iam tibi fidem. I begin in manner now to trust the.

Tandem cognosti, qui siem. At last you know what manner a man I am.

Ea gratia simulaui, ut uos temptarem. I no thing but feyned, to thentent that
I might thoroughly prove your minds.

Sic res est. It is even so, or, so is the matter: or, so stands the case,
or, thus the matter goes.

Nunqua^ quiui ego istuc intelligere. I could never perceyne that.

Vah consilium callidum. Oh subtle devise.

Vt hinc te iussi introire oportune hic fit mihi obuia^. After that I had bed
the go in this man met me happily, or, as well as could be.

Hem, numnam perijmus? Ah well, are we not in ill case trow we? or be we not in
the briers, or in the dyche?

Quidnam audio? what do I here?

Oro uixque exoro. I prayde him, and with much ado intreted him, or obtained
of him.

Occidi. I am undone, or my joy is paste in this world, or, my welthe is at
an end, or my good days are paste.

Hem quid dixti? Ah well what saydst thou?

Optime factu^. Passing well, or very well done

Nunc per hu^c nulla est mora. Now on his behalf is no let nor delay.

Quoniam solus mihi hoc effecisti. For as much as only thou and no man else
haste brought this to pass, or haste brought this about for me.

Corrigere mihi gnatum enitere. Labor to amend, or to bring into the
straight way again, or, to bring to goodness my son.

Faciam hercle sedulo. I wol do my diligence

Animus mihi irritatus est. My stomach is incensed, or thoroughly angered.

Mirum ni domi est. It is meruaylle but he is at home.

Nullus sum. I am undone, or, I were as good be out of the world, or, I were
as good be deed.

Quid causae est, quin hinc in pistrinum recta proficiscar uia? what reasonable
excuse may I make, but that I must go streighte from hens into prison? or,
what color or pretext may I allege or lay for my self, why I should not go
from hens straight into prison? Pistrinum was a place where the corn was
ground and bredde made: and it is deriuied of pinso, sis, sui, situm, uel
pistum, which is either to grynd with mylstones or to beat to meal with a
pestle in a mortar, as (for lack of mylles) men in old time did: and because
it was a very peynefull thing, and full of labor, many used for punyshement
of their servants to put them to that office, which was to them a sore and
grievous punishment and enprisonment.

Nihil est praeci loci relictu^. Prayinge may have no place: or, it is paste
entreatynge.

Perturbaui omnia. I have troubled all together, or as we say prouerbially. I
have stered the cooles, or I have made the pot sethe, or, my self have
brought all out of frame, or in the brier^*

Herum fefelli. I have beguyled my master or, I have played the false fellow
with my master.

Feci inspaerante Simone, atque inuito Pamphilo. I have done it Simo nothing
lokyng for it, and clean contrary to the mind and will of Pamphilus.

Quod si quieuissem nihil euenisset mali. That if I had sytte in rest, there
should no ill nor harm have chanced at all.

Eccum ipsum uideo. Lo yon I se him.

Vtinam mihi esset aliquid hic, quo nunc me precipitem darem. Would I had some
place here, that I might now cast my self down heedlonge.

In the fyfthe Scene.
Vbi illic scelus est qui me perdidit? Where is that ungracious knave that has
undone me, or that has brought me in the briers?

Perij. I am undone, or, I am but deed, or I am utterly cast away, or, god
have mercy upon me: with all other englyshes of occidi. 13. and Nullus sum.
20. in the Scene next before this.

Confiteor hoc mihi iure obtingisse. I grant this to have rightfully chanced
unto me.

Qua^do quidem ta^ iners tamque nullius consilij sum. for because I am so
foolysshe or dulle, and have no cast in the world, nor discretion nor wit.

Seruone fortunas meas me commisisse futili. To have put and committed all that I have unto a light prating or bablyng knave. for there is understood
oportuit or decuit as says Linacer in his sixth book entitled De figuris
constructionis. And Melancthon in his Syntaxi notes such manner of spekinges by
indignation, asking a question to be commonly pronounced and spoken by the
infinitife mode absolutely, and so use we to speak also in our english
speech. Hic et haec futilis et hoc futile, is he that can keep nothing secret
but is as a vessel full of chynnes, and (as Appuleius says) Leuis homo et
futilis, ac rimarum plenus.

Praecium ob stultitiam fero. I am served even like a fool. or, I am served
well enough for my foolishenes.

Inultum id nunquam auferet. He shall never escape unpunished for it: or, I
shall assuredly requyte hit him. or, he shall surely abye for it.

Posthac incolumem sat scio fore me, nunc si deuito hoc malu^. I know right
well, that I shall do well enough from hensforthe, if I may now escape this
myschefe.

Qua fiducia id facere audeam? How may I be so bold as to do that?

Quid me faciam nescio. I can not tell what to do with my self.

Id ago sedulo. Theraboute I goo as fast as I can.

Huic malo aliquam producam moram. I will delay or prolonge or put of, or
drive forth my punysshment yet a little while.

Elio dum bone uir, quid ais? Oh come hither gentylman, how say you?

Viden me tuis consilijs impeditum esse? Do thou not se me brought in the
briers through thy devise and counsel?

At iam expediam. But I wol bring you out again shortly.

Tibi ego credam furcifer? should I give credence unto the noughty knave, or,
villayne?

Tu rem impedita^ et perdita^ restitues? wolte thou recover a thing so
encombred and so far gone? or wolt thou recover a thing inta^gled, intricate,

Hem, quo fretus sie^. Lo what a sure spear I have of the* or, what a sure man
I have of the to bear me bold on.

Me hodie ex tranquillissima re coniecisti in maximas turbas. Thou haste now
cast me out of meruaylous great quietness into very great trouble.

An non dixi hoc esse futurum? Did not I say that hit would be thus. Or, said
not I that hit would comme thus to pass, or to this point.

Quid meritus es? What haste thou deserved, or, what art thou worthy to have?

Crucem. Hanging.

Sine ad me ut redeam. Suffer me to gather my wits unto me again.

No^ habeo spatium ut de te sumam supplicium ut uolo. I have no leisure to
punysshe the as I would do.

Hoc tempus me haud sinit te ulcisci. The time woll not suffer me now to be
revenged on the, or, I have no leisure now to be revenged on the.

Out of the fourth Act
In the first Scene.
Id ne est uerum? Is that true?

Id genus hominum pessimu^ est. That sort of men be very ill.

Tempus est promissa perfici. Hit is time that all promyses be fulfilled and
kept, or, it is time that all couenantis be performed.

Tum necessario se aperiunt. Thanne they must needs show what they be, than
do they without remedy utter them selfs.

Res cogit me denegare. The thing self causes me to deny it.

Impudentissima eoru^ ratio est. Their words are without all shame.

Quis tu es? Who be you I pray you?

Quis tu mihi es? whatte good master of mine are you? or, what do thou for
me, that I should be bound of duty to do for the?

So are these words taken here in this place of Terence, and in that sense
used.

Proximus sum egomet mihi. I must be best friend unto my self, or I must serve
mine own self first, or, I love you well, but I love my self better, or,
every manne for him self.

Vbi fides est? where is honesty now adays? or, where is truth or faithfulness
of promis.

Nihil eum pudet. He is ashamed of nothing, or there is no shame in him.

Quid agam? what may I do?

A deamne ad eu^? Is it best I go unto him?

Cum eo ne iniuriam hanc expostulem? Is it best I chyde with him, or take him
for this displeasure or not.

Ingeram ei mala multa. I shall multiply and give him many shrewde words. or,
I shall give him many checkes, or, I shall lay many things to his charge.

Nihil promoueris. You shall be never the nere, or, it shall nothing avail you.

Multum molestus illi fuero. I will vex him a little, prouerbially, or, I
woll anger every vein in his heart.

Animo morem gessero. I woll satisfy my mind, or, I wol ease my heart or
stomach, or, I wol follow mine appetite.

Et me et te imprudens, nisi quid dij recipient, perdidi. I have undone or
cast away both my selffe and also you unware, if god help not, or except
god find some provision.

Soluisti fidem. You have broken promise.

Quid tandem? Why, what now?

Etiam nunc me subducere istis dictis postulas. Thou would yet again*
deceive me with thy words.

Subducere has diverse sygnifycations, emonges other it is to convey away
privily. But here hit is taken for the Greek word hypagin, which is syllable
for syllable subducere, and that vocable the greeks use figurately for fallere
that is to deceive, and so is subducere used also in that the comedy of
Plantus entitled Menechmi. Rapide raptorem pueri sub duxit fides.

Tuum animum ex animo spectaui meo. I have espied thy mind by mine own.

Falsa me spe producis. Thou leadest me, or fedest me forth with a false or
vain hope.

Ah nescis quantis in malis uerser miser. Ah thou know not in how great
distres I am, unfortunate man that I am. or. thou canste not tell in what
myserie I am poor soul or unhappy man that I am.

Ah quantas mihi tuis consiliis confecisti sollicitudines? O what care thou
have wrought me, through thy means or advise, or, counsel?

Hic meus est carnifex. This is my scourge or tourmentour.

Quid istuc tam mirum est de te exemplum capit? What great wonder is that? he
takes example of the.

Haud istuc dicas, si me cognoris. You would not say so if you knew me well.

Cum patre altercasti dudu^. You multiplied words with your father right
now, or ere while.

Is uu^c tibi succenset. He is now angry with the, or he has now taken
displesure with the.

Non quiuit te hodie cogere ut faceres. He could in no wise make or cause the
to do it.

Scis erumnas meas. Thou know what miserye I am in.

Non postulabat quisquam. No man desired, or required, or, there was no man
willing* or, about it.

Coactus tua uoluntate es. Thou were compelled with thy good will.

Non dum scis. Thou know not it yet.

Imo equidem scio. Yes mary do I know it well enough.

Cur me enecas? why do thou kylle my Heart? or why do thou moleste me?

Nunquam destitit suadere, orare, instare, donec perpulit ut dicerem. He never
ceased counsaylynge, prayinge, and calling upon me, till he had made or
caused me to say it.

Dauus interrurbat omnia. Dauus troublith all together, or brings all in the
briers. Dauus marreth all.

Mihi deos satis scio fuisse iratos, qui illi auscultauerim. I know very well,
that god was displeased with me, that I gave ear unto him, or that I was ruled
by him, or that I followed his counsel.

Quid ais scelus? what say thou harlotte? villayne?

Dij tibi dignum factis exitium dent. God give the mischief as thou have
deserved, or, god give the death acordyng to thy deserts.

Si omnes hunc coniectum in turbas inimici uellent, quod nisi hoc, consilium
darent? If all thennemies he has would desire to cast him into trouble, what
other counsel would they give him, than this?

Deceptus sum. I was deceived or beguiled.

Hac no^ successit, alia aggrediemur uia. It has not prospered or comme to
effect this way, now I will assaye an other.

Primo porcessit parum. At first it prospered but shrewdly, or at the
beginning hit went forewarde but easily.

Non potest ad salutem conuerti hoc malu^. This mischief can never be
recovered, or remedied, or, eased, or, this sore can not be healed, or recured.

Si aduigilaueris. If thou give and do thy good diligence.

Ego hoc tibi pro seruitio debeo. I owe you this of duty as being your
servant.

Conari manibus, pedibus, noctesque and dies. To endeavor and to do all that I
can hand and foot, day and night. Manibus pedibusque, Hand, and foot, that
is to say with all our might and with all good effect and diligence to be
possible. Erasmus in the prouer.

Capitis periculum adire dum prosim tibi. To put my life in adventure and
Ieoperdie, so I might do you good.

Tuum est. It is your part.

Praeter spem euenit. It has chanced other wise than I trusted, or hoped.

Parum successit, quod ago. Hit comes but easyly to pass that I go about.

Facio sedulo. I do my diligence, or the best I can.

Melius tu aliud reperi. Find or invent you some better thing.

Me missum fac. Let me alone.

Restitue in quem me accepisti locum. Restore or set me again in the same
place, or case, or state, that thou foundest me in.

Atiam opus est. But it must be done by and by, at ones, quickly.

Concrepuit ostium. The door craketh.

Nihil ad te. That is naught to the. or, hit appertains not unto the, or,
that have thou naught to do withal.

In the second Scene.
Vbi ubi erit, inuentum tibi curabo et mecu^ adductu^. where so ever he be I
shall find him out for you, and bring him with me.

Anime mi noli te macerare. Dear heart do not hurt your body with thought.

Optime mihi te offers. You mete me as well as can be.

Orare iussit, si se ames, herus, iam ut ad sese uenias. My master commanded
me to desire you, if you love him, to com now unto him.

Orat, iam ad se ut uenias. He prays you to come to him.

Hoc malum integrascit. This mischief begins even new of fresshe.

Siccine me opera tua nu^c miseru^ sollicitari? Ought I poor soul or

before we noted.

Age, si hic non insanit satis sua sponte, instiga. Go to if he be not mad
enough of him self, set him on, or help him forward.

Aedepol ea rest est. Forsothe that is even the matter.

In merore est. He is in sorrow.

Per omnes tibi adiuro deos, uel superos. I swear to the by god and all the
saints in heaven.

Si capiundos mihi sciam esse inimicos omnes homines. If I knew that I should
have all the men in the world mine ennemies, or, against me, or, though I knew
that I should have the displesure of all men lynynge.

Hanc mihi expetiui. I have desired, or my mind has been to have this woman.

Conueniunt mores. Our manners be one, or our conditions agree.

Valiant qui inter nos dissidium uolunt. Farewell they, or, a straw for them,
that would have us two at debate.

Hanc, nisi mors, mihi adimet nemo. There shall no man take away, or depart her
from me but death.

Non Apollinis magis uerum atque hoc responsum est. Not the answer of Apollo
is truer than this. This is as true as if god had spoken it.

Volo, ne per me stetisse credat, quo minus fieret. I would have him think
that there was no fault in me, that it was not done.

Si id fieri non poterit, faciam quod in procliui est. If that may not be, I
woll do that that may easily be done, or that comes to hand, or, that mine
own mind serves me best to do. For here In procliui quod est, is expouned by
Petrus Marsus in his commentaries upon this place.i. ad quod sum procliuis.i.
inclinatus ob amorem, qui trahit animum. Item in procliui est, quod facile est,
et ad quod sine magno labor descenditur. Plautus in his comedy entytled
Captiui duo. and Caesar in his commentaries uses procliue, for facile,
simpliciter.

Videris miser aeque atque ego. You seem to be in as ill case as I, or you
appear to be in miserable case as well as I.

Scio quid conere. I know whatte thou go about.

Hoc tibi effectum reddam. I woll bring it you to pass.

Huic non tibi habeo, ne erres. I have it for this man, and not for you that
you be not deceived.

Sat habeo. I am content with that, or that is enough for me.

Dies hic mihi ut sit satis ad agendum uereor. I fear that this day woll not
suffyse, or wolle not be sufficient for me to do my business.

Ne uacuum esse me nunc ad narrandum credas. Think not that I am now at
leisure to talk, or, to tell tales.

Hinc uos amolimini. Get you hens. Amolimini.i. recedite (inquit Nonius)
cytinge this place of Terence. Pacuuius, Non tu hinc e conspectu amolire?
Sisenna, Impedimentum omne de iteneribus amoliuntur.i. auferunt. Ergo uos hinc
amolimini.i. uos hinc auferte, uos hinc recipite.i. recedite, abite. ex Petr.
Mars.

Mihi impedimento estis. You let me, or, hinder me.

Quo hinc te agis.i. recipis, confers? whither go thou now?

Veru^ uis dica^? wolt thou that I say truth?

Quid me fire? What shall become of me?

Non sat habes impudens, quod tibi dieculam addo? Art thou not content
shameless fellow, that I wyn or get the a day.

Domi ero. I wolbe at home.

Parumper operire me hic. Tarry me here a littel while.

Facto opus est. It must needs be done.

Matura. Hye, or, make haste.

Iam hic adero. I wolle be here again by and by.

In the third Scene.
Quid istuc obsecro est? what is that I beseech the? or, what means this thy

Opus est mihi tua exprompta memoria. I had need that thou show thy good

Memoria i. ingenio, exprompta.i. ut expro matur et exerceatur.

Ante nostram ianuam humi depone. Lay it down on the ground before our door.

Quamobrem id tute non facis? why do not thou it thy self?

Noua nunc religio in te istaec incessit. This holiness or supersticion is
come upon the but now of late.

Move te ocyus. Go on, or, get the hens at ones, or hye apace.

Repudio consilium, quod primum intend ram. I renounce and for sake now my
first pretense. or, I now leave of the counsel, purpose, or devise which I
first pretended or purposid. Intenderam.i. proposuera^, institueram, taken by a

bent.

Cic. in Catone, Intentum animu^ tanquam arcu^ habebat, nec languens
succu^bebat senectui. Nescio quid narres. I can not tell what you say. or, I

Hinc ab dextera uenire me adsimulabo. I wol make as though I came this other

Subseruias orationi utcu^que opus sit uerbis. Se that you uphold and help my

Ego quid agas nihil intelligo. I perceive not wherabout you go.

Si quid est, quod mea opera opus sit. If there be any thing that my help be

Ne quod uestrum remorer commodum. That I may not let or hinder you of any

In the fourth Scene.
Reuertor postquam quae opus fuerunt, paraui. Now that I have ordained and
made redye such things as neded I am comme again.

Reliquit me homo, atque abijt. The fellow has left me alone and is gone his
ways.

Quid turbae est apud forum? what a do, or how much a do is abroad.

Quot illic hominum litigant? How many persons be there stryuynge and
chiding.

Tum annona cara est. Besides that corn is at a high price.

Quae haec est fabula? what array have we here, or what a recknyng is this? or,
what have we here a do.

Puer hic unde est? From whens came this child?

Sarin sanus es, qui me id rogites? Art thou well in thy wit to ask that
question of me?

Hic neminem alium uideo. I see no man else he re.

Miror unde sit. I marvel from whens he comes.

Concede ad dexteram. Come hither to this side, or, come on the right hand.

Deliras. Thou dootist, or, thou art folishe.

Verbum mihi unum praeterquam quod te rogo, faxis cave. Beware that thou speak
not one word to me, but that that I ask and demand of the.

Dic clare. Speak out aloude.

Quantum intelligo. As far as I perceive.

Adeon, uidentur uobis idonei, in quibus sic illudatis? Seem we unto you so
mete folks, with whom thus to dalie.

Veni in tempore. I came even in seson.

Cave ne quoquam ex isto excedas loco. Beware that thou go not one foot out
of the place thou standeste in.

Dij te eradicent, ita me miseram territas. A vengeance light on the, that so
do fere me.

Tibi ego dico an non? Do I bid the or not? or, Do I speak to the or not? or, I
speak to the, do I not?

Quid uis? What would thou?

At etiam rogas? And do thou ask yet again?

Mitte id quod scio, dic quod rogo. Let pass that that I know, and tell me
that I ask.

O facinus animaduertendum. O deed worthy to be punyshed.

Quid clamitas? what a crienge makist thou?

O homine^ audace^. O a saucy bold fellow.

Vidi Cantharam suffarcinatam. I saw the woman Canthara tucked up with her
lap full of gear.

Dijs habeo gratias. I thank god highly.

Tanto hercle magis dabit. In faith he wol give it so much the sooner.

Vt tu sis sciens. I would thou should well know it.

Hunc in mediam uiam prouoluam. I woll cast or tu^ble him in the myddis of the
street.

Si nihil attuleris, ibis Homere for as.

Foris.
Foris is used with verbes betokenynge being or resting in a place abroode or
from home* as my father is forth, Pater est foris. I have some thing to do at
home, but abroad I have no busynes, Est mihi domi quod agam, foris nihil est
negotij.

Siccine ais? Say thou so?

Qua gratia te huc accersi iussi, ausculta. Here now the cause why I bydde you
to be gone for or called. Accerso, is, iui, accersere, et arcesso, of the
third conjugation is to go to call. Denotat enim actum corporis, Ex Valla
cap. 23. libro primo elegantiarum. Accersio, is, iui, ire, of the fourth
conjugation is to call. All be it Accersio (as Valla says in the place above
cited) is often times used for accerso.

Dic mihi hoc primum. Tell me this first.

Potisne est hic tacere? Can this fellow keep any counsel?

Lege hac. On this condition.

Tibi meam astringo fidem. I promise you faithfully or by my faith.

Quae uera audiui taceo et co^tineo optume. Such things as I here true I can
hold in and keep secret very well. Taceo and co^tineo here be taken for one
thing, and the one does expound the other. For this conjunction copulatife,
et, is often times put for id est.

Sin falsum aut uanum, aut fictum est, continuo pala^ est. But if it be falls or
a lie, or else unlikely and to no purpose, or else feined by subtyltie. Donatus
in commentarijs. Falsum.
Falsum est, Vanum.
quo tegitur id quod factum est. Vanum quod fieri non potest. Fictum, Fictum.
quod no^ est factum, et fieri potuit. Vel, Falsum est fictu^ mendatium simile
ueritati: Vanum, nec possibile nec uerisimile: Fictum, totu^ sine uero, sed
uerisimile. Falsum loqui, menda cis est: Vanum, stultifictum, callidi. Falsum
loqui, culpae est: Fictum, uirtutis: Vanum, uecordiae. Falsis decipimur, Fictis
delecta mur, Contineo pro taceo
Vana contemnimus. And Contineo for taceo, metaphora est a uasis liquorum
continentibus, a metaphore or translation taken of vessels that can hold
lyquor, and not leake nor run out.

Plenus rimarum sum, hac atque illac perfluo. I am fulle of chappes or hooles,
and rounne out or leke at this place and that place, or every where. Tracta
metaphora a uasis fractis rimosis, minimeque liquoris continentibus. Perfluo.
Perfluo.
is, luxi, perfluere, perfluxum, is to run out in every part or on every
side, ut, Lagena male materiata, Lagena.
uel male compacta perfluit. Lagena is an erthen canyken or pytcherde more used
for wine than for water, which if it be not just made or else crased, it wol
leke and ro^ne out in every place. Cave illi quicquam committas, quod tacitum
uelis, nam undique perfluit. Beware that you trust not him with any thing that
you would have kept secret, for he runs out in every part as a broken
pitcherd. So in this place, hac atque illac perfluo.i. in modum non fidelis
lagenae, quae mihi co^mittuntur, effundo. And transfluo is of the same
sygnification, that is perfluo. Valla. li. 5. eleg. ca. 31.

Taceri si uis, uera dicito. If thou wolte have it kept secret tell truth.

Ea habitabat Rhodi. She dwellid at Rodes.

Arbitror, certum non scimus. So I think, but the truth or certayntie we know
not.

Matris nomen et patris dicebat. He told the name of his father and mother, or
he told the names of his parents.

Per aetatem non potuerat. He could not he was so young, or because he was
very young, an elegant manner of speaking observed and noted, apud Hadrianum
Cardinalem de ser. lat. Et in Thesauro linguae latinae. Plin. ep. 190. Totum
denique ordinem rei, cui per aetatem non interfuisti. Ye and all the hole
course or order of the matter at which doing you were not present because you
were very young. Liuius ab urbe condita. Qui per aetatem ac ualetudinem
poterant, Which were of age and in health able so to do, or else which were
neither so young nor sick but that they might. Ibide^ omnes qui per aetatem
arma far possent. All that were of age, able to were harneis. Cic. Caio Mario.
Cum per aetatem posses, uenire tamen noluisti. where as, or, when you were of
age able enough, yet you would not come.

Mercator hoc addebat. The marchaunte said this more over or further.

Matter cepit educare ita ut si esset filia. My mother began to bring her up as
if she had been her own daughter. where note the use of the tenses of verbes. It
is to be said in the latin authors of old time here esset and not fuisset,
and yet many etiam qui sibi uide^tur esse aliquid, posteriore loquendi mo do
nunquam non vtuntur.

Sororem plaerique esse credeba^t mea^. Many believed her to be my sister, or,
many thought verily, that she was my sister, or many believed surely, that she
had been my sister* and the latyne phrase says, esse and not fuisse.

Mihi reliquit haec quae habeo omnia. He left me all this that I have.

Vtrumque hoc falsum est. Both this is false.

Sine me peruenire quo uolo. Let me come to that point that I would, or suffer
me to say that I would say, or, suffer me to tell out all my tale.

Me amare occaeperat. He began to love me. Occeperat for caeperat. The
compou^de for the simple.

In Cariam profectus est. He went forth, or, he is gone forth, or, he has
taken his journey into the country of Caria.

Interealoci. In the mean time or in the mean space. Donatus in his
commentaries upon the second scene of the second Acts of this same comedy
redeth interealoci, as hit were one word.i. per subunionem. Subunio, is a
mark, that the Greeks use when two sundry dictions or vocables are to be
joined into one. And so redeth Donate here interealoci. Vt pronun cietur acuta
antepenultima. Duae, inquiens, parts orationis cum coniunctae, vnam fecerint,
mutant accentum. Interealoci ergo.i. interea, in the mean while, in the mean
time or space.

Post illa.i. ab eo tempore. Sins that time. Li nacre in his.vi. book, which
is entytled De figuris constructionu^, of the figures of construction,
understands tempora, and like wise in post ea, and says that in these and
such other manner of spekynges, as Ex quo, ex eo, ex illo, post ea, post illa.
etc. is eclipsis of this nowne tempus, as in these examples Verg. Ex quo
Tytides. Idem. Ex illo fluere, ac retro sublapsa referri. Suetonius in the life
of Domitius. Neque cessauit ex eo struere insidias fratri. for ex quo, illo, et
eo tempore, and like wise in such as this. Verg. Ante expectatu^ positis stat
in agmine castris. for ante expectatu^ tempus. But as Thesaurus linguae latinae
notith, and also the same Linacre in an other place, Post illa, is an aduerbe
of time, or used aduerbsially and is made one word of twain by submission,
like as interea loci, next before.

Mea consilia tibi credo omnia. I commit all my secrets unto you, or, I
trust you with all my counsel, or, I show you all my counsel, or, I hyde
none of my secrets or counsel from you.

Ne hoc quidem tacebit. He will not keep that secret.

Dubiumne id est? Is there any doubt of that? or is that any doubt.

Hoc agite amabo. I pray you take heed to this, proprely said in latin as
before in Andria. Simo. Hoccine agis an non? Do thou take heed to this that I
say or not? Ego uero isthuc. s. ago. Yes forsouthe sir, (answers Dauus.)

Matter mea illic mortua est. My mother dyed there.

Aliquantum ad rem est auidior. He is somewhat covetous for to get money.
Laurentius Vallensis in the first book of his elegancies and the.16. chapitre
says, that these ad verbes, Tantum, quantum, aliquantum, multum, paulum, with
others like be joined with positiues, and words of like signification with
positiues, as tantum probus, quantum doctus, as honest, as well learned, quantum
potes, tantum elabora, labor so much as thou may. Tanto, quanto,
aliquanto, multo, paulo, with others like be joined with comparatifes, as Cic.
quanto es mayor, tanto te geras summissius.

The higher or greatter man that thou art, so much the more lowely behave thy
self. Yet this notwithstanding (says Valla in the place above alleged) for
as much as the said words tantum, quantu^, aliquantu^ etc. are changed into
the nature of aduerbes, therefore they may sometimes be used for tanto, quanto,
and the others ending in O. specially if there be not such contraposytion of
two diverse things, that is to say, express and plain setting of one
contrary against an other.

Forma uidet honesta uirginem. He saw that hit was a goodly fair maiden. or,
he saw that the maid had a very good face, or, was very well faced or
fulle of beaultie.

Precium sperans illico producit, ac uendit. He trusting to get money, or to
be a gayner by hit, set hit out to sale, or, set it out and sold or made
money of it.

Forte fortuna adfuit hic meus amicus. As hap was this my friend was present.

Emit eam dono mihi. He boughte her to give unto me.

Imprudens harum rerum ignarusque omnium. Unware and unknowing of al these
things or matters.

Postquam sensit me tecum rem habere. when he perceived that I had to do
with you, or, after that he perceived that there were matters between you and
me.

Fingit causas. He pykethe quarelles, or, he faynethe excuses, or lettes, or,
occasyons.

Si fidem habeat. If he might believe, or, if he might be in surety, or, if
he might surely trust.

Ait se iri praepositum tibi apud me. He says that he shall or should be more
set by, more made of, or more in favor with me, than thou.

Ait uellem se illam mihi dare. He said that he would give her unto me.

Ait se id uereri. He says that he fears that.

Quantum ego suspicor. As I think, mystruste, or deem.

Ad uirginem animum adiecit. He set or cast his mind, affection, heart, or,
love unto the maid.

Multae sunt caussae, quamobrem cupio. There be many causes wherefore I would
feign.

Vt eam suis restituam ac reddam. That I may restore and surrendre her unto her
parents, or kyns folks.

Sola sum. I am a lone woman.

Habeo hic neminem, neque amicum, neque cognatum. I have no man here or in
these parties neither friend nor kynseman. Three negacions sometimes do make a
more strong or vehemente negacion or deny all. Cic. in tusc. questionibus.
Nihil nec disputare, nec scribere pretermisi. I have nothing let pass
neither to dispute, reason, debate, nor to write.

Cupio aliquos parare amicos beneficio meo. I would feign make, or get sum
friends by some benefyte or pleasure doing or showing.

Amabo adiuta me. I pray you help me. Amabo is an aduerbe of praying, and is
the same thing that we say in english, as ever I shall love you, or as ever I
shall do you good turn, or pleasure. etc. and hit has the second syllable
long, amabo and not short amabo, as some pronounce it.

A diuta me quo id fiat facilius. Help me that hit may be the better or more
easylye done.

Sine illu^ prioreis perteis per hosce aliquot di es apud me habere. Suffer him
to have the preemine^ce with me or in my house for a few days, or, for these
two or three days.

Nihil respondes? make you me none answer at all? or, woll you not speak to
me? For in such manner of spekynges, where as the phrase of the english
tongue uses to speak by the future time, the latyne men speak by the present
tense.

Egone quicquam cum istis factis tibi respondeam? Should I make the any answer
or, should I speak unto the, doing by me as thou do.

Laudo. I conne the thank* or, I commend the.

Vir es. That is a man.

Ego nesciebam quorsum tu ires. I knew not to what end you would bring your
tale, or I could not tell to what purpose, effect, or end, your tale should
come.

Paruula hinc est abrepta. She was taken, conveyed, or stolen away from hens
when she was but a little one, or, when she was a very little gyrle.

Omnia haec uerba huc redeunt denique. All these words at last come to this
point.

Ego excludor, ille recipitur. I am shut out of doors and he is received into
the house* or, I am put out of doors, and he is taken in.

Qua gratia? wherefore? or, for what cause?

Illum plus amas quam me. Thou love him better than me.

Istam times quae aduecta est, ne illum tale^ praeripiat tibi. Thou art aferde
of this maiden that is new come to the town, left that she should beguyle the
of him being such a ioly fellow, or, him being a man of such price, or, so
greatly to be set by.

Egone id timeo? Am I aferde of that? or do I fear that?

Quid te sollicitat? cedo. What thing troubles your mind? tell me.

Number solus ille dona dat? Does no man give no gifts but he? Number, in asking a
question is propresy used where the answer is to be made by, non: And an,
where the answer is to be made by, ita, etiam, or by some other word of
affirmation or granntynge, as well appears in the translation of Aristotle's
problemes.

Nuncubi mea^ benignitatem sensisti in te claudier? Have you perceived my
liberality or goodness toward you to halt, to faint / or to be slack at any
time, or in any thing?

Vbi mihi dixti cupere te. After you had told me that you were disirous and
feign.

Relictis rebus omnibus quesiui. Al other things let alone, I sought it, or,
leuyng all other business I set it or sought for it.

Heri minas uiginti pro ambobus dedi. I paid yesterday.xx. li. for them
twain. Haec habui in memoria. I remembered this ge are well, or, I have these
things well in mind.

Ob haec facta abs te spernor. For all that I have done this much for the,
thou set naught by me, or al these things not withstanding thou do not
regard me, or thou dispisest me, or haste me in contempt.

Hac re arbitror id fieri posse maxume. I think that it may best or sonest be
brought to pass by this menes or by this thing doing.

Potiusquam te inimicum habeam, faciam ut iusseris. Rather tha^ I wol lose a
fre^d of you I wol do as you shall co^mande me, or rather than I woll have your
displeasure, I woll do as you bid me, or, rather than I wolle have you at
debate with me, I woll do as you would have me to do.

Vtina^ isthuc uerbu^ ex ai^o ac uere diceres. would god thou spakest that word
with thy heart, and truly, or, without any dissimulation.

Si isthuc credere^ sincere dici. If I might think or believe that to be spoken
without any cloak or saynynge.

Quid uis possem perpeti. I could suffer, or I could be content to abide and
endure any thing what so ever it were.

Labascit, uictus est uno uerbo. He faints or gives over, and is ouercommed
with one poor word.

Ex animo dico. I speak it with my heart.

Quam rem uoluisti a me quin perfeceris? what thing did you ever require of
me, but that you had your parpose? or what thing have you ever wylled me to
do, but that you have had your mind? or, but that you have brought it to
pass, or to effect.

Impetrare abs te nequeo. I can not obtain of the.

Profecto non plus biduum. In good sothe no more but two days. Hadrianus de
sermon latino has noted that the latyne men did elegantely use plus, in
such manner speakynges (as this is) joined sometimes with the nominatife case,
and sometimes with the accusatife, and sometime with the ablatife indifferently.
Examples of plus joined with the nominatife. Liuius de bello Mac. Plus
quingenta hominum ceciderunt, more than five hundred men were slain. Idem de
bello Punico. Hominum eo die caesa plus duo millia, That day were slain mo
than two thousand me^. Plus joined with the accusatife. Caelius Ciceroni. Hic
multu^ ac diu ludetur, atque ita diu, ut plus biennium in his tricis moretur,
Here shall be much and long dalyeng, and that so long, that we shall continue
above two years in these encombra^ces or combrous business. Cato in re rustica.
Ne plus quatuor digitos transuersos eminea^t, Let them not stand or arise up
above the breadth of four fyngers. Vitruuius. Ita a pariete distent, ut ne
plus pateat palmum, Let them so be set distaunt from the wall, that there be
no more space left between than the breadth of a man's hand. Plus joined with
the ablatife. Cicero in that his oration pro P. Quintio. Ac tecum plus anno
uixit in Gallia, And he made his abode with you in Gallia more than one holle
year. Ide^ in the oration pro Planco: Non possum dicere eum praefuisse, neque
possum negare eu^ abfuisse, sed no^ plus duobus aut tribus me^si bus, I can not
say that he was co^tinualli present, Nor I can not deny but that he was away or
absent, but not above.ii. or three moan these i al. Liuius de bello Punico: Ab
utraque perte sexcentis plus peditibus, et dimidiu^ eius equitum cecidit, Of
either part were slain of footmen more than six hundred, and of horsemen
half of the same nombre.

Non fiet hoc modo. It shall not be so, or it shall not be so done.

Sine te exorem. Let me entreat you.

Scilicet faciundum est quod uis. Ye mary I must needs do as you woll have me.

Merito te amo. I have good cause to love you, or my love is well bestowed on
you.

Been facis. You do well, or, well said.

Rus ibo. I wol go into the country.

Ita facere certum est. I am utterly determined or appointed so to do.

Mos gerendus est Thaidi. I must be ordrid or ruled by Thais, or, I must do as
Thais byddeth me.

In hoc biduum uale. Fare ye well, or, god be with you for these two days.

Tu number quid uis aliud? wolle you any thing else?

Dies noctesque me ames. Love me both day and night.

Me desideres. wysshe for me.

Me somnies. Dreme of me.

De me cogites. Think all on me. or, let all your mind be on me.

Me te oblectes. Let all your delight, pleasure or felicity be in me only.

Forsan mihi paruam habet fide^. Percase he gives small credence unto me, or,
percase he believes or trusts me not very well.

Ex aliorum ingenijs me iudicat. He iugeth or demeth me by the nature or
disposition of others.

Ego qui mihi sum conscius. I the which know mine own heart, breast, or
thought. Hoc certo scio. This I know for a surety.

Scio me non finxisse falsi quicquam. I know or I am right sure, that I have
not forged any thing at all false or untrue.

Quicquid huius feci.i. quicquid id est quod nunc feci. what so ever hit is
that I have now done.

Caussa uirginis feci. I did it, or I have done it for the maidens sake.

Spaero me propemodum iam repperisse. I trust I have almost found it now.

Is hodie uenturu^ ad me constituit domu^. He made poyntement to come home to
my house this day.

Concedam hinc intro. I wolle get me hens in.

Expectabo dum uenit. I shall tarry and look for him till he comes.

In the second Act the first Scene.
Fac ita ut iussi. Do as I did co^ma^de the.

Satis ne hoc mandatum est tibi? Is this bidding enough for the? or nedeste
thou any more bidding than this?

Vtinam tam aliquid inueuire facile possis* quod hoc peribit. would god you
could find or get some good thing so well or so surely as this shall be
lost and cast away.

Ne isthuc ta^ iniquo patiare animo. Take not this so unkindly.

Quin effectum dabo. Tusshe I woll bring it to pass, or do it.

Numquid aliud imperas? woll you command me with any other service?

Munus nostrum ornato uerbis, quod poteris. Set out my gift with good words
as much or as well as thou can.

Memini etsi nullus moneas. I remember hit well ynowgh, though you speak never
a word.

Censes me posse perpeti? Thynkeste thou that I shall be able to abide or
endure hit?

Non hercle arbitror. In good soothe I think nay.

Opus faciam. I woll do work and labor.

Eijciunda haec mollicies animi. This tenderness of heart must be put away?

Nimis mihi indulgeo. I follow mine own appetite to much.

Ego non illa caream, si sit opus uel totu^ triduum? Could not I lack her, or
could not I abide out of her company, although it were for holle three days
together, if need were?

Vide quid agas. See or beware whatte you do.

Stat sententia. I am utterly determined or appointed, or, minded.

Dij boni quid hoc morbi est. Good lord what manner a sickness or disese is this.

Adeone homines immutari, ut non cognoscas eundem esse? Is hit possible for men
to be so far changed, that a man can not know whether one be the same man or
not? In such manner spekynges by the infinitive mode put absolutely Valla
understands Ita ne uerum est, as here, Itane ueru^ est adeo hoi^es immutari.
etc. Linacre in the vi. book of his latyne grammar, which is entitled of the
figures of construction says that hit is eclipsis of oportet, or decet. The
good judgment of a diligent reder can not mysse nor fail to supply such
verbes, as the place and sense shall require.

Quis hic est, qui huc pergit? what is he yon that comes by there ward?

Ducit secum una uirginem. He brings a maiden with him.

In the second Scene.
Dij immortales, homini homo quid praestat? Good lord in heaven how much is
some one man better than an other?

Stulto intelligens quid interest? what difference is between a fool and a
wise man? Venit in mente^ mihi. It com^th to my mind or remembrance, or I begin
to remember.

Conueni hodie quenda^ mei loci atque ordinis. I spoke with one to day of my
degree and order or state, or hauour. Donatus expou^dith it thus, Mei loci.i.
ingenuum, Fre borne, ordinis.i. pauperem Poor. Illud natalium, hoc fortunae
est. The one that is to wit, loci, has respect and relation to the degree of
birth, ordinis, does refer the hauiour in goods and the state of Fortune.

Conueni hominem impurum, patria qui abligurierat bona. I have talked with a
naughty fellow, that has spent out all his fathers goods in good chere, or,
that has wasted all the goods that his father left him in making good chere.

Quid isthuc ornati est, inquam? How art thou arrayed, apparayled, decked, or
trymmed, quod I?

Miser quod habui perdidi. I have lost that I had poor soul, or, I a man
undone have lost all that I had.

Quo redactus sum? To what point, or into what case, or unto what state am I
brought? Omnes noti me atque amici deseru^t. Al mine acqueyntance and friends do
forsake me or give me over, or cast me of.

Ego illum contempsi pre me. I thought my self much better and much more
honest man than he was.

Itane parasti te? Haste thou so ordered or behaved thy self.

Spes nulla reliqua. There is no hope left.

Simul consilium cum re amisisti? Haste thou lost thy goods or substance and
thy wit to? or, dyddest thou lose thy wit also when thou lost thy goods?

Vides ne me ex eodem loco ortum? Do not thou se me that am of the same
degree of birthe that thou art?

Quae habitudo corporis? How round and fat is my body? or, in what, or in
how good liking is my body?

Omnia habeo, neque quicquam habeo. I have all things, and yet I have nothing.

Nihil cum est, nil defit tamen. Though I have nothing, yet I lack nothing.

Tota erras uia. Thou art very foul deceived. a proverb taken of them which
in their journey go clean out of their way. Erasmus in Chil.

Hoc nouum est aucupium. This is a new craft to get a lyuinge, or to get
money, a metaphore taken of foulynge, or taking of foul. For Auceps proprely
is a fouler, and Aucupium is foulynge, and by a metaphore it is used for all
manner of ways to get any thing by wyles, trains, or craft, and it is
derived of the verb aucupor, aris, to go a foulynge and to take birds, and by
translation aucupari laudem, is to go about to get preise and commendacion,
aucupari quaestum, to go about to get money.

Ego hanc primus inueni uiam. I was the first that found out this way, or, I
found out this way first.

Est genus hominum, qui esse primos se omnium rerum uolunt. There is one sort
of men, which would have preemynence above all others, or, which desire to be
hygheste of all, and to be most had in honor.

Hisce ego non paro me ut redeant. Among such men I do not so fasshon,
order, or, use my self to make them to laugh at me.

His ultro arrideo. To such (what so ever they say or do) I show a merry
smyling countenance for the nones, or, of mine own self.

Eorum ingenia admiror. I make a great maruaillyng at their high wits.

Quicquid dicunt, laudo. What so ever they say, I praise, commend, or allow
it.

Id rursum si negant, laudo id quoque. That if they deny, the self same
again, that also I commend and hold withal.

Is quaestus nunc est multo uberrimus. By so doing as the world goes now a
man may geat a meruaylous good living, or that is now a days a meruaillous
ready way to get money, or, now a days that is the very chief and principal
way to get money enough.

Dum haec loquimur. While we were thus talkyng, or communyng.

Me salutant, ad caenam uocant, aduentu^ gratula^tur. They beade me good eue^,
and bead me to suppar, and said that I was welcome or, that they were glad of
my coming.

Vbi uidet me tam facile uictum quaerere. wha^ he saw that I got my lining so
easily.

Ibi homo coepit me obsecrare. Then the fellow began to desire and pray me for
gods sake. Obsecrare enim est quasi per sacra rogare.

Viden otiu^, and cibus quid faciat alienus? Do you not see what ydelnes or
living in ease, and an other man's table do?

Parmenonem ante ostium triste^ uideo. I see Parmeno stand sadly before the
door.

Gnat, tristis es? Par. nihil equide^. Gnayow ar sad.P. not a wit I.

Vro hominem.i. dolere cogo. says Donat, I beat or wring or vex the fellow.
For uro, uris, ussi, ustu^, is to burn, or to perch and it is as well of cold
as of heat. Lucanus. Vrebant montana niues. All the hills and that that
grows upon them were perched with snow or cold. Vergil. Aut boreae
penetrabile frigus adurat. Or when the sharp nyppyng or persing cold of the
north wind percheth. etc. Vrere hominem, By translation is to vex a man, and
to make him sorry at the heart, and as we also by translation say in english,
to byte, to nyppe, to wring, to make woo. And the passive Vror, is sembleably
used for cruciari to be vexed, to be woo, or to be sorry at the heart.

Plaut. in Bacchid. Quam magis id repeto, tam magis uror, The more I remember
or consider it, the more am I sorry and vexed or grened withal. Idem in Persa.

Vritur cor mihi. My heart burned. Ide^ in Menech. Viden tu illi oculos urier
ut uiridis exoritur color ex temporibus? Do thou not see his eyes burn as
reed as fire? and how that his temples wex or begin to be as grene as grass?
Virgil used, uro, in the same signification for a verb neutre passyue.

Omnium rerum uicissitudo est, The world changes every day. It is a
proverb, by which is signyfyed, that in this world is nothing stable
permanent nor durable, but like as the sea does continually flow and ebb, so
do all things in this world daily change, now up, now down, now merry, now
sad. etc. Eras. in chil.

Sex ego te totos menses quietum redda^, ne sursum deorsum cursites. I woll set
the at rest for runnynge up and down for one full six months, or, I woll
ease or release the of runnyng up and down for the space of one hole half
year.

The third Scene of the second act.

Vbi quaeram? where shall I seek him?

Vbi inuestigem? where shall I find which way he went? Vestigare, est per
uestigia querere, seu indagare, To seek by the steps and printe of the foot,
and by the trace. Plaut. in truculento. Haec una opera circum it per familias,
puerum uestigar, She goes all under one to every man's house, and traceth the
boy. And by translation hit is to make diligente enserche of or for any thing.
Cic. L. 3. de oratore. Ipsa tractatio et question cotidie ex se gignit aliquid,
quod cum desidiosa delectatione uestiges, The very handelinge or exercise and
the matter felfe does every day of it self bring forth some thing, which a
man may or would seek out with quiet delectatio^ and pleasure. Inuestigare
est per uestigia inuenire, To find out by the foot, by the steps, or by the
trace and trede of the feet, as they that hunte and trace the wild beasts
and follow them by the foot, and by the trace find them out, and (by a
metaphore thereof taken) it is to find out any thing by diligent enserching.
Plautus in Mercatore, Non con cedam, neque quiescam, usque noctu, neque dius,
prius profecto quam aut amicam aut mortem inuestigauero, I woll not give over
nor rest in any place, neither by night nor by day at the lest wise before or
until I shall have found, either my love, or else death. Terentius in
Heautontimorumeno. Nihil est tam difficile, quin quaerendo inuestigari possit,
There is nothing so hard but that by diligente serching and seeking it may be
found out. Ci. in or^oe, pro Q. Ligario. Sed quoniam diligentia amici
inuestigatum est quod latebat, confirendum est ut opinor, But saying that by
the good diligence of a friend, that thing is now found out which was hydden
and unknown, it should be confessyd as I think.

Que^ perconter? Of whom might I ask?

O faciem pulchra^. O fair and goodly face.

Tedet harum cotidianarum formaru^. I am weary of these, that among us are
called and taken for fair women, or I have done with those fair women that we

O infortunatum senem. O unfortunate or unhappy old man.

Ludum iocumque dices fuisse illum alteru^, prae ut huius rabies quae dabit.
Thou would say, that that other was but a play and sport, in comparison of
tho things or of such prankes as this fellow woll play in his rage. Donatus
does order it thus, Prae ut sc illa sunt, quae huius rabies dabit.i. faciet,
patrabit, monstrabit, ostendet. Prae, somtymes is very elegantly used
importynge a certain respect and comparacion to an other thing. Terence in
this comedy a little before. Ibi ego illum co^tempsi prae me, Than did I nothing
regard him in comparison of my self. And in this significacion prae is often
times (specially in Plaut. and Terence) found compou^de with quam, and ut, as
Hadrian has noted. Exemples, prae alone having an ablative case after him.
Plaut. in Mostellaria. Video te nihili pendere omnes homines prae Philolache, I
see that you fette by no man in co^paryson of Philolace. Ide^ in Mil. Prae
illius forma quasi spernas tua^, As who says you set not by your beauty in
comparyson of hers. or, as we should say, you think not your self fair in
comparison of her.

Cic. Seruio Sulpitio: Non tu quidem uacuus molestijs, sed prae nobis beatus.
Indede you are not void of some troubles, or without some care, but yet in
comparyson of me blisfull, or, in heaven.

Prae quam, Plaut. in Aulul. Sed hoc etiam pulchrum est prae quam ubi sumptus
petu^t, But this is even a goodly or ioyly thing, in comparyson of when they
require cost, charges, or expense of money. Idem in Amphitryo. Parua res est
uoluptatum in uita, prae quam quod molestum est, There is small pleasure in
this life in comparison of the troubles and cares that be in the same. Prae ut.
Plautus in Mil. Nihil hercle hoc quidem est, prae ut alia dicam. Nay this is
nothing in comparison of other things that I woll tell or show you. Ide^ in
Amphit. Parum etiam prae ut futurum est praedicas, Thou say or spekest eue^
very little, or in manner nothing, in comparyson of that that shall be in deed.
Idem in Menech. Modestior nunc quidem est de uerbis, prae ut dudum fuit, He is
now sobre in his words in comparison of that he was right now, or a little
while agone. Idem in Merc. Pentheu^ diripuisse aiunt Bacchas, nugas maximas
fuisse credo, prae ut quo pacto diuersus distrahor, They say, or, it is a
saying, that the women that did sacrifice unto Bacchus, did tere one Pentheus
all to peaces, but I think verily it was but a very small matter and a trifle
in comparison of this how diversely or how many ways my heart and mind is now
as who should say, pulled in peaces and torn in sundre. The fables do
report that there was one Pentheus king of the Thebans, whose father was
called Echion, and his mother Agaue, This Pentheus despised the sacrifice of
Bacchus the god of wynes, wherefore his own mother Agaue cut or stroke of his
head, And his systers with the other company of women, which than did
celebrate the feast and sacrifice of the said Bacchus, tore his body and
members all to peaces.

Vt illum dij deaeque senium perdant. God and all the saints in heaven give
that old churl a mischief, or, a vengeance light on that old churl, I pray
god and all the saints in heaven. Vt here, and in such other like manner
spekinges is taken for, Vtinam, execrando uel optando, in cursyng or bannyng or
wishing. Plautus in Persa. Vt istum dij deaeque perdant, God and all the
saints give this knave a mischefe. Idem in Mostellaria: Vt dij deaeque omnes
me pessimis exemplis interficiant, nisi ego illam interfecero siti fameque,
atque gelu, I pray god and all the saints send me the most shameful death
that may be to the example of al others, if I do not surely kill her up with
thirst, and hu^gre, and cold. Se moo examples in Hadrian, De sermon latino.
Further note that in this word, Senium, is emphasis, which is a figure either
when something, that is hidden and not spoken, is meaned, or else when it is
spoken for a more express, plain, and strong setting out and expressing of a
thing: which may be many ways, but the principal and most elegant way, is
when a substantyue is set for an adjective, that is to say when any quality
is put for the person that has the quality: as, scelus, ungraciousness, pro
sce lesto, for one that is ungracious, and so here Senium, agednes, or old
age, pro seen, for him that is old. For (as Donatus says) Senex, is referred
too the age of years, senium, to the contumelyouse, and dispytefull, and
contemptuouse words. And therefore I english, illum senium, that old churl.

where note that al be it that seniu^, is the neutre gendre, yet it has
joined with him an adjective of the masculyne gendre, and the relatyfe that
comes after is also put in the masculin gendre. For (as Donatus witnesses)
the gendre both of the adjective and also of the relatife is referred to the
thing that is understand, that is to say, senem, or, hominem senem, So
Terence in the prologue of this second comedy. Eas se non negat personas
transtulisse in Eunuchu^ suam ex graeca, He says not nay, or, he does not
deny, but that he took the same parts that are in the greek comedy, from
thens into this his comedy entitled Eunuchus. Eunuchum is the masculine
gendre, and yet because there is meaned by it comae diam, therefore the
adjective suam, is put in the feminine gendre. Idem in Andr. Vbi illic.i. ille
scelus est, qui me perdidit? where is that ungracious knave, that has cast me
away? Idem in Adelphis. Festiuum caput, qui omnia sibi posthabe^da putarit
esse prae meo co^modo, The gentylest companion or the most honest or best
fellow alive, which could find in his heart to let all other things
alone, or to set all other things a part in respect of my co^modite and
pleasur or for my comodite and pleasure. By caput is understand Eschinum, and
therefore the relatife qui, having respect and relacion to the person that is
meaned, and not to the substantyue, that is expressed is put in the masculin
ge^dre and not in the neutre ge^dre.

Me remoratus est. He caused me to tarry, or, tarried me or hyndred me of my
way..i. detinuit me.

Qui illum non floccifecerim. That I set so much as a straw or a rusche by
him.

Floccus is any little ragge of a lock of woulle the which as unprofitable,
and the which woll serve for no thing, is pulled from the flice and cast
away and flies away, or some like thing of no value, price, nor estimation,
whereof the latin men, wha^ they wol signify or show that they set nothing
by a man or any other thing, use to say prouerbially. Flocci non facio, or
else Flocci facio i^differently. For which we englishmen in a like proverb
say, I set not the left straw or rusch under my foot by it, or I set
not the lest here in my heed by it, al be it for this later, both the greeks
and latin men have a peculiar pronerbe taken of the same thing, Pili non facio.
I set not a here by it.

Quid tu es tristis? why are your sad?

Nescio Hercule, nec unde eam, nec quorsum eam, In good south I can not tell,
neither from whens I come, nor whether I goo.

Prorsum oblitus sum mei, I have quite and clean forgotten my self.

Fac nunc promissa appareant, Now let your promises appear or be seen, or, se

Est paulo habitior. He is somewhat fat, round, or in good liking.

Noua figura oris, color uerus, corpus solidu^, et succi plenum. Such favor
of face and visage as you have not much seen, true and natyfe color, and not
of paintyng, her body sound, lusty and nothing decayed, but full of good
blood and wholesome humors.

Mihi uel ui, uel clam, uel precario fac tradas. See that thou get it me or in
to my hands either perforce and by strenghte of hands, or else by preuye
conueyaunce and stelthe, or else of lone for a little while, and than to be
restored home again. For precarium carij, is that thing, which is by prayer
instaunce and petition granted to any body to use, to occupy, or to enjoy so
long as it shall please and co^tente the party, that does so lend or
grant it, and no longer. So Alexander, apud Q. Cur. says, Mori prestat quam
precario imperator esse, Better it is to dye than to be a captain or a king at
the pleasure of other men and no longer. Ibide^. Precariu^ spernebatur
imperiu^, He set nothing by that pouer, dominion, or rule, which should
continue so long as pleased them that gave or granted it, and no longer. And
under this meaning did Seneca say, hominem esse precarij spiritus, That the
life of man does continue at the pleasure of nature, fate, or destine, which
take it away when they woll, and not when pleases us. Of this the nown
precario, in the voice and terminacio^ of the dative case is used aduerbially
in the same significacion, Pau. iureco^su. Precario he^re uidet quam
possessione^ corporis ul iuris adeptus est hac solu^modo causa, quoth adhibuit
preces and impetrauit, quod sibi possidere aut uti liceat, ueluti si me
precario rogaueris, ut per fundum meum ire uel agere liceat, uel ut in tectum
meum stillicidium, uel tignum in parietem immissum habeas. A man to have a
thing precario (says Paulus) seems to be when any body has obtained and
gotten the possession of any body or bodyly thing, or of any right and title
to any thing only for because he made request and instaunce for the same, and
thereby has obtained, that it may be lefull for him to possess or to use and
occupy it, as if you should make request and instaunce unto me, and desire me
that you might for a while as long as it should please me, to have a way or to
goo through my ground, or to do any thing therein, or else that you might have
a little gutter or synke to come by my house, or else to have a rafter, a logge,
or a beam set within or upon the wall of my house. Plinius de uiris
illustribus. Seruius Tullius, quasi praecario regnare coepit, sed recte
imperium administrauit, Seruius Tullius began at the first to reign in the
city of Rome as who should say, precario, that is at the will and pleasure of
the people, and as long as they would permit and suffer him and no longer,
but yet afterward he ordered and ruled the same Impire well enough. Plinius in
epistolis. Quibus ex causis precario studeo, studeo tamen. For which causes I
study only when please them, or as long as they wol suffer me, and yet some
study I have. Cic. in the oration, pro Aulo Cecinna. Ne id quidem satis est,
nisi docet ita se possedisse, ut nec ui, nec clam, nec precario possederit, But
yet that is not sufficient or enough, neither except he plainly show and
declare, that he had it so in his possession, that he held it neither by force
and strength, nor privily and unknowing to the owner, or else that he had it
lent him for a season to be restored home again, when it should be required.
For that be the three ways unlawfully to keep away any thing of an other
man's. And Precario in this significacion may, be vsid aduerbially in many
diverse and sondrie manner spekynges. For precario co^cedere, is to lend or to
grant a thing till you shall require it again. Precario petere uel rogare, is
to desire to have a thing as long as may please the owner, and than make
surrendre and redeliuerie of the same, when it shall be required.

Mea nihil refert, dum potiar modo. I care not so that I may have, opteine, or
get it.

Virgo cuia est? what maid is it? or, what is she?

Qua ratione amisisti? How, or, by what chance or mean did you lose it?

Equide^ adueniens mecu^ stomachabar mo^, As I was coming hither right now, I

Neque quenquam esse homine^ arbitror, cui magis bonae felicitates omnes
aduersae sient. And I think, there is no man alive, that has al good fortune
and chances more against him, than I have. These two words homo quisquam, be
often times elega^tly thus joined to gether, not withsta^dyng that quisquam alone
by it self signifies as much as homo quisquam. Liuius ab urbe cond. Victoria
cui nec deus, nec homo quisquaminuideat, A victory, at which neither any of
the goddis, nor yet any man has, or should, or may have envy, or groudge.

Quid hoc est sceleris? what abominable act is this? or what ungracious deed

Incommode hercle. Il, or to your displeasure truly.

Illum liquet mihi deierare his me^sibus sex uel septem prorsum non uidisse
proximis. I may clearly and boldly swear, that by the space of these six or
seven months now last paste, I never saw him.

Nisi nu^c cu^ minime uellem, minimeque opus fuit. But now at this time, wha^
it was my lest desire, or, lest in my mind and will, and wha^ it was nothing
nedeful, or, least expedient.

Nonne hoc monstri simile est? Is not this like a very monstre? or, is not this
a very strange thing? Mo^strum, i, o, is deriuied of monstro, as, aui, are, to
show whereof. Mo^strum is any thing, that in signifienge any other thing,
does show it, Cicero de natura deoru^: Monstra, praedictiones, et prae
sensioues rerum futurarum quid aliud declarant, nisi hominibus ea, quae futura
sunt, portendi, praedici, ex quo illa ostenta, monstra, porte^ta, prodigia
dicu^tur, These strange tokens or shewingis, propheciengis, and foreknowingis
and fore perceyuinges of thingis to follow and come: what other thing do they
declare, but such things as shall in deed follow after, to be before signified,
betokened, and showed unto men, and by reason thereof, such or the same thyngis
be called in latin ostenta, monstra, portenta, or prodigia. And therefore
Vergil used mo^strum for and instead of the nowne verbal monstrario. Monstra
deum refero, I make relacion unto you of such things, as the goddis have
showed unto me. But because that such things, for the most part, are noted
by strange sightes and chances (which not only in our english tongue, but
also in all other tongues for the most part ar called monsters after the latin
word) Therefore monstru^ is most commonly used and taken for all such thingis
as ar contrary or against the common order and course of Nature, either in
defaute and lackyng, orels in exceeding, as to have two thummes upon one hand,
or to be borne the hele standing in the place of the toes, or to be borne
without a nose, with others like.

Continuo ad me accurrit. He comes runnynge unto me by and by.

Scin' quid ego te uolebam? Wot you what I would with you? or, what I would
have you do?

Cras est mihi iudicium. I must be before the judge to morrow.

Comites secuti sunt? Did there any company follow?

Alias res agis. Thou art, or thou go about other matters, as who should
say, thou takeste no head to that that I say: And so does Terence use it and
speak it here, as I have often times englished it before.

Vidi, noui, scio quo abducta sit. I saw her, I know her, and I can tell

Duras fratris parts praedicas. My brother has the worse part or side, by

Faciam sedulo, and, Dabo operam, I wol do my diligence, or, I wol do the best
