Quid agis? iocabar equidem. what now? or, what meanest thou? or, wherabout
go thou? I spoke but in sport. Valla li. 4. eleg. ca. 16. notes, that
Iocari and iocus be proprely in words, ludere and ludus in deeds. Al be it
they be in authors co^funded, that is to say the one used for the other, as
Valla proves and shewith by examples i^ the forsaid place.

Quid ego egi miser? what have I done miserable fellow, or wretch that I am?

Isihaec in me cudetur faba. The fault of this shall be laid to me, or this
mischife shall light on my neck. A proverb, of which read in Chil. Erasmi.

Flagitium facimus. We do, or, we should do an heinous offense. Facere
flagitium, is to commit, to perpetrate or to do an heinous offense, or a great
trespass. Plaut. in Paenulo: Hae fores fecerunt magnum flagitium modo. Ad. Quid
flagitij est? C. Crepuerunt clare. This door did a great trespass right now. Ad.
what great or heinous offense is that? C. It gave a great loud crack, or it
made a great loud crekynge. Idem in Mer. Ait flagitiu^ et da^nu^ fecisse. He
says, that he has done a great offense and harm, or shrewde turn.

An id flagitium est? Is that any great trespace or heinous offense?

Eos itidem fallam, ut ab illis fallimur. I wol even so begyle them, as they
begyle me, and such others as I am.

Aequum est fieri. It is good reason that it be done, or that it should be so.

Merito factum omnes putent. All men may think it well done, and not without a
good cause.

Si certum est facere, facias. If you be utterly purposed so to do, do it, or
if you woll needs do it, do.

Ne conferas culpam in me. Put not the fault or blame on me.

Parm. Iubes ne? Chaer. Iubeo, cogo, atque impero. Parm. Do thou bead me?
Cher. Ye mary do I bead the, and compel the, and also command and charge the.

Nunquam defugiam authoritatem. I wol not do ageynste your authorite, That is,
I wol not be aferde to do as you bead me, nor to follow your authority and
commandment. Defugere authoritatem is to avoid, and (as who should say) to be
aferde to follow and to do that thing that any person has authority to
co^mande, orels may do by authority. Cic. pro P. Sylla. Tu remp. reprehendis,
quae domesticos hosts, ne ab ipsis ipsa necaretur, necauit. Itaque attend iam
Torquate, quam ego defugiam authoritate^ consulatus mei, Thou reproueste the
common weal, for that it has put to death familiar ennemies and rebellious,
that were within the city, lest that by them it self might have be^ oppressed
and brought to utter confusion and desolation. Therefore o Torquate, se now and
mark well, how greatly I am aferde to stand by that that I did by or in the
authority of mine office of Co^sulshyp, as who should say, se that I am not
aferde to stand by it, and that I do not now repent or go from that that I
did, when I was Consul, in sleinge Catiline and puttyng him to death. etc. as by
the place in Tully, the judgment of a diligent reder may well se.

Dij uertant been. God turn it to good, or bring it to a good end.

In the third Scene.
Non tam ipso quide^ dono laetus est, quam abs te datu^ esse. He is not so glad
of the gift or present self, as that it was given by you.

Id uero serio triumphat. And of that he is merry or glad in deed, or, for that
he triumphs or glories ernestly, or in ernest.

Huc prouiso ut ubi tempus siet, eum dedu came. I come forth hither to se, that
when time is, I may bring him, or wait on him.

Est isthuc datum mihi, grata ut sint quae facio omnia. It is a gift given me,
that all things that I do, every man lyketh well.

Qui habet salem, qui in te est.i. sapientiam et le porem. Who so has the
wisdom and pleasant facion, that you have. Donatus expounds Salem.i.
sapientiam, wisdom. Where he notes that sal, neutraliter condimentum
significat, masculinum pro sapientia accipitur uel ponitur. Al be it sal, when
it is latin for salt, is both the masculine and also the neuire ge^dre in
both nu^bris. Cato in, re rust. Ex sale qui apud Carthaginenses fit Of the salt
that is made in the parties about the cite of Carthage. Salu. in Iug. Neque
sale^, neque alia gulae irritamenta, Neither salt, nor any other things to
provoke the appetite. Columel. Carne^ salibus aspersam, Flesh over cast with
salt. Paul. iur. con. Cotem ferro subigenda^, necessaria quoque hostibus
uenu^dari, ut ferru^, and frumentu^, and sales, non sine capitis periculo
licet, It is not lefull under the pain of death, to sell to our enemies either
a whetstone to make any knyfe sharp, or any other necessaries, as iron, or
knyues, and wheat and salt. etc. Sal, by translation is taken pro urbanitate,
lepore, uenustate, ioco, Good and pleasant fashon, and merry co^ceytes both in
words and otherwise, as here in this place of Terence. And Catullus: Nulla in
tam magno est corpore mica salis, There is not one crum or drop of good
fashon in all that great royles body. For Catullus there speaks of a certain
maiden, that was called Quintia, whom many esteemed and called fair,
beautiful, and goodly. In deed (says Catullus) I grante that she is white of
skin, tall, and slendre of making, and bolte up right, but that she is
formosa, that is fair or beautyful, that I deny, for there is no manner
pleasauntenes nor good faciou in her. The verses of Catulles be these: Qui^tia
formosa est multis, mihi ca^dida lo^ga

Recta est, haec ego sic singula confiteor. Totu^ illud formosa nego na^ nulla
uenustas,

Nulla in ta^ magno est corpore mica salis.

Salis.i. uenustatis, leporis, plesa^tnes, grace, and propre feture, or good
facio^ in her gesture, behauior, or pleasant words, which may delight the^ that
se her, here her, or be in her company. Plau. in Ca. Nec pote quicquam
co^memorari, quod plus salis, plusque leporis habeat hodie. Nec pote. s. est.i.
no^ potest quicquam. etc. It is not possible to rehearse or to show any body,
that has more grace, amiablenes, or pleasant facion at this hour. where,
que, is taken for, id est, plus satis, plusque leporis. For plus salis.i. plus
leporis, as I have noted in other places before. And here of Sal, in the singlar
number and, sales, in the plurell be taken for merry co^ceites, or delectable
and pleasant co^municacion, that may make or cause the herers to laugh, and
yet is sumwhat sharp and biting withal, as witnesses Quin. li. 6. And Plin.
l. 31. cap. 7. of the natural history, where he says thus: Ergo Hercule
uita humanior sine sale nequit degere, adeoque necessarium elementum est, ut
transierit intellectus ad uoluptates animi quoque. Nam ita sales appellantur,
omnisque uitae lepos, et summa hilaritas, laborumque requies non alio magis
uocabulo co^stat, Than truly the life of man being in any thing reasonable
good condition or state, can not continue without the use of salt, which is
so necessary and profitable an help and sustinance or sustentation to and for
the life of man, that the mind, intelligence, and under standinge has taken
and made from the same a metaphore or translation unto the pleasures and
delectations of the mind. For the said pleasures and delectations of the mind,
or called in latin sales, and al manner pleasure of our life, and the highest
myrthe and pastyme, that we have (which consists in witty, merry, and
pleasante co^municacion) and also al quiet rest and ease after peyneful
labors, ar by none other latin word in the world better, or more expressly
and generally signified, than by this word sales, of which be metaphorically
derived many pretty adagies or proverbs, of the which read Erasmi Chiliades,
and specially (for this purpose) the pruerbe, Salsitudo non inest illi.

Sicubi eum satietas hominum caeperat. If he were at any time weary of the
company of men.

Negotij eum siquando odium caeperat.i. tae dium. If he were weary of his
business, or great labors.

Requiescere ubi uolebat. when he was disposed to be at quiet, or to rest aft
his labors.

Me conuiuam solu^ abducebat sibi. He took awey with him me and no man else to
be his guest, and to dyne or suppe with him.

Sic homo est. Such is his facion, or, this is the facion of the man.

Inuidere omnes mihi, ac mordere clanculum. for inuidebant and mordebant. Every
man had envy or grudged at me, and spoke very ill by me behind my back.

Vbi molestus mihi magis est. When he began to be some what busy with me, or,
when he disquieted or vexed me, or, would not let me be in rest.

Eo ne es ferox, quia habes imperiu^ in beluas? Eo ne for Ideo ne. etc. Art
thou therefore hasty on men, because thou art a master of brute beastis?

Pulchre me hercle dictum, et sapie^ter. well spoken by my southe and wisely.

Quid illud, quo pacto Rhodiu^ tetigerim in conuiuio, nunquid tibi dixi? What
that, how I took up a fellow of Rhodes as we sat at the table did I never
tell it the?

Nunquam, sed narra obsecro, Never, but tell it, I beseech you.

Plus millies iam audiui, I have herd it already a thousand times and above.

Vna in conuiuio erat hic, quem dico, Rhodius adolescentulus. This young fellow
of Rhodes, that I speak of, and I sat together at a table.

Coepit me irridere. He began to mock me.

Quid ais, inquam, ho^ impude^s? what say est thou shameless or thou saucy
fellow quod I?

Tuum ne, obsecro, hoc dictum erat? I pray you heartily was that your saying?

Audieram saepe, and fertur in primis. I had herd it many times, and it is a
saying as comen, as any is.

Dolet dictu^ imprudenti adolescenti. This word or saying grieved the folysh
young man.

Risu omnes qui aderant emori. All that were in company, were almost deed
with laughture. Emori.i. emoriebantur, per Antiptofin. For Donatus in many
places notes, that the infinytife mode in such spekinges is more vehemente and
of more virtue* strength, and efficacy, than is the indicative.

Metuebant omnes iam me. Then were they all aferd of me.

Id ut ne fiat, haec res sola est remedio. That that shall not be, this thing
only is a remedy and help.

Phaedriam intromittamus comessatum. Let us have in Phedria to make good chere
with us. Comessor, aris, ssatus su^, sari, depon. is proprely that we say in
english, to bankette after suppar, or, to make ryer suppars. Sue. in
Domitiano. Conuiuebatur frequenter and large, sed paene raptim, certe no^ ultra
solis occasu^, nec ut postea comessaretur. He took repastes and feasted both
often and also eat much at ones, and yet in manner never but by snatches, and
of truth never longer tha^ till the soon went down, nor never to banquet nor to
have any ryer suppar after. Plau. in Rude^te. Veru^ si uoletis plausum fabulae
huie claru^ dare, comessatu^ oe^s uenitote ad me ad a^nos sedecim. But and if
you wol clappe your hands to gether, that hit sownde loud in approving and
allowyng this come die, that we have played, come every one of you home and
banquet or make good chere for these sixtene years. Liui. L. x. De bell. Mace.
Reports that Demetrius, after that he had made a certain suppar to his
companions said unto them. Quin comessatu^ ad fratre^ imus? Why go we not to my
brothers to bankette? And of comessari comes a nowne verbal comessatio,
deriuied (as Festus Po. witnesses) a uicis, quas graeci komas dicunt, that is
of littel stretis. For in such men dwelled before that towns were builded,
and there one would bid an other to drinking or ban kettynge for good
neighborhood. For of the greek nowne kome, is deriuied a verb komazo to
bankette, and of komazo is derived comessor in latin, prima longa, with one m.
Of comessor comes comessatio for bankettynge, or making good chere after
supper. Suet. in Vitel. Epulas trifariam sem per, interdum quadrifariam
dispertiebat, in ientacula, pra^dia, coenas, et comessationes, He divided his
meals into three evermore, and somtymes into four, that is to wit, break
fastes, dyners, suppers, and reresuppers or bankettis, or collations after
supper.

Pamphilam cantatum prouocemus. Let us pray Pamphilam to singe, or, let us
call forth Pamphilam to singe. For as I think Terence uses here prouocare for
euocare, to call forth, as prodire.i. exire to go forth, and that is his most
propre significacion, and specially in this place of Terence. for he said
before, Intromittamus Pamphilum, Let us have in Pamphilum, and now contrary to
that prouocemus Pamphilam, Let us call forth Pamphilam. So Plau. in Pseu.
Herus si domi est tuus, quin prouocas, If thy master be at home, why do thou
not call him forth. Prouoco has other significations, but they perteyne not
to this place.

Par pari referto. Do like for like.

Quando illud quod tu das amat, te amat, quando pro quoniam, Saying that he
sets store by that that you give unto him, he loves your self well.

Metuet semper, quem ipse nunc capit fructum, ne quando iratus tu alio
conferas. He wol always fear left that fruit and profit, which him self
takes and has now, you being angrye with him, upon displeasure wol bestow
an other way.

Mihi isthuc non in mentem uenerat. I remembered not so much.

In the second Scene.
O Thais mea, o meum suauium, quid agitur? O my dear Thais, o mine own
swetynge, how is it with you?

Ecquid nos amas? Do you love me ought? or, set you any store by me?

Eamus ad coenam, quid stas? Go we to supper, where about stand you?

Vbi uis, non moror. when pleases you, there is no let in me.

Adibo atque adsimulabo quasi uunc exeam. I wol go to them, and make as though
I com forth but now.

Iturus ne quopiam es? Are your about to go any whither?

Hunc uides? Se you this man?

Quid stamus? cur non imus hinc? where about do we stand? why go we not hens?

Quaeso ut liceat dare huic quae uolumus. I pray you that we may have license
to give unto this man such things as we would.

Pace tua. By your leave.

Perpulchra, credo, dona, haud nostris similia. Very goodly gifts I am sure,
not like nor to be compared unto mine.

Res indicabit. The thing shall show hit self.

Heus, iubete istos for as exire ocyus. How syrs, bead those fellows there come
forth quickly.

Procedere tu huc. Come thou forth here, and stand by me.

Est ex Aethiopia usque hic. This fellow is come as far as from Ethiopia.

Vbi tu es? accede huc. where art thou there? come hither.

Ita me dij ament honestus est. As God help me hit is a goodly fellow: or (as
we use commonly to speak) as I shall be saved: or, as I trust to be sauedde,
hit is a goodly fellow.

Tacent, satis laudant. They say nothing, and in that they praise hit
sufficiently. For holding a man's peace and saying nothing, specially in a
man's adversary, is a certain kind of preysynge or granting. whereof there
goes a proverb in latin: Qui tacet, consentire uidetur, He that holds his
piece, and says nothing, seems to consent, that is to say, seems to think as
the other party said, and to be of the same mind.

Fac periculum in literis. Prove him in learning. There is understood, de eo.

Fac periculum in palaestra. Prove him in wrastlynge.

Fac periculum in musicis. Prove him in singinge and playenge on instrumentis.

Non sibi soli postulat te uiuere. He does not desire you to bestow all your
life on him alone.

Non postulat sua causa excludi caeteros. He desires not to have all others
shut out of doors for his sake.

Neque pugnas narrat, neque cicatrices suas ostentan. He craketh not of the
batailes, that he has been in, nor makes no boste she wyng the skarres of the
wou^des that he has had.

Vbi molestum non erit. when it shall be no disease unto you.

Vbi tu uoles. when it shall be your will.

Vbi erit tibi tempus. When you shall have time, or be at leisure.

Sat habet si tum recipitur. He is contented, if he may than come into your
house, or, he desires no more, but at such times to be received into your
house, or company.

Apparet seruum hunc esse domini pauperis. This fellow seems to be servant to
some poor man, or, to serve some poor man to his master.

Nemo posset hunc perpeti. No man were able long to abide or suffer this fellow.

Sat scio. I know very well.

Te esse puro infra omneis infimos homineis. I repute the to be the most
villayne of al villaynes.

Qui huic assentari animu^ induxeris. That coudest find in thy heart to
flatter such a fellow as this is. Val. li. 5. ele. c. 66. shows the
difference between these three verbes assentor, adulor, and blandior. Assentari
is to flater any body, affirmyng his sayengis and upholding his ye, and his
nay, or preysyng him to much, or else many times other wise than the truth
is, to the end to get some profit and auantage thereby, and it is proprely in
words. And therefore this kind of flaterye called Assentation, is not in any
brute beast, but only in man. Plau. Exte^plo, quasi res cum ea esser mihi,
coepi assentari, mulier quicquid dixerat, idem ego dicebam. A non as though I
had had to do with her, I began to hold up her Ye and her Nay, and what so
ever she said, I said the same. Ide^. Assentandum est quicquid hic mentibitur,
what so ever lie this fellow shall make, we must uphold it and say as he does.
Tere^ce self in the second scene of the second act of this same comedy,
does best of al declare the nature of this verb assentor, aris: where Gnato
says thus: Hos consector, hisce ego non paro me ut rideant, sed his ultro ar
rideo, et eoru^ ingenia admiror simul, quicquid dicu^t, laudo, id rursum si
negant, laudo id quoque. Negat quis, nego: ait, aio. Postremo imperaui egomet
mihi omnia assen tari, is questus nunc est multo uberrimus. Such men do I
follow at the tail, and among such persons I do not so fashon my self,
that they may laugh at me, but contrary wise, what so ever they say or do, I
show them a merry countenance of mine own self, and also make a great meruailing
at their high wittis. what so ever they say, I commend it, that if they deny
the same again, that also I co^me^de: if a man say nay, I say nay also: if he
say ye, I say ye to. And for a conclusion to be short, I master and rule mine
own self, to uphold his ye and his nay, and to say as he says in all manner
things, for that is the next way now a days to get money enough.

Adulari is to flater an other man in humblyng them selfs and being
serviceable about him, and to labor by such facions to win and get his favor,
whether it be by voice and words, orels by gesture of the body, or by any
other way and mean what so ever it be. Nonius Marc. says thus: Adulatio est
proprie canum blandimentu^, quod ad homines consuetudine translatum est,
Adulation proprely signifies the fauning and leapinge of dogs upon their
masters, from which propretie by translation it is applied to men only by
use of speaking, and not by the propre signification of the word. Some
gra^marians form adulor of the word au la (which is latin for a princis
court, where such flatery is very much used) by changinge the dipthonge au,
into u long, taking that etymology out of Ovid, where he says Agmen adulantu^
media procedit ab aula. But Valla holds with them, that deriuie adulor out of
the greek word dulos, which is feruus. For that (as al agree) adulari is a
servile thing, and is only in such as can abide to be subject and bond to
an other body, and not in honest men. Where note, that adulor is a verb
deponent, and governs a dative case. Val. Max. Diogenes Syracusis, cum olera
ei lauanti Aristippus dixisset: Si Dionysio adulari uelles, ista non esses, Imo
inquit, si tu ista esse uelles, Dionysio non ad ulareris, Diogenes in the city
of Sarragous (when that Aristippus had said unto him, as he was washyng herbs
for a salet, these words, If thou would seek favor of Dionysius the tyran
and flater him, thou should not eat such meat as that) answered and said
again: Nay if thou would eat such meat as this, thou should not need to
flater Dionysius. Quint. Transeo oblata^ uole^ ti muneru^ uacationem, et
blandius quam militiae disciplina postuler, adulatum militi tribunu^, I pass
over that he had offered unto him liberty to be void of al offices in the
army, when he would none of it, but refused it: and that the Tribune labored
to win him more gently than the law of arms or the order of warfare required.
Yet Cornelius Tacitus joined the same verb deponent with an accusative,
saying: Tigillinum, aut quem alium adulatus est, He flatered Tigillinus or
some other man. There is also red adulo, as, aui, are, an active and
transsitiue, gouernynge an accusative case. Cic. li. 2. tusc. quest. where he
translateth certain verses out of a tragedy of the greek poet Eschylus speking
in the person of Prometheus of the egle, that fed of his liver: Tum iecore
opimo ferta, et satiata affatim, clangorem fundit uastum et sublime aduolans
pinnata cauda, nostru^ adulat sanguinem, Than she being stuffed and satisfied
even at full, with as much as she would eat of my fat liver, gave an horrible
shreche, and taking her flight high up in to the air, with her forked and
styffe fethered tail houered, playenge and dalyinge at my blood. Al be it the
Dictionaries take adulat there, for lambit or bibit, as who should say in
english, She sweted her lips licking and suckyng up my blood. And Cic. li.
1. of. used the passive of the same: Cauendum est, ne assentatoribus
patefaciamus aures, neue adulari nos sinamus. we must beware that we open not
our ears to flaterers, nor suffer our selfs to be won or overcome with
faunynge or humble behavior of others toward us. For there Cicero does
manifestly put a difference between assentation and adulation. Lucretius used
adulo, as, aui, as a verb neutre absolutely, that is to say not ioynyng any
case with him in that verse: Long alio pacto gannitu uocis adulant. And
Linaker in the place of Val. Max. above cited redeth thus, Diogenes Syracusis,
cum olera ei lauanti Aristippus dixisset, Si Dionysio adula ri uelles, ista non
esses: Imo inquit, si tu Dionysium non adulares, illa non esses. But I fnid not
that lettre in any exemplarie that I have hitherto seen, but the co^me^taries
read, si Dionysio non adulares, that it be a verb neutre gouernyng a dative,
where Olinerus notes, that many verbes be neutres in o, and deponentes in one
and the same significacion as populo and populor, impertio and impertior, adulo
and adulor. etc.

Blandiri belongs proprely to touching and handlynge, and by vnpropre using
it is by metaphore translated and referred to other parts of the body, ye and
many times to the mind, exemplorum plena sunt omnia.

Iam ne imus? Shall we go now?

Hos prius introducam, et quae uolo simul imperabo. I wol first have in these
folks, and give in commandment such things as I would have done.

Postea continuo exeo. That done I woll come forth by and by. For in such
manner spekinges as this, and Iam ne imus? before. and Ego hinc abeo next
following, with others like, where as the propretie of our english tou^ge is
to speak by the sign of the future tense, shall or woll, the phrase of the
latin tou^gue is to speak by the present tense.

Ego hinc abeo. I wol begone hens.

Quid tibi ego multa dicam? what should I make many words with the?

Domini similis es. Such master such man.

Quid rides? whereat laughest thou?

Praecurre, ut sint domi parata omnia. Run before, that al things may be ready
at home.

Diligenter fac cures. Se thou bestere the busyly.

Si Ghremes huc forte uenerit, ores ut ma neat. If Chremes shall happly come
hither, desire him to tarry.

Si id non commodum est, ores ut redeat. If he may not conveniently so do, pray
him to come again.

Si id non poterit, ad me adducito. If he may not do that neither, bring him
to me.

Domi adsitis facite. Se that you keep home or, se that ye keep you within the
house.

Vos me sequimini. Come you after me.

In the third scene.
Quanto magis magisque cogito. The more and more that I cast in my mind, or
bethynke me.

Dabit mihi magnu^ malum. He woll do me a great shrewde turn.

h Cum primum iussit me ad se accersi. when te first commanded me to be sent
fore un o him.

Quid tibi cum illa? s. est negotij, uel rei. per eclipsin. what have thou to
do with her?

Ne noram quidem. In feythe I could not have told.

Vbi ueni, caussam ut ibi manerem, repperit. when I was ones come, he found an
excuse or occasion to make me tarry there.

Ait rem seriam uelle agere mecu^. He said he would commone with me of a sad
and weyghty matier.

Iam tum erat suspicio dolo malo haec fieri omnia. Even very than I mystrusted
all ready, that all to gether should be done by fraud and collusion. Dolus
(says Donat) a dolendo, that it makes men sorry when they are begyled, uel a
dolando, that is hurting or diminishing, for dolos in greek is laesio in
latin, hurtyng in english, and there of it is taken for all manner gyle and
deceyte or trumperie. Doli uocabulo (says Nonius Mar.) nunc tantum in malis
utimur, apud antiquos autem etiam in bonis rebus utebantur: unde ahuc dicimus,
sine dolo malo, nimirum quia solebat dici et bonus, and for his authorite and
exeample he cites this place of Terence: so that dolus is al manner deceit,
and dolus malus is that that we use to say in english, craft and collusion.
Read de dolo malo in the third book of Cice. de offic. where among other
things he says thus: Non dum enim Aquilius collega and familiaris meus
pertulerat de dolo malo for mulas. In quibus ipsis cum ex eo quereretur, quid
esset dolus malus, respondebat, cum esset aliud simulatum, aliud actum. For my
fellow in office and familiar friend Aquilius had not yet made and
established the form of the writes of dolo malo. In which writes when it was
demanded of him what was dolus malus, he made answer and said, that dolus
malus was, when one thing was pretended and outwardely showed, and an other
thing done and executed in deed.

Ipse accumbere mecum, for accumbebat, He sat hard by me at the table.

Mihi sese dare for dabat. He gave atte^da^ce on me, to do what I would have
him to do.

Sermonem querere.i. querebat. He went about to find co^municacion.
Sermonem.i. sermonis materiam et causas.

Quam pridem pater mihi et matter mortui essent. s. rogabat. He asked me how
long a gone my father and my mother dyed.

Sperat se id a me auellere. He hops to pulle it away from me.

Haec cur quaeritet? why should he enquere such things?

Ea si uiuit, annos nata est sedecim non mayor. If she be alyne, she is syxtene
year old, and no older.

Thais, ego quam sum, maiuscula est. Thais is somewhat elder than I am.

Misit orare ut uenirem serio. He sent one to pray me to come for a sad and
weighty mattier.

Aut dicat quod uult, aut molestus no^ sier, Either let him tell me what he
would have, orels let him not trouble nor disquiet me.

Non hercle ueniam tertio. In says I wol not comme the third time.

Hic quis est? who is there?

Ego sum Chremes. It is I Cbremes.

O capitulum lepidissimum. O littel petite feate goldpol. Albeit it is the
figure synech doche, that is to say, a part of the hole, set for the hole.
capitulum for hominem.

Rus eo. I go into the country.

Apud nos hic mane dum redeat ipsa. Tari here with us until she come her self.

Nihil minus. No point.

Si isthuc ita certum est tibi. If you be utterly determined, and appointed on
that.

Illuc transies, ubi illa est. Go thither, where she is.

In the fourth Scene.
Heri aliquot adolesce^tuli coimus, Yesterday three or four young men of us met
together.

Cheream ei rei praefecimus. we made Cherea the chief captain and doer in
that mattier. Here Terence spoke of a promys, that was made for to meet to
gether, and to make good chere: so that in this place and meaning, it might
conveniently be englished thus: we made Cheream our steward and master of the
feast.

Locus, tempus constitutum est. The place and time was appointed.

Praeterijt te^pus. The time or hour is past.

Quo in loco dictum est, parati nihil est. In the place, that was named, is
nothing ordained.

Neque scio quid dicam, aut quid coniectem. And I can not tell what I may say,
or what I may coniecte and think.

Mihi hoc negotij caeteri dedere, illum ut quaeram. The rest of the company
have put me to this labor, or has assigned me this office to go seek him.

Visam si domi est. I woll go se if he be at home.

Quisnam hinc a Thaide exit? who comes forth from Thais house here? Quisnam
for quis: It is called parelcon, that is wha^ a letter or a syllable is added,
which makes nor helps nothing to the sense.

Is est, an non est? Is it he, or is it not?

Quid hoc hominis? i. qui hic homo est? what manner of fellow is this? or, what
manner a fellow have we here?

Qui hic ornatus est? what manner apparel call you this?

Nequeo satis mirari, neque conijcere. I can not leave merueilynge, nor
perfectly coniect.

Libet sciscitari. I have a phansy to enquere, or, I have a great desire to
ask. Sciscitor, aris, etc. is to ask to the end for to know a thing, as the
voice self shows, for it comes of scio. Percontari proprely is to ask to
the end to reprove a man, and to take him in a trippe. Interrogo is also to ask
to the end to know: Al be it it is indifferently used for percontor, to oppose
a man, as we say.

In the fyfte Scene.
Nu^ quis hic est? Is there any body here?

Nemo homo est. There is no man. Hadrianus de ser. lat. notes, that homo is
elega^tly some times joined with nemo, not with standing, that nemo is the
same that nullus homo.

lam ne erumpere hoc mihi licet gaudium? May I now out with this my joy and
gladness?

Proh Iuppiter. Oh lord.

Nunc est, interfici cum perpeti me possum ne hoc gaudium contaminet uita
aegritudine aliqua. Now at this present time so it is that I could be
content to dye, that life might not here after disteyne this gladness that I am
in, by any mysfortune, displeasure, or sorrow.

Ab eo gratiam hanc inibo. I wol get or have that thank of him.

Quid est, quod sic gestis? what is the matter that you lepe and skyppe so?
Gestire says Donat) is to notify what the mind thinks or desires, by the
mouynge and gesture of the body. And it is manifest (says he) that it is by
translation taken of the propretie of brute beasts, and referred to man. And
hit may be referred as well to sorrow as to gladness, or to any other
affectionate appetite or passion of the mind.

Quid sibi hic uestitus quaerit? what means this apparel?

Quid est, quod laetus sis? what is the matier why thou should be so glad?

Quid me aspectas? why stand thou gasynge upon me? or, what starest thou
upon me?

O festus dies. O a high and a merry day.

Amice salve. Good fellow god you save, or o loving friend god rest you merry.

Nemo est, quem ego magis nu^c cuperem uidere quam te. There is no man living,
whom I would more gladly se now at this present time, than the.

Narra isthuc quaeso, quid siet. I pray the tell what the matier is. siet for
sit, per epenthesin. Epenthesis is when a letter or a syllable is added in the
myddis of a word.

Imo ego te obsecro, ut audias. Nay mary I pray you, that you wol here it.

Nostin ne ha^c, quam amat frater? Do you not know her here, that my brother
is in love withal?

Elegans spectator formarum. Very well skilled or seen in fair women, or, a
deyntie fellow in choosing of fair women. For so does Donatus expowne it.

Quid multa uerba? s. loquar. what should I make many words?

Forte fortuna domi erat. As hap was he was at home. Haec fors (says Nonius
Mar.) o fors, ab hac forte, is proprely a chance, that so dainly and casually
falls or happens at a time: and Fortuna is the goddess self, that is, the
fortune that every person has given unto him, that such or such things
shall happen to him, or go from him. Accius in Astyanacte: Itera in quibus
partibus, namque audire uolo, si est quem exopto, et quo captus modo, fortuna
ne, an forte reperitur, Tell and rehearse again in what parties, for I would
feign here, whether it be he that I desire to se, or not, and how he was
gotten, and whether he was fou^den by fortune, orels by chance and casualtie.
Ide^ in Andro. Multi, quibus natura praua magis, quam fors aut fortuna obsuit,
Many, unto whom the frowardnes of their naughty nature has done more harm,
then has either chance or fortune. Lucilius in Saty. Cui parilem fortuna
locum, fatumque tulit fors, Unto whom his fortune gave like place and degree or
state of living, and chance gave destynie of death semblable and answereable
to the same. Ibid. Aut forte omnino, aut fortuna uincere bello, Either by
blind chance or else by fortune to get the victory in bataile. Many times
fors fortuna and forte fortuna ar joined to gether by subunion, as says Donat
in the third scene of the third act of Hecyra: and then it betokens bone
adventure or sudden good chance and good fortune. Teren. in Phor. O fortuna, o
fors fortuna quantis co^moditatibus Antiphoni hunc onerastis diem. Oh fortune,
oh most good fortune, with how many commodities and good chances have thou
replenishid this day unto Antiphon. Vbi Donat. Fortuna dicta est i^certa res,
fors fortu^a euentus fortunae bonus. Fortuna is called a thing uncertain, Fors
fortuna is a good chance and end of that thing that was uncertain. And again
in the same place fortuna and fors fortuna be two diverse things: For fors
fortuna is the goddess, whom they serve that live having no occupacion,
whereby to get their living: whose temple was on the fursyde of Tyberis: so
that fortuna is in uncertain and fors fortuna is in good chance. And there of
forte fortuna some time as a nowne and sometime as an aduerbe, is that that we
say in english, In a good hour: or, As good hap was.

Submonuit me Parmeno. Parmeno put me in mind by a bye word.

Tacitus, citius audies. Holding your peace you shall here it the sooner.

Vt uestem cum eo mutem. That I should thunge garmentis with him.

Quid ex ea re capies commodi? what benefit shall thou get by that thing?

Number parua caussa, aut parua ratio est? Is that a small cause or a small
consyderation?

Mihi, ne abscedam, imperat. He charged me, not to go away.

Ego ad coenam hinc eo. I go hens forth to suppar.

In conclaui sedet. He syttith in the parlour.

Iouem Danaae misisse aiunt quonda^ in gremium imbre^ aureum. They say, that
Iuppiter did on a time let fall into the lap of Danae a shower of drops of
gold.

Impendio magis animus gaudebat mihi, My heart was meruaillous ioy^ul and still
more and more. Impendio aduerbium intendendi is as much as impense, ualde
apprime, greatly, or very much: and it is commonly joined with the comparatiue
degree. Aul. Gel. li. 1. ca. 2. Erat ibidem nobiscum simul adolescens,
philosophiae sectator disciplinae, ut ipse dicebat, stoicae, sed loquatior
impendio, et promptior, There was in the same place to gether in company with
us a young man, student in philosophy, and (as he said him self) of the sect
of the stoikes, but very talkatiue, or toto full of words, and marvelous ready
thereto. And therefore it is red elegantly joined with magis. Cic. Atti. At ille
impendio magis odit senatum, But he toto much hatith the senators or the
nobles of the cite. And also with minus. Plau. in Aul. Atque ille minus minus
que impendio curare, minusque me impertire honoribus, But he set by me still
less and less by a great way, and did me less honor, or showed me less
courtesy.

Ego homuncio hoc non facerem? Should I a fellow of no reputation not do that?

Ego illud feci, ac lubens. I did that same, and that with all my heart.

Dum haec mecum reputo. while I cast or recounted these things in my mind.
Putamus instantia, reputamus praeterita. Donat.

Accersitur lauatu^ uirgo. The maiden was called to goo and wash her.

Sto, expectans si quid mihi imperent. I stood still and looked whether they
would beade me to do any thing.

Cape flabellum, and uentulum facito. Take a flabelle, and make a little wind.
Flabellu^, a diminutif of flabrum, as labellu^, of labru^.

For As siml omnes proruu^t se. They rushed out of the doors all together in a
clustre.

Abeunt lauatu^. They went to wash them.

Perstrepunt, ita ut fit, domini ubi absunt. They made a rumblyng in every
corner that all the house was to litel for them, as commenly it chances, when
the master of dame is out of the way.

Interea somnus uirginem opprimit. In the mean while sleep took the maiden,
or, the maiden fell in sleep.

Pessulum ostio obdo. I sparred, or bolted the door. Pessulus, li, lo.i.
paruus pes, a diminutyue of the nowne pes, and it is a bolte or some like
instrument, with which the doors be shit fast. Apuleus. Subdita claui pessulos
reduco. I put the kaye into the hole, and pulled back the bolte. Plaut. in
Aulul. Occlude fores, ambobus pessulis, iam ego hic adero. Shit fast the door
with both the boltes: I woll be here again by and by.

An ego occasionem mihi ostentata^, tam breue^, tam optata^, tam inspaerata^
amittere^? Should I have leat goo such oportunite and occasion, being offered
me so short of time and respyt so greatly desired, so soden and nothing looked
sore?

Sane hercle ut dicis. In good south as iou sai

Interim de symbolis quid actu^ est. In the mean season what is done with our
banquet money. Symbolu^, li, lo, of the neutre gendre, is the money that diverse
persons lay together as in a common purse to make good chere withal, and that
such ba^kettis are called collacions, a collatum, tu, that is of laying
together every one his porcyon.

Perlo^ge est, sed ta^to otius properemus. It is very far hens, but let us
make so much the more speed.

Domo exulo. I am banished from our house, as who should say I dare nor goo
thither, nor come there.

Metuo fratre^ ne intus sit. I fear my broher lest he be within.

Metuo patre^ ne rure redierit. I fear my father lest out of the country be
come again.

Eamus ad me, ibi proximu^ est ubi mutes. s, ueste^. Goo we home to my house,
there is the nighest place, where you may change you.

Consilium uolo capere una tecum. I woll take counsel with you, and se what
is best to be done.

Out of the fourth act, In the first scene.
Timeo, ne quam ille hodie turbam faciat, I fear, lest that fellow wol make
some business and trouble to day.

Id faciebat retinendi illius caussa. That he did for to keep that other man
there.

Ad eam rem tempus non erat. There was no time for that.

Heus puer Pamphilam accerse. How thou lad go call Pamphilam.

Minime gentium. No, in no wise in the world. Minime gentium (says Festus)
di cebant ueteres pro eo quod est omnium ge^tium iudicio minime esse faciendu^,
The latin men of old time did use and take these words, minime gentiu^, as
who should say thus, a thing as all the people in the world would judge, in no
manner wise to be done. So that minime gentium (as restifieth Valla in annot.
contra Rau.) is taken for minime, and gentium is a voice either emphaticos, or
else as an expletiue added to these aduerbes, that follow, minime. Teren. in
Adel. Get. Quapropter quoquo pacto caelato opus est. Sostrat. Ah minime
gentium, non faciam, Get. wherefore in any wise hit is expediente, that it be
kept secret. Sostrat. Tushe nay in no wise, I wol not so do. Quo. Plaut. in
Rud. Non hercle quo hinc nunc gentiu^ aufugiam, scio, By my treuth I wot not
whither in the world to go now from hense. Quando. Plau. in Am. Amph. Quis
te misit furcifer? Sos. Qui me rogat. Amphitrye. Qua^do gentiu^? Amph. who sent
the knave? Sos. who says who? Amph. when? Long. Cic. Atti. lib. 6. Non quo me
aliquid iuuare posses, quippe res est in manibus, tu autem abes long gentium.
Not that you could do me any help, For the matier is all ready in hand, and
you ar distaunt a great way from me. Idem P. Cornif. li. 12. ep. fa. Oppressa
omnia sunt, nec habent ducem boni, nostrique long gentium absunt, Al things
ar put down by oppression, and the good and honest men have no man to be their
captain, for that ioly fellows of ours that used to kylle up tyrauntes be
far out of the way. And also with these aduerbes nusquam, unde, undecunque,
ubinam, ubiuis, ubique, ubi, with all other their compou^des, as sicubi. etc.
where note that with these last rehearsed is red joined not only gentium, but
also terrarum, and locorum, and with ubi is also joined so^tymes the voice of
the genitive singular, loci, per subunione^. Plaut. in Cap. Propemodu^ ubi loci
tuae fortunae su^t, facile intelligis, Thou perceyuest very well in manner, in
what case or state thou and all that ever thou have does stand. Ide^ in Mer.
Na^ si istuc ius est senecta aetate scortari senes, ubi loci res summa nostra
publica? For if this be leefull that old folks in their old age may use the
company of harlottes, then where is our great high common weal? And in all
these the compoundes signify no more than the simples.

Vbi primum poterit, se illinc subducet, sat scio. As soon as he may
possibly, he woll privily stele away from thens, I know very well.

In the secunde scene.
Du^ rus eo. As I was going into the co^tre.

Vbi quid in animo est molestiae. wha^ there is any grefe in the heart, or,
when there is any groudge in the stomach.

Coepi mecum, inter uias, aliam re^ ex alia cogitare. By the way as I went I
began to cast in my mind, and to remember one thing of an other.

Du^ haec reputo preterij imprudens uillam. while I recou^ted those things in
my mind I was gone past my house in the country. Villa proprely is a ferme
house, or, a manor house, or any other house, made and set without the city to
dwell in, and to have housbandry occupied, deryued (as Varro L. i. de re
rustic. c. 2. says, a uehendo of caryenge) for that the housband man carries
in thither such fruits as come out of the ground: and semblably carries
them out again, when they must be sold. In uilla most commonly ar two
partis, the one that the fermer or what so ever person else, occupyinge the
housbandrie belonginge unto the same, dwells in, and there makes or keeps
all manner instruments apperteynynge to his housbandrie, as corn, carts,
yokes for oxen. etc. and that was called by the latin men of old time Villa
rustica. The other part is that, which the lord or owner reserueth and
keeps for him self to dwell in for his pleasure, and that was called Villa
urbana, because it was in all points as goodely and as well appointed as
becomed a house standing within the city too be, and was more pleasauntely
builded and more goodly decked, and better furnished with all implementes of
household, and kept more nete and clean then for a house of the country. Varro
li. 1. de re rust. Fructuosior certe fu^dus est propter aedificia, si porius ad
antiquorum diligentiam, quam ad horum luxuriam dirigas aedificationem. Illi
enim ad fructuum rationem faciebant, hi ad libidines indomitas. Itaque illorum
uillae rusticae era^t maiores quam urbanae, quae nu^c sunt pleraeque, Of a very
certaynte a man's ground is the more fruitful and profitable for the housynge
that is builded upon it, if a man order and fashon the buyldinge and framing
and setting up of it after the diligent fashon of me^ of old time, rather tha^
after the riottous and prodigal fashon of men that are now a days. For the
said men of old time made their buyldynges in the countreye, as was most
convenient for the fruits of the ground to be received there into: and now a
days they build for pleasure, that is never ruled by any reason, nor never
satisfied: Therefore their uillae rusticae were beggar and larger than many
uillae urbanae that are now a days.

Long iam abieram. I was all ready gone be yonde it a great way.

Cum sensi, redeo rursum. when I perceived it, I came back again.

Vbi ueni ad diuerticulum, constiti. when I came to the place were the way
turns in, I stood still.

Occaepi mecum cogitare. I began to think in my mind.

Biduum hic manendum est. I must abide here by the space of two days.

Quid tum postea? what then?

Si non tangendi copia est, eho ne uidendi quidem erit? If I may not have
leave to touch it, what shall I not be suffered to look upon it neither?

Si illud non licet, saltem hoc licebit. If I may not do that, yet at leest
way this may I do.

Timida subito egreditur Pythias. Pythias being in fear, comes forth hastily.

In the third scene.
Vbi ego illum scelerosum, atque impium in ueniam? where may I find that
ungracious and wicked fellow?

Ludificatus est uirginem. He begyled the maiden.

Veste^ omnem misere dissidit. He all to cut her cote, that pity it was to se.

Ipsam capillo conscidit. He tore her by the hear.

Vt ego unguibus illi in oculos inuolem. Oh how I would fle on him with my
nails, and scrache out his eyes.

Nescio quid absente nobis turbatum est domi. There has been some business or
trouble, or there has been some shrewed turn done at home, what so ever hit
is, why^e I have been away forth. Here is (says Donat) either absente set
for a preposition gouernynge an ablative case, as when it is said coram nobis,
coram amicis. etc. orels (which I more allow, and think better said) the
figure that is imitation of speaking of the old time. For the antiquite used
absente nobis for absente me, or, absentibus nobis. Plau. in Am. Si in action
ha^c absente nobis inuenerit puer, If the boy found and took her with the
manner in the deed doing, while I was away. And like wise praesente nobis, for
praesentibus nobis. Plau. in Am. Nec nobis praesente aliquis nisi seruus
Aphricanus adest, Neither any man but the servant that came out of Aphrique was
here while I was present. Ibide^. Nec nobis praesente quis qua^ aliquis ausi
sunt, And while I was present they durst not, not any one of them all.
Pomponius. Qui apud forum praesente restibus mihi uendidit, Which sold it
unto me openly in the street before witness. Idem. Sine ergo isthuc, praesente
amicis inter coenam, Let this alone now, we woll speak more of it in the
suppar time when our friends be present. Varro in Marcellum, Id prae sent
legatis omnibus, exercitu pronunciat, He spoke that openly in the host all the
ambassadors being there present.

Quid festinas, aut quem quaeris? why hiest thou so fast, or whom sekest thou
fore?

Abi hinc quo dignus es, cum donis tuis ta^ lepidis. Il speed or ill luck take
you for me with your gifts being so goodly and propre.

Quid istuc est rei? what is the matier?

Rogas me? Do thou ask me?

Quas turbas dedit? what trouble, or what array has he made?

Virgine^ uitiauit. He has deflored a mayde^.

Temulentus es. Thou art dronken.

Vtinam sic sint qui mihi male uolunt. would god that mine evil wyllers were in
that case.

Quidnam isthuc monstri fuit? what a monstre was that? what monstru^ is, it is
showed before.

Ego illum nescio qui fuerit. I know him not what he was.

Hoc quod fecit res ipsa indicat. This that he has done the thing self does
plainly show.

Ille bonus uir nusquam apparet. That honest man can not be seen.

Suspicor aliquid domo abeuntem abstulisse. I mystruste that he stole and took
away somewhat, when he went out of the house.

Nequeo mirari satis, quo ille abire ignauus possit longius, nisi si domum
forte ad nos redierit. I can not marvel enough whither that fuske could go
far, except if per aue^ture he be gone home again to our house.

Vise amabo number ibi sit. Go se as ever I shall love you, or, as ever I shall do
you good turn, whether he be there. Amabo is after some an aduerbe of
exhorting, or after some other an interiection of flateringe and loving, and it
is the same that we say in english, as ever I shall do you good turn, or
pleasure, or as ever I shall love you. Plaut. Noli amabo Amphitruo irasci Sosiae
caussa mea, As ever I shall love you Amphitrou be not angri with Sosia for my
sake. And hit is indifferently referred to one singular orels to many. Idem in
Trucu. Properate, mensam afferte amabo, Hye at ones and bring the table I
pray you. Orators use in the same signification also amabo te, and ama me.
Cic. Cassio, Et amabo te, cum dabis post hac aliquid domum literarum, mei
memineris. And, as ever I shall love you, when you shall from hensforth send
any letters home, remember me. Id. Attico. Amabo te incumbe in eam rem, and ad
me scribe. As ever I shall owe my hearty love, stycke well to that matter, and
write unto me, Eidem. Amame, no^ libenter uidi, sed modo succenset, modo
gratias agit. As ever you may trust, or love me, I saw it not gladly, but
somwhile he is angry, and somwhyle he gives thanks. Se more examples in
Hadrian, De fm. Latino. Linacer says that amabo, and amabote, is enallage,
that is a verb (says he) or a hole sense, for an aduerbe. For enallege is,
when one part of speech is put for an other. And amabo has not the secunde
syllable short, as Calepine and Thesaurus Linguae lati. and others would have
it, setting acutum accentum in prima, but long as Catullus. Amabo mea dulcis
Ipsiphilla. Martial. l. 8. Dic uerum mihi Marce dic amabo. Sidonius ad Felicem.
Dic dic quod peto magne dic amabo. That no man be deceived in accenting that
word.

Tam infandu^ facinus ne audiui quide^. So abominable a deed I have never
herd of.

Pol ego amatores audieram esse mulierum eos maximos. In deed I had herd say
that they were the greattest lovers of women that be.

Illum aliquo conclusissem. I would have shut him up fast in some corner.

In the fourth scene.
Exi for as sceleste. Come forth thou naughty pack.

At etiam restitas? what do thou stop and draw back still.

Prodi fugitive. Com forth thou runaway.

Illud uide os ut sibi distorsit carnifex.i. scelestus. Se yonde how the

Iam ornabat fugam. He was even about to run away.

Habes ne hominem amabo? Have you got ten the fellow I pray you?

An tu hu^c credidisti esse, obsecro? Did you think that hit had been this
manne, I pray you?

Ne comparandus hic quidem ad illum est. Nay this fellow is not to be compared
with him, or this fellow is not to be likned to him.

Ille erat honesta facie et liberali. He had a goodely and a welfauored face.
or, he was both fair and well favored. For that signifies liberalis,
sometimes joined with facies, as here: or with forma. Teren. in An. Et quia

what she was.

Ita uisus est dudum. So he semid erewhile.

Varia ueste exornatus fuit. He was trymmed in gay apparel. Varia.i.
multiformi et uersicolori.

Nunc tibi uidetur foedus. Now you think him foul and yllfauored. Foedum is
that that is foul ylle favored and lothesome to look on.

Tace obsecro. Hold thy piece, I pray the.

Quasi uero paulum intersiet, for intersit. per epenthesin, As who says,
there was but small difference between them.

Ad nos deductus hodie est adolesce^tulus, quem tu uero uidere uelles. There
was brought home to our house to day, such a young man, as a man would be
glad to se, or such a young man as a man would be the better in his heart to
look on.

Hic est uetus uietus, ueternosus senex, color mustelino. This is an old
rustye thief and rotten and weak, unlusty, and all to ryueled, and as tawny
colored as a weesle. Vetus, is old. Plaut. in Amphitr. Veterem and antiquam
rem, nouam ad uos proferam. I wolle show you an old and antyke thing,
burnyshed and made new again, or, I woll show you a new thing made of an
old and antique thing. And sometime (as witnesses Donatus in this place of
Terence) Vetus is referred to rebukyng and reprouyng. Terentius in prologo
Andr. Qui maleuoli ueteris poetae male dictis respondeat, How he may make
answer to the railyng of that old rusty poet his adversary. Id. in prologo.
Heautont. Tum quod maleuolus uetus poeta dictitat repent ad studium hunc se
applicasse musicum.i. musarum, hoc est bonarum literarum. And further where as
the enuyous old naughty poet allegeth, that this man suddenly applied him
self to the study of good letters. Id. in prologo Phorm. Postquam poeta
uetus, poetam non potest retrahere a studio. etc. Now that the naughty rotten
poet can not withdraw this poet Terence from study. Vietus, Donate
expounds, mollis, flaccidus que, and flexibilis corpore. weke, and lanke, and
limer of body. Vietus, (says Fest. Pomp.) dicitur languidus, sine ui, and
naturalibus priuatus uiribus. weke or weryshe, without any strenghte, or
lacking natural strength. Cicero de senectu. Sed tamen necesse fuit esse
aliquod extremum. and tanquam in arborum baccis, terraeque frugibus maturitate
tempestiua, quasi uietum and caducum, quod ferendum est molliter sapiente, But
yet hit could not be chosen but that there should be sum term and end, and
(like as in the beries of trees, and in the corn growyng on the ground, at
what time they be through ripe) as who should say faintness, wekenesse, and
faylynge in strength, and to be ready to fall and to decay in strength, as
being mortal, which a wise man ought to take patiently, gently, and mekely. So
that uietum is weke, lymer, faynte, and without any strength: and thereof
comes uimina and uimenta oseours, that is the lymer twigs of wyllowes that
the tumelours or coupers ocupy about their houpes, with which they bind their
tubbes or batelles. And uiere is to bind tubbes or barrels or other vessels
with such twigs. And uietor is the coupar that does make and bind them.
Veternosus proprely is he, that has the disese which is called in latin
ueternus, in greek letargus, of Auicen and physicians subetum, or subetium,
that is a disease engendrynge in the hodye contynuall desire of slepynge by
reason of to much rest, idelnes, and ease, otherwise called slaggardy or
slouthfulnes. Which disease because hit is most commenly in aged persons,
therefore ueternosus is much taken and used for him that is diseased with
slepynes, or (as they say drowsynes) so that he has no lust to bestere him,
but rather to sit still as oppressed with contynuall slumbryng and sleep. For
ueternus is taken for unmeasurable and over much sleep, Plin. l. 8. not. hist.
spekyng of bears. Primis diebus bis septenis tam graui somno ursi premu^tur,
ut ne vulueribus quidem excitare queant, tunc mirum in modu^ in ueterno
pinguescunt. The first fourtene days bears be oppressed with so heavy sleep
that they can not be wakened and made to arise though a man wound them right
sore. And And than they wax wonderful fat in that long and contynuall sleep.
Cato takes ueternosum for one having the ydropsy where he says: Veternosus
quam plurimum bibit, tam maxim sitit. He that has the ydropsye the more he
drynketh, the more thyrstie he is. Senex (in this place of Terence) is taken
for rugosus, wythered and ryueled.

Quae haec est fabula? What a tale, or saying have we here?

Eo me redigis ut quid aegeri^ egomet nescia^. Thou bryngest me to that point,
that I can not tell mine own self what I have done.

Venistin' hodie ad nos? Came thou to our house to day?

At ille alter uenit annos natu sedecim. But that other came being of the age
of syxetene years.

Age dum hoc mihi expedi. Come of, tell me this at ones.

Istam, quam habes, unde habes uestem? This garment that thou haste on the,
where gottest thou it? or, how came thou by it?

Quam dudum? How long agone? or, how little while a gone?

Qui cum? With whom?

Noras ne eum prius? Dyddest thou know him before.

Vnde igitur fratre^ meu^ esse sciebas. whereby the^ knew thou, that he was
mi brodre?

Is dedit hanc uestem mihi. He gave me this rote.

Vna ambo abierunt for as. They went forth of doors both to gether.

Iam satis credis me nihil mentitu^? Do you now believe well, that I made no lie?

Certum est uirginem uitiata^ esse. Certain it is and well known, that the
maiden was defloured.

Credis huic quod dicat? Do thou believe such a one as this is, what he
says?

Quid isti credam? What should I believe this peuishe fellow? For iste most
times betokens and imports a certain contempt.

Res ipsa indicat. The matier self shows plainly.

Concede isthuc paululum. Go a littetl haiway. but here, come a little nere
hitehr to me. For isthuc here, is taken for huc.

Dic dum doc rursum* Tell me this yet ones again.

Iuppiter magne, o scelestum, atque audacem hominem. Good lord, oh what a
naughty or ungracious and bold fellow is that?

Vae mihi, etiam nunc non credis indignis nos esse irrisos modis. Alas, do
thou not yet believe, that we have been deluded and scorned shamfully and in
ungoodly manner?

Mirum ni tu credas, quod iste dicat. It is marvel, but you do believe that
this peuishe fellow says.

Possum ne hodie ex te exculpere uerum? Shall I not be able to get out the
treuthe of the er I go? Exculpere proprely is to grave out, or to kerue, as a
man kerueth an image, and per metaphoram it is taken sometimes pro extorquere
to extorte or to get out by violence or punyshement, the truethe, or any
thing that a man desires to know. Plaut. Quot illi bla^ditias, quid promisi
boni, quot admoui fabricas, et quot fallacias in quaestione? uix exculpsi ut
diceret. with what fair words did I handle him, what fair and goodly
promises did I make unto him, how many wyly imaginations, and how many
subtilties, wherewith to begile him, did I set and lay unto him, in examinynge
him? and yet I could uneath get out of him to tell it, for any thing I could do
unto him. Fabrica proprely is a forge or frame of a carpenter, or other work
man, of fabricor, aris, to frame, and per metaphoram to inue^t, or to imagine,
and thereof fabricas here is taken for fallatias, and those two words signify
one thing, and the conjunction copulative and, coming between them, is taken
for id est.

Non potest fine malo fateri, uideo. He can not be made to confess it without
punysshement, I see well.

Sequere hac. Com after me this way here.

Modo ait, modo negat. Some while he says ye, and somewhyle nay.

Iintro. Go thy ways in.

Honest quomodo hinc abeam nescio. I can not tell how to get or how to
depart hens with mine honesty.

Tu me hic nebulo ludificabere? Shall thou mock me here thou knave? or, thou
villeyn shalt thou scorn and make a lawghynge stock of me here?

Parmenonis tam scio esse hanc techna^, quam me uiuere. I do as well know that
this is the crafty imagination of Parmeno, as I know that I am alive. Techna
is a greek word and proprely signifies any craft, and by translation it is
taken for crafty and subtle imaginations. Plau. in cap. Ego erumniatus, de
artuatus sum miser scelesti hominis technis qui me, ut lubitum est, ductauit
dolis, I unfortunate body am brought in misery, and in manner pulled in pieces
through the craftines and suttiltie of this naughty fellow, which has led me
with his traynes and guyles even as lifted. Deartuare, proprely is to cut or
to hew in pieces one part or membre from an other, as traitors be,
deartuati when they be quartred.

Inueniam hodie parem ubi referam gratia^ s. illi. I woll er I sleep find some
thing wherein to requyte him.

Quid nunc faciendum censes? what thynk you best to be done now?

Vtrum taceam, an ue praedicem? whether should I keep it secret, or else utter
it?

Tu pol si sapis, quod scis, nescis. Verily if you wise be, that that you
know, you woll not know.

Hac re et omni te turba euolues, et illi gratum feceris. By this thing doing
thou shall both rydde, wind, and quyte thy self out of all trouble, and also
do him great pleasure.

Id modo dic, abisse Dorum, for abijsse per syncopen. Say thou nothing but
this, that Dorus is gone his way hens.

Cum inde abeo, iam tum inceperat turba inter eos. when I came my ways from
thens, they had already begone to be at strife and to make busynes, or to quarrel
together. Aufer aurum hoc. Have away this gold. Ego scibo ex hoc quid siet. I
woll know of this man here what the matter is.

In the fyfte scene.
Data uerba mihi sunt. I am deceived.

Vicit uinum quod bibi. The wine that I have drunken, has ouercomed me, as who
should say I feel my self drunken.

Dum accubabam, uidebar mihi pulchre so brius. All the while that I sat at the
table, me thought I was very sobre, and in very good tempre. Pulchre.i. ualde,
oppido, minus, admodum.

Postquam surrexi, neque pes, neque men's satis suum officium facit. Now that I
am up, neither my feet, nor my wit seruethe me very well.

Vah quanto nunc formosior uidere mihi, quam dudum. Oh how much fayrer seem
you to me now, than you did while ere.

Verbum hercle hoc uerum est. Certes this is a true saying.

An abijt iam? Is he gone al ready?

Lites sunt inter eos maxim. There is very great strife and debate between
them, or they be at great words to gether. For that signifies proprely lites,
stryuing in words.

Abiens mihi innuit. when he went away, he becked on me.

Nonne id sat erat? was not that enough?

Nesciebam id dicere illam. I knew not, that she meaned that by her saying, or
by her words that she spoke.

Intellexi minus. I understood it not.

Me extrusit for as. He thrust me out of the doors.

Miror ubi ego huic anteuerterim. I marvel in what place I got before this
man, that I saw him not.

In the syxte scene.
Credo illum iam affuturum esse. I think verily, that he wolbe here anon.

Si illam digito attigerit uno, oculi illico of fodientur. If he touch her
with one finger, his eyes shall be pulled out of his heed immediately. Vno digito
a proverb red in chil. Erasmi.

Ego illius far possum ineptias, et magni fica uerba, uerba dum sint. I can
very well bear and suffer his foolyshenes, and his royal high words, as long
as they he nothing but words.

Verum enim si ad rem conferentur, uapulabit. But in feythe if they turue to
deeds, he shall abie, or, he shall smarte. Conferentur.i. transferentur,
conuertentur. s. uerba: And it is a propre and elegante manner of speaking,
says Donat: as if a man should say in latin. He began to do as he said, he may
say it proprely in latyne thus. Verba ad rem contulit.i. id quod uerbis
dixerat, re facere aggressus est.

Iamdudum adsum. I am here and have been a good while. Dudum and iamdudum
betoken and be spoken of shorter time as of one or two, or three, or four
hours. Nuper pridem, and Iampridem of somewhat longer time, as of six months
or years, or more or less as the matter is. But dudu^ and iamdudu^, pridem and
iampridem, have this difference, that dudum, nuper, and pridem, do signify the
act of the verb to be ended and paste, and therefore they be most commonly and
most elegantly joined with verbes of pretertenses, as, he went away a good
while sens, Dudum abiit. He wrote unto me long agone of that matter, Ea de re
pridem ad me scrip sit. He was in the city of late days, Fuit nu per in urbe.
Iamdudum, and iampridem, signify the act of a verb, remaining and
continuing still, and therefore most usually they be joined with verbes of the
present tence thus. The master is in the schole, and has been a pretty while,
Iamdudum praeceptor est in schola. He is a very greatte friend of mine and has been many a day, Iampridem est mihi amicissimus. Yet both these are much and
often times read ioynid with verbes of pretertenses, and contrary wise that
others with verbes of the present tense, as shewith Laur. Vall. l. 2. eleg.
cap. 34.

O mi Chreme teipsum expectabam. O good sweet Chremes I looked for you, and no
man else.

Scin' tu turbam hanc propter te esse factam? Do thou know or consider that
all this busynes or trouble has been for the?

Scin ad te attinere ha^c omnem rem? Do thou remember that all this matter
perteyneth to the? or lies the upon?

Dum tibi sororem studio reddere, et restituere. while I go about and labor to
rendze, and to restore unto you, your sister.

Haec atque huiusmo^i multa passus sum. I have suffered these things, and many
other like, or have had this and much other like trouble. Est domi apd me. He
is at home at mi house.

Hoc tibi dono do. I give this unto you freely. Dono dare, to give freely. For
diverse verbes be construed and elegantly joined with a double datise, of which
one is set in manner aduerbially and much like an aduerbe, and yet is none. As
thou hopest to have laud and preise for the same thing, which thou layst to me
as a fant, Speras id tibi laudi fore, quod mihi uitio uertis. Take thou no care
nor thought for the matter. Ne sit tibi curae. He has laid his cote to pledge
to me, Dedit mihi uestem pignori. He has put money in my hands in the way of
usury, Dedit mihi pecunia^ foenori. He has lent me a grote, Dedit mihi mutuo
drachmam. He has lent me a gown, Dedit mihi uestem commoda to. And so here in
Terence Hanc tibi dono do, for hanc I put hoc, because it is more in use, and
more convenient to be spoken. For which cause I change almost every where in
this book the feminine gendre into the masculine or neutre, as the place best
admits.

Cave ne prius quam hoc a me accipias, amittas. Beware that you lose it not,
before you receive or have it of me.

Cistellam domo effer cum monumentis, Bring forth the little fosser with the
tokens. Monumenta here in this place (after Donat) is taken for that that the
greeks call and of which vocables, the former signifies the tokens, by which
any thing is brought to mind and knowledge, as cognisances, arms, and such
other things, and ar crepundia puerilia, for is the swathynge bond and also
the clothe or bed, in which sucking children ar wrapped and swathed.

Number formidolosus obsecro es? Art thou fearful or false hearted, or does thy
heart faylle the man I pray the? for (as Donate says) formidolosus is the same
that terribilis, metuendus, to be feared, and also timidus, fearful, or false
hearted.

Egon' formidolosus? Nemo est omnium qui metuat minus. I falseharted? there is
no man alive that fears less.

Ita opus est. So is it need.

Haud metuo. I am not aferde.

Quem tu me hominem existimas? i. quale^. what manner fellow thynkest thou that I
am?

Cogitato qui cum res tibi est. Remember, with whom thou have to do. Qui cum.i.
cum quo: For some prepositions at sometime contrary to the nature of
prepositions set after their case: as mecum, tecum, secum, nobiscum, uobiscum,
and not cum me, te, se, nobis, uobis. The matier, where of we co^mened
yesterday, came nothing to pass, as I would hane had it, Res qua de sumus heri
collocuti, minime mihi cessit ex sententia. So ore tenus, Italiam uersus, ad
orientem usque, qua propter. etc. But yet where as quis has the ablative both
quo and qui* Yet I remember not that I have red quo cum, or qua cu^, but cum
quo and cum qua, nor contrary wise cum qui, but qui cum, in all gendres. Cic.
Ser. Sulpit. li. 4. epist. fam. Nemo est omnium qui cum potius mihi, quam tecum
co^municandum putem, There is no man alive with whom I think I ought rather
to commen of mine affairs than with you. And also plurally we say,
indifferentely cum quibus, and quibuscum, quis cum, or, cum quis.

Peregrinus est, minus potens quam tu minus notus, amicorum hic habens minus.
He is a stranger here, and less may he do than you, he is less acqueynted,
and has fewer friends in these parties.

Scio isthuc. That I know.

Quod cauere possis, stultum est admittere. It is a folysshenes to suffer that
ill to be done that a man may avoid. Admittere.i. sieri sinere says Donate,
and cauere here I take for propellere et declinare.

Malo ego nos prospicere, quam hunc ulcisci accepta iniuria. I have lever, or I
had rather that we provide for the matter before, than to be revenged on him
after that we have had a shrewde turn. Prospicere here is taken for
prouidere, to se before, and so to provide a remedy against ill that might else
chance, Cic. de Sen. Multa, cum remissi ac liberi sunt, futura prospiciunt,
when they be at quiet and at liberty they forsee many things that shall
follow and come after. Idem Ser. Sulp. Tanquam ex aliqua specula prospexi
tempestatem futuram, I foresawe the tempest that should follow, as though I had been in sum high place to see every way round. For that signifies proprely
specula, whether it be an hill, or a tower, or a rock, or a stone, or a tree,
or else what being of such height that one may se far every way round about.

Tu abi, atque ostium obsera intus. Go thou, and lock the door in the infyde.

Ego hinc transcurro ad forum. I wol ro^ne or, make a sterte from hens into
the street, how forum is to be taken in such manner speakynges as this, hit
is showed before in Andria.

Si uim faciet, in ius ducito hominem. If he shall offer to do any thing by
force, violence, or strength of hand, commence a fair actio^ against him, and
goo to the law with him.

Fac animo haec presenti dicas. See that thou speak this with a bold spirit,
as who should say, that thy wits be thyn own, and that thy heart fayse the
not, when thou shuidest speak this.

Attolle pallium. Take up your cloak about you. Pallium here is taken pro
chlamyde.

Huic ipsi est opus patrono, quem ego defensorem paro. He whom I goo about to
make mine advocate or man of law, or defender of my cause, him self has need
of one to defend him. Patronus is he that defendethe any manne, being in
trouble or peril.

In the seventh Scene.
Hanc cine ego contumeliam in me accipiam? Should I suffer such a notable or
shameful despite to be done unto me? hic, haec insignis, et hoc insigne, is
that that is very notable, whether it be in good or in ill, as we say insignis
uirtute, and insignis flagitiis.

Mori me satius est. I were better be deed.

Male mulctabo ipsum. I wolle punysshe him sharply. Mulctabo.i. puniam here,
for mulctare proprely is to punysshe by the pourse.

Imperatoris uirtutem noueram, et uim mi litum. I knew the manlynes or
valiantnes of the captain, and the strength or powar of his soldiers.

Noueram sine sanguine hoc fieri no^ posse. I knew well that this could never
be done, or ended without blood sheddynge.

Solus Samnio seruat domum. No man but Samnio keeps the house.

Omnibus signum dabo. I woll give unto all the company a token of knowledge
when they shall begin.

Illuc est sapere. That is one point of wisdom.

Vt hosce instruxit, ipse sibi cauit, loco. After that he had set the others
in array, he provided for him self to stand in a sure place out of all peril
and danger.

Idem hoc Pyrrhus factitauit. The very self same thing used Pyrrhus to do.

Viden' tu, quam hic rem agit? Do thou see where about this fellow goes.

Nimirum consilium illud rectum est, de occludendis aedibus. Certes that is
right good connsayle that I gave you to shut in your doors against him.

Hic nebulo magnus est. This is a greatte lubbarde knave.

Ne metuas. Be not aferde.

Omnia prius experiri, quam armis, sapien tem decet. It is the part of a wise
man to prove and assay all other ways, er he fight for any thing.

Qui scis, an, quae iubcam, sine vi faciat? What know thou whether he woll
do that ^^ others in despite and for a great contumely or check, as now in
this time the Turks call us christen men dogs, and one enemy in time of
war calls any soldier of the contrary part, dog in despyte. And it is
taken of the Greeks for those, whom the latin men call impudentes, shameless
fellows the greeks call proprely.i. canino aspectu homines, men lokynge like
dogs.

Quis tu homo es? what man art thou? or who art thou?

Edico tibi ne uim facias ullam in illum. I charge the that thou lay no violent
hands on him.

Tu me prohibeas, meum ne tanga^? Shall thou let me to lay hand on that,
that is mine own?

Hic furti se alligat. He charge him self of felony, or trespass. Alligat se
furti (says Donatus).i. rerum se efficit fraudis. Alligat.i. astringit,
illaqueat, and obnoxiu^ facit. entangleth and brings him self in danger of
impechement. And furtum is not only theste, or robbery, but also all manner
felony, or other injury, fraud, deceyte, guile, or any trespass what so ever
it be, that is in latyne, omne maleficium generaliter.

Satis hoc tibi est. That is enough for the.

Idem hoc tu ais? Sayeste thou even the same to?

Quaere qui respondeat. Look whom thou wolte to answer the, for I woll not.

Quid nunc agimus? what shall we do now?

Iam tibi aderit supplicans ultro. He woll come to you anon of his own mind,
and desire you to be good unto him.

Noui ingenium mulierum, nolunt ubi uelis, ubi nolis cupiunt ultro. I know the
nature and guyse of women, they wol not, wha^ a man would have them, and when
he would not, than are they desirous and feign with all their hearts and of
their own minds.

Domi focique fac ut memineris. Se that thou remember to go home, and to make a
good fire.

Iamdudum est animus in patinis. My mind is on my dynar, and has been a good
while.

Vos me hac sequimini. Comme you after me this way.

Out of the fyfthe Act in the first Scene.
Pergin' sceleste mecum perplexe loqui? Do thou stylle yet speak ambiguously
and doubtfully unto me thou naughty pack?

Non tu isthuc mihi dicturus aperte es, quie quid est? wolte thou not tell it
me plainly, what so ever hit is?

Quid factum est? what has been done?

Quid tibi ego dica^? What should I tell the?

Quis fuit igitur? who was it than?

Quid ais uenificia? what say thou poysonfull queen.

Certo comperi. I have certain knowledge of hit.

Occidi, si quidem tu isthaec uera praedicas. I am undone, at leest wise if
that be true that thou say.

Number id lachrumat uirgo? Does the maiden weep for that?

Quid ais sacrilega? Whatte say thou naughty fylthe?

Is^huccine interminata sum hinc abiens tibi? Did not I charge the upon a
greatte pain, when I went hens, that this should not be done?

Quid facerem? what should I have done? a propre and elegant locution or manner
of spekyng, and much in use among authors noted in Hadrian de sermon. lat.

Ita ut iuss isti soli credita est. She was left with him alone according to
your co^ma^dme^t.

Oue^ lupo co^misisti. Thou myghtest as well have committed and left a sheep
with a wolf to keep: Ouem lupo co^mittere is a proverb used when so ever we
wol signify any thing committed unto him, for whose cause and for fear of
whom it had rather been expedient and requisyte to have had an other keeper.
Erasmus in chil.

Dispudet sic mihi data esse uerba. I am ill asshamed, that I should be in
such wise beguyled.

Quid hominis illic est? What fellow is that?

Habemus hominem ipsum. We have espied or found very him that did the deed.

Quid illi faciemus? what shall we do unto him? Hadrian has noted that authors
use elegantly this manner of speaking by the dative case, as well by facio, as
also by fio passiuely thus. Quid illi faciemus? Quid illi fiet? for that which
some folyshe fellows (says he) speak thus: Quid de illo faciemus? Quid de
illo fiet? Cicero. in Academ. quaestionibus. Quid enim faceret huic
conclusioni? what should he do to such a conclusion as this is? etc. Idem in
Rullum. Quaero, si, qui uolunt uendere, non fuerint, quid pecuniae fiet? I
demand this question, if there shall be none that wolle selle hit, what shall
be done with the money? And Cato de re rust. uses the same manner speaking
without interrogation. Si ante non deportauerit dominus, uino quod uolet
faciet, If the owner do not carry it down before, he shall do with the wine
what he woll. This notwithstanding I have noted diverse places in Terence,
where he uses to speak by the same verbes joined with the ablative case
without a preposition, as much as with the datife. Teren. in Heauton act. 1.
sc. 2. Quia enim incertum est etiam quid se faciat, For because that in deed he
is yet uncertain or in doubt what to do with him self. Ibid. act. 2. sc. 3.
Cedo, quid hic faciet sua? s. amica, why tell me what shall Clitipho here do
with his own lover? Id. in An. act. 3. sc. 5. Pamph. Nec quidem quid me faciam
scio. Da. Nec equidem me, Pamhil. and in good sothe I can not tell what I may
do with my self. Da. Nor I with my self. Ibid. Quid me fiet? what shall become
of me? I omytte that many of thexemples that Hadrian cites and allegeth, may
be taken as well in the ablative as in the datyue, that no child fear nor
doubt to speak by both cases. Al be it Plautus in Casina (at leest wise if the
book be not corrupt but have the true letter, as all exemplaries do consent
and agree) speaks in the same sense by the accusatyue. Quid agis tu marite?
mi uir? unde ornatu hoc aduenis? quid fecisti scipionem? aut quod habuisti
pallium? what do you husbonde? my good sweet husbond? from whe^s come you thus
arayde? what have you done with your staff? or what clooke have you had? Quid
fecisti scipionem for de scipione, or (more elegantly to speak) scipioni in
the dative, or scipione in the ablative. Scipio, onis, is latyne for a staff:
And thereof the family of the noble Scipiones, in Rome had their first name.
For one Cornelius did lead about his father being blind, and stood him instead both of his eyes and also of a staff, for which thing men took up and
used to call him Scipio, and so all his familye after him were semblablye
named Scipiones of the cognominacion of the said Cornelius.

Vide amabo, si non, cum aspicias, os impudens uidetur. See I pray you, if when
a body looks on him, he seem not to have an impudent look, or a shameless face.

Quae eius confidentia est? How bold he is? or, how great impudency or
lack of shame is in him? Confido, dis, confisus sum, confidere, coufisum, su,
to have sure hope, trust, and confidence, as now we say also in english,
and it is referred as well to the present time, as to the time paste and also
to the time to comme (as witnesses Donat and Valla) And confido, dis, is used
in bonam partem, and yet confidens, and confidentia coming of the same verb
is observed by custom and use of speaking, to be almost ever taken and used
in malo, and very seldom in the good. And fiducia is in manner evermore taken
in the better part.

In the second Scene.
Vterque, pater et matter, quasi dedita opera domi erant, ut nullo modo introire
possem quin uiderent me. Both my father and my mother were at home, as if it
had been appointed for the nones, so that I might by no means get in, but
that they must needs have seen me.

Dum ante ostium sto. while I was sta^dyng before the door.

Notus mihi quidam obuia^ uenit. One of mine acqueyntance came and met me.

Ego me in pedes, quantu^ queo, conijcio. I took me to my fete as fast as I
might rounne.

Miserrimus fui fugitando, ne quis me cognosceret. I was very ill troubled,
encombred, and vexed in runnyng out of the way, that no man might know me. And
it is (as Donatus notes) a very elegant manner of speaking to say miser
fugitando, as miser amando. Albe it he does interpret miserrimus here, lassus,
fatigatus, et languidus, weary, tyered, and faynte, and cognosceret is taken
for agnosceret.

Quid faciam? quid mea autem? quid faciet mihi? what shall I do? tushe what
recke I? what woll he do to me?

Bone uir Door salve. Honest manne Door, god you save.

Satin' id tibi placet? Do thou like hit very well.

Credin' te impune abiturum? Do thou think to escape unpunished?

Vnam hanc noxiam mitte, si aliam unqua^ ullam admisero, occidito. Pardon me
this one offense or trespass, and if I shall ever do so any more slay me: Noxiam
(says Donate) pro noxa, trissyllabum pro dissyllabo. All be it Valla denies
this word noxa to be latyne, saying (as I take him) that hit is no where
founden, contrary to other grammariens. And in deed Calepinus redeth here noxam
and not noxiam. Al be it noxia is in diverse places in Plaut,

Number meam seuitiam ueritus es? Dyddest thou fear that I would be to sharp or
cruel on the?

Hunc metui ne me criminaretur tibi? I was aferde of this man leese that he
would accus^ me unto you. Criminari aliquem is to accus^ a man, and to lay any
thing to his cha^^^ in open court, and crimen is not only the offense or
trespass self, but also the crimination, or accusation for the same. Valla. li
4. elegan. cap. 58.

Vix me contineo, quin inuolem in capillu^, I can uneath refreyne my self from
fleinge upon him to pulle him by the hear.

Etiam ultro derisum aduenit. Besides all the other things, he comes for the
nones (...) laugh us to scorn.

Abi hinc insane. Get the hens mad fellow.

Quid ita uero abeam? why should I get me hens I pray you?

Credin' isti quicquam furcifero? Do you give any credence to this galowclapper?

Missa haec faciamus. Let we pass all this.

Non te dignum Cherea fecisti. Cherea you have done otherwise than becomed you.

Si ego dignus hac co^tumelia sum maxim, at tu indignus qui faceres tamen.
Although I be never so well worthy to be thus spytefully

Quid nunc co^silij capiam, nescio. I can not tell what counsel or way now to
take.

Conturbasti mihi rations omnes. Thou haste troubled all my reckenynges and
accomptes, or, thou haste troubled and dyspoynted all that ever I rekened upon.
Conturbare rations, is the same that we say in english to bring one out of
his recknyng that is to say to bring him out of his purpose and to dispoynt
it.

Dehinc spero aeternam inter nos gratiam fore. From hens forth I trust there
