shall be perfyte love between us for ever, or that we shall be friends and
lovers for ever.

Ex huiusmodi re quapiam. Of or by some semblable thing as this.

Saepe ex malo principio magna familiaritas conflata est.i. contracta,
comparata, consiliata. Often times of a lewd beginning has grown greatte
famylyarytie and frendeshyppe.

Quid si hoc aliquis uoluit deus? what if this were the will of god, or of
sum good sayncte?

Equidem in eam partem accipio, et uolo. Truly that way do I take hit, and
would right gladly that it might so be.

Vnum hoc scito. Know and be sure of this one thing.

Scito contumeliae no^ me fecisse caussa, sed amoris. You shall wit, or
understand that I did it not for any despyte or villanye toward you, but for
love.

Ignosco tibi. I forgive or pardon the.

Non adeo inhumano ingenio sum. I am not of so ungentle a nature or facion.

Tibi ab isto hera cauendum intelligo. I well perceive dame that you had
need beware of this fellow.

Nihil tibi quidquam credo. I trust nor believe the nere a whytte.

Ego me tuae commendo, et co^mitto fidei. I commytte and put me hoolely in your
honesty, goodness, or grace.

Ego te oro in hac re mihi adiutrix sis. I beseech you to be my helper in this
matter. Adiutrix is a nowne verbal of the feminine ge^dre, and therefore referred
to the feminine sex: and adiutor is of the masculine, and referred to the manne.

Te mihi patronum cupio. I would feign have you to defend and to help me.

Emoriar, si no^ hanc uxore^ duxero. I pray god I dye but I woll mary her.

Volet, certo scio. He wolle be glad, I know very well.

Iam frater hic aderit uirginis. The brother of the maiden woll be here by and
by.

Domi operiamur potius quam hic ante hostium. Let us tarry for him within the
house rather than here before the door.

Hunc tu in aedeis cogitas recipere posthac? Do you mind to let this fellow
ever com within your house again after this?

Creed hoc meae fidei. Believe this at my word. As who should say, trust me in
this, for I promise you faythefully and lie not, nor I speak not but as I
think and as my fidelity and faith that I owe toward you byndeth me.

Dabit hic aliquam pugnam denuo. This fellow wolle cause sum debate or fraye
again.

Parum perspexisse eius uidere audaciam. You seem not to have well marked nor
considered the boldenes of him.

Neque seruandu^ tibi quidquam dare ausim, neque te seruare. I dare neither
commyt any thing to the to keep, nor yet to take the charge to see well to the.
For seruare here is put, for diligenter obseruare.

Obsecro, abeamus intro. I pray you let us goo in.

Nolo me in uia cu^ hac ueste uideat. I would not that he should see me in the
street with this garment.

I prae, sequor. Go before, and I will com aftir.

Tu isthic mane, ut Chremem introducas. Tarry you there, to bring Chremes into
the house.

In the third Scene.
Quid uenire in mentem nunc possit mihi, qui referam illi gratiam? what might
I now deuyse wherewith to requyte him? qui pro quo.

Move te ocyus. Bystere the apace.

Moueo. I stere.

Video, sed nihil promoues. I see the move or stere, but thou make no way,
Video. s. te mouere, sed. etc.

Been narras. Ye say well.

Illi faueo uirgini. I favor, or love that maiden well, or I bear good mind
and favor to that maiden.

Iamdudum hera uos expectat domi. My dame looks for you at home and has done
a good while.

Parmenonem incedere uideo, uide ut ocio sus sit. I see Parmeno come iettynge
like a lord, but see how idle he is, as one out of all care and thought. v
t. i. quom odo, uel qualiter. Incedo, dis, inessi, dear, incessum, incessu, is
ambulare to walk, that is to go. But proprely incedere differs from
ambulare. For incedere proprely is to go with astately pace, as who should
say, to show a greatte grauytee or majesty in going, as princes do when
they show them selfs in their astate. Seneca. Tenero ac molli passu
suspendimus gradum, nec ambulamus, sed incedimns, we stay and prolonge our
goinge with a nyce or tender and soft, delicate, or gingerly pace, and do not
go as others do, but iette or go like great estates. Of incedo comes a verbal
incessus us, ui, which is used for any manner going, but most proprely it is
the pace, that great princes and noble men use, when they show their estates
or majesty. Ver. Et uera incessu patuit dea, And she showed her self by her
pace, to be a goddess. Valla. li. 5. c. 79. ociosus.i. securus, taking no
thought nor care for nothing.

Si dijs placet. In the name of god. It is a proverb never used in latin
speaking but ironice, and in indignation. Read examples in Hadrian de ser. lat.
There is an other proverb, Si deus uoluerit, if it shall please god, used in
serious matters of any thing to com, the end of which depends or hangethe
in the favor of god. And it is taken out of thepistle of saint James, and
recited in chil. E.

Spaero me habere, qui hunc meo excruciem modo. I think I have found a way
to vex him, and to anger every veyn in his heart, even as I woll my self.
Habere.i. inuenisse, or scire. meo modo.i. ut uolo.

Hunc perterrebo sacrilegum. I woll make this wicked fellow thoroughly aferde.
Sacri legum.i. impium, nefarium, scelerosum. Al be it sacrilegus proprely is he
that steals hallowed things, and sacrilegium stealyng of hallowed things.
Valla li. 6. eleg. c. 40.

In the fourth Scene.
Reuiso quidnam hic rerum gerat. I com again to see what he is doing here.

Astu rem tractauit. He has handeled the matter wylely. Astu.i. astute, and it
is here an aduerbe, but some time it is the ablatife of the nowne astus, tus,
tui. For it follows in the same comedy, An in astu uenit.

Dij uestram fidem. O good lord, it stands always in the place of an
interiection of meruaylynge, and not of calling on, admirantis non inuocantis.

Confeci sine molestia, sine sumptu, sine dispe^dio. I brought it to an end
without any troublous busynes, without any charge or expenses, and without any
loss or damage.

Id mihi puto palmarium.i. palma dignum. For that thing I think my self
worthy to be crowned. Palma, mae, in latin, and in greek is a tree, that was
wont to be given to such as had won any victory. For because that it is
always grene, and (as Plutarchus says) of that propretie and nature, that
there can no weight nor burden oppress it, but that it woll arise under it,
and stand up as it should do, Read chil. Eras. in the proverb, Palmam far.
Mihi is here datiuus. Festiuitatis.i. iucunditatis gratia adiunctus, of which
read in libello de octo partium constructione.

Cum cognorit, perpetuo oderit. After that he knows hit, he woll hate it ever
as long as he lives.

Foeminis, dum foris sunt, nihil uidetur mu^dius, nec magis compositum
quidquam, nec magis elega^s. when women be abroad out of their own howses,
nothing in the world seems to be more clenly than they be, nor nothing more
demure, nor more propre or feate.

Cum coenant, liguriunt. When they eat, they feed nycely or deyntely, and not
but of the best. For ligurire, is furmed (as says Donate) of the greek word,
which is in english sweet. Ligurire somtymes is auide et helluose, that is
gredily and rauenningly or gluttonously to devour very much.

Harum uidere ingluuiem, sordes, inopia^, quam inhonestae solae sint domi,
atque auidae cibi, quo pacto ex iure hesterno panem atrum uorent, nosse omnia
haec, salus est adolescentulis. But to see the rauenynge or cogellyng, the
sluttyshnes, and the penury of them, and how uncleanly and greedy on their
meat they be, when they are alone by them selfs at home in their own houses,
and how they woll slaber or sosse up brown bread with gruel of the leuyng of
the other days before, all these things to know is the preservation of
youth to be ware of them. Hesternus, na, number, is not always taken for the
day before this day, but is mock used for pridianus, na, nu^, that is for
the day before any other day. Cice. Videre uideor alios intrantes, alios
exeuntes, quos dam ex uino uacillantes, quosdam hesterna e potatione
oscitantes, Me thinks I see some goinge in, some goinge forth, some roylynge
by reason of to much drinking of wine, and some yeanynge and gapyng of the
revel that they kept the day before. And semblably cras and crastinus is abused
and spoken of the day after any other day. Verg. in georg.

Si uero ad solemn rapidu^, lunasque sequentes, Ordine respicies, nunquam te
crastina fallet Hora, nec insidijs noctis capiere serenae.

Ego te pro istis dictis et factis ulciscar, ut ne impune in nos illuseris. I
woll revenge or requite the for those thy sayings and doings, that thou shall
not laugh us to scorn and escape free thy self.

In the fyfthe Scene.
Proli facinus foedum. Oh a shameful and abhomynable deed.

O infelicem adolescentulum. Oh unfortunate young man.

O scelestum Parmenonem. O ungracious fellow Parmeno.

Quae futura esse exempla dicunt in eum in digna. How sore and how grievously
they say, that he shall be punysshed to thensample of al others? Exempla
aedere, or, exempla facere in aliquem is to punyshe any body openly to
thexample of others. And exempla edi, or exempla fieri in aliquem is to be
punysshed openly, to thensample of others, as if one be set upon the pyllory,
eduntur, or fiunt in eum exempla. So here in Terence anon after these words,
Quae futura esse exempla dicunt in eum indigna, Parmeno says unto Pythias: In
quem exempla fienty? who is that that shall be punysshed to thensample of all
others?

O Iuppiter, quae illic turba est? Oh Iuppyter what greatte trouble, or what a
do is there?

Perdidisti istum adolescentulu^. Thou haste utterly undone and cast away this
young man.

Studes dare uerba nobis. Thou go about to deceive us.

Scis eum hinc ciuem esse. Thou knoweste that he is of this city here.

Scis frarrem eius adprime nobilem. s. esse. Thou knoweste that his brother is
a very great gentle man.

Ille ubi resciuit factum. He when he knew what was done.

Colligauit eum miseris modis. He bound him fast hand and foot, that pity it
was to se.

Atque equide^ orante, ut ne id faceret, Thaide. Ye and that when Thais desired
and prayed him that he would not so do.

Nunc minatur facturum se id, quod ego nunquam uidi fieri, neque uelim. s.
uidere fieri. Now he manaceth and thretneth to do such a thing unto him, as
I never saw done to any, nor would not see by my good will.

Qua audacia tantum facinus audet? How darethe he be so bold to do such a
peryllous act?

Ne hoc nesciatis. That you may not sey, that you knew not so much before,
or, that you say not, that you were ignorant of this.

Dico uobis, nostrum esse illum herilem filium. I tell you he is our masters
son.

Ne quam in illum Thais, uim fieri sinat. Let not Thais suffer any violent
hands to be laid on him.

Cur non egomet intro eo? why goo not I in my self?

Vide quid agas, ne illi prosis, et tu pereas. Beware what thou do, and that
thou do not him pleasure, and cast away thine own self.

Putant, quicquid est ex te esse ortu^. They think all that ever has been
done to come first of the.

Quid igitur faciam miser? quid ue incipiam? What shall I than do unfortunate
man? or, what shall I first begin?

Video rure redeuntem senem? I have espied the old manne comme out of the
countreye.

Dicam huic, an non? Shall I tell him the matter, or not?

Dicam hercle, etsi mihi magnu^ malu^ scio paratum. In faith I woll tell him,
and yet I know, that I shall surely have a greatte harm or shrende turn
thereby.

Necesse est huic dicere ut subueniat. It is necessary to show this man of
the matter, to the end that he may do some help in it.

Tu isti narrato omnem rem ordine. Show you all the matter unto this man in
order.

In the syxte Scene.
Ex meo propinquo rure hoc capio commodi, neque agri, neque urbis odium me
unquam percipit, ubi satias coepit fieri, commuto locum. Of my ground that I
have without the city this commodity and pleasure I have, that I am never
through weary neither of being in the country, nor of the cite. For wha^ I
begin to be weary of the one or the other, I change places. Odium.i. fastidium
uel taedium. Satias.i. satietas, hoc est taedium ac fastidium.

Quem praestolare hic ante ostium? whom do thou tarry for here before the
door?

Saluum te aduenire gaudeo. I am glad that you are come home in good health.

Perij, lingua haeret metu. Alas that ever I was borne, my tongue is tied, nor
I can not speak for fear.

Quid est quod trepidas? what is the matter that art so ferde?

Here, primum te arbitrari, quod res est, uelim. Master first and formeste I
would have you think as matter in deed is.

Quicquid huius factum est, culpa non factum est mea. what so ever has been
done here, it was not done by my fault.

Recte sane interrogasti. You did well to ask.

Oportuit rem praenarrasse me. I owght to have showed you the matter first.

Quanti emit? How bowght he it? or, how much paid he for it?

Emit uiginti minis. He bought it for twenty pounds.

Here, ne me spectes. Master look not on me, or, sir, eye not me so.

Aliud ex alio malum. One myschiefe on an others neck.

Me impulsore haec non facit. He does not such things as these by my
instygacion, counsel, or setting on. Impulsore.i. authore, consultore.

Omit de te dicere. Speak no more of thy self.

Isthuc, quicquid est, primum expedi. First tell me this matter quyckely, what
so ever hit is.

Audaciam meretricu^ specta. See the boldness of harlottes or strumpettes.

Nunquid est aliud mali damniue, quod non dixeris? Is there any other myschiefe
or mysfortune besides this, that thou haste not yet told nor spoken of?

Non dubiu^ est, quin magnum mihi ex hac re sit malum. There is no doubt, but
that I shall have sum greatte dyspleasure by this thing.

In the seventh Scene.
Nunquam aedepol quidquam ia^ diu, quod magis uellem euenire, mihi euenit. In
good southe there never happened unto me now a great while any thing, that I
would more gladly should have happened.

Intro ad nos uenit. He came into our house

Mihi rediculo fuit. I laughed well at him.

Quid timeret scieba^. I knew what he fered.

Id prodeo, ut conueniam Parmenonem. I come forth of doors to speak with
Parmeno. Id, hoc est, propter id, hit is eclipsis praepositionis.

Vbi obsecro est? where is he, I pray you? Men'quaerit haec? Does this woman
seek me?

Quid est inepta? quid tibi uis? quid rides? What is the matter folyshe
gyglotte? what meanest thou? whereat laughest thou?

Defessus iam sum, de te ridendo. I am even weary with laughing at the.

Nunquam aedepol stultiorem hominem uidi, nec uidebo. By my truth I never
saw a more foolysshe fellow, nor never shall.

Non possum satis narrare, quos ludos prae bueris intus. I can not well
express, what sport and lawghynge we have had at the within.

Ilicone credere ea, quae dixi, oportuit te? Must thou needs have believed that
that I said by and by even at the first?

An poenitebat flagitij? Dyddest thou take remorse, or repentance of thy great
offense? Flagitum is propreli scelus flagris dignum, on heinous offense worthy
to have sharp correction and punysshemente. Valla. li. 4. eleg. ca. 58.

Quid illi credis animi tum fuisse, ubi ueste^ uidit illam esse eum indutum
pater? Where was his heart trowest thou, when his father saw him in that
apparel? we may say induor ueste, or ueste^: for induo is one of the verbes
that govern a double accusatife after them, and of al such verbes their
passiues require the later accusative of both. As I teache the grammar, Ego
doceo te grammaticen. By the passyue. Thou art taught grammar, Tu doceris
grammaticen.

Itanc lepidum tibi uisum est nos irridere? Dyddest thou think it such a
pretty sport, to mock us?

Siquidem isthuc impune habueris. If thou escape quyte for this same.

Reddam hercle. s. uices. I woll pay the again, or, I woll serve the as well,
or, I wolle requyte the, or, I woll be even with the in faith.

Vterque in te exempla edent. Both they will punyshe the, to thensample of al
others, or that all other shall take ensample by the.

Nullus sum. I am undone, (as who should say) I were as good be deed and out
of this world.

Hic pro illo munere tibi honos est habitus. This honesty, and this promotion
have you gotten by that benefyte or pleasure domge.

Egomet meo iudicio miser, quasi sorex, ho die perij. I am cast away and
betrayed this day by mine own words, like as the rattes by their loud
squeakynge betray them selfs, shemynge where they be. Suo ipsius iudicio
perijt sorex, The ratte betrayed her selle with her own noise, and so was
taken, is a prouerbiall speaking of any body that are betrayed by their own
words. And as Donate thinks, the use of the metaphor, was taken of this,
that the propretie of the the rattes is to make a louder squekyng tha^ do the
myse. See chil. Eras.

In the eight Scene.
Qua spe, aut quo consilio huc imus? In hope of what, or in what entente comme
we hither?

Quid coeptas Thraso? what entende you, or, what begin you to do Thraso?

Thaidi me dedam, et faciam quod iubeat. I woll yield my self unto Thais, and
wol do what she shall command me.

Qui minus, quam Hercules seruiuit Omphale? why not, as well as Hercules did
service unto Omphale? Omphale was a maiden, and queen of the country of Lydia,
whom Hercules did love, and to win her favor, did at her commandment
slay a great serpent about the flode of Sagaris, and than after served the same
Omphale as her womman servant, and as if he had been her bond maiden: In so
much that she compelled him to pycke wolle, and to spynne and card, where as
her self used to shoot, and to bear a mace in her hand, and to were upon her
the heed of a lion (as if she had been a valyant knight) and refused to go in
apparel meet and conuenyent for her sex.

Exemplum placet. I like that example or comparison well.

Vtinam ribi commitigari uideam sandalio caput. would god that I might see thy
palad tanned or made sobre with a slypper.

Quid hoc est mali? what mysfortune is here befallen?

Hunc ego nunquam uideram. This manne had I never seen before.

Quidnam properans prosilijt? wherefore comes he forth so fast skyppynge and
leaping?

In the nynthe Scene.
Ecquis me uiuit hodie fortunatior? Is there any man living this day more
fortunate, or more happy tha^ I am? Hadrian in de ser. lat. has noted that
this particle, ec, has a very great grace in askyng a question, wha^ it is
compowned with quis, quae, quod, uel quid. Cic. Attico, Ecquis unquam tam ex
amplo statu, tam in bona caussa, tantis facultatibus ingenij, consilij, gratiae
tantis prae sidijs bonoru^ omniu^, concidit? was there ever any man suppressed
or undone being of so high degree, having so good and rightful a cause,
having so high a gift and induemert of wit, polycie, and grace to have the
favor of men, and having so great assistence and aid of al good and honest
men? And sometime it is used infinitely with semblable and no less grace or
elegancie. Cic. Attico. Quod quaeris, ecquae spes pacificationis sit, quantum
ex Pompeij multo et accurato sermon perspexi, ne uoluntas quidem est, As
touching that you desire to know whether there be any hope of atonement and
peace to be made between Caesar and Pompeius, as far as I have well perceived
by much and very curious or precise communication with Pompeius, there is no
such mind ne will neither.

Nemo hercle quisquam. In faith no man in the world. Of nemo elegantely joined
with quisquam hit is noted in Hadriane de sermon. latino.

In me plane dij potestatem suam omnem ostendere. In me the goddis have
plainly showed all their hole power, and how much they are able to do.

O mearum uoluptatum omnium inuentor, inceptor, perfector. O thou that haste
ben the deuyser and fynder out, the begynner, and also the fynyssher of al my
plesures.

Scis in quibus sim gaudijs? Do thou know in what great joys I am?

Scis Pamphilem meam inuentam ciuem? Do thou know that my best beloue
Pamphila is found to be free borne of this city?

Scis Pamphilam meam sponsa mihi? Do thou know that my best beloue Pamphila
is promised and made sure to me, to marye with me?

Audin'tu illum? Do thou here him?

Meo fratri gaudeo amorem esse omnem in tranquillo. I am right glad and
joyous that my brothers love is all quiet, and out of trouble.

In clientelam et fidem nobis dedit se. He is become our client, and has put
him self holely in our hands.

Hoc aliud est, quod gaudeamus. This is an other thing, of which we may be
glad, quod.i. propter quod, eclipsis praepositionis. Miles pellitur for as. The
soldier is clean expoulsed, or out of doors, or banysshed the house.

Frater ubi ubi est, fac quam primum haec audiat. Help that my brother may
here of all this at ones where so ever he be. Vbi ubi.i vbicunque. For all
voices that are relatiues, may sometimes be interrogatiues, as when they ask a
question, and sometimes they be neither relatiues nor interrogatiues, but are
put and taken infinitely, and than if they be dowbled, that is to say,
compowned with them selfs, they sygnifye as much as if they were compowned
with this partycle cumque, as quisquis.i. quicunque, qualisqualis,.i.
qualiscunque, quoquo.i. quocunque, quantus quantus,.i. qua^tuscunque, ubi
ubi.i. ubicunque, and so of all others. Quamprimum.i. ualde cito, or
primoquoque tempore, as soon as may be. For quam, in composition signifies
ualde, and therefore is joined with the superlatiue degree. And note that there
is a great difference between quamprimum, and cumprimu^, of which read Laur.
Vall. l. eleg. c. 17.

Numquid dubitas, quin ego perpetuo perierim? Do thou thynk any other, but
that I am utterly undone for ever? or, do thou not think verily, that I am
utterly undone for ever?

Sine dubio opinor. Without doubt, I think so.

Quid commemorem primum? what should I speak of first?

Quem laudem maxim? whom may I most commend or preise?

Dedit mihi consilium ut facerem. He gave me counsel to do it.

O Iuppiter, serua obsecro haec nobis bona. Oh Iuppiter, keep and contynne us
in this good fortune, felicity, or prosperity, I beseech the.

Incredibilia modo narrauit. He told wonders while ere.

Vbi est frater? where is my brother?

Praesto adest. He is even here ready.

Satis credo. I believe well, or I think well.

Nihil est Thaide dignius quod ametur. There is nothing more worthy to be
beloved than is Thais.

Nostrae est omni fautor familiae. He favors or loves all our house well.

Quanto minus spei est, tanto magis amo. The less hope there is, the more am I
in love.

Perfice hoc praecibus, pretio, ut heraea^ in part aliqua apud Thaidem. Bring
this to pass with prayers, or with money, that I may be an hanger on in one
part or other, with Thais.

Difficile est. It is hard.

Si quid collibuit noui te. If thou be well disposed to a thing, I know the
well enough what thou canste do.

Hoc si effeceris, quoduis donum, et praemi um a me optato id optatum feres. If
thou may bring this to pass, wysshe or desire of me what so ever gift or
reward thou wolte, and thou shall have thy desire. Donum est, quod gratis
datur, praemium quod pro meritis confertur.

Postulo, ut mihi tua do mus, te present, te absente, pateat semper. I require
that your house may be open for me at all times, whether thou be in the way, or
out of the way.

Do fidem ita futurum. I promise the faith fully that it shall so be.

Quem hic ego audio? who is that, that I here speak here?

Tu fortasse, quae facta hic sunt, nescis. Thou do not know peradventure
what things have benne done, or have happened here.

Cur in his te conspicor regionibus? why do I se the in these parties or in
these quarters?

Edico tibi. I tell the openly. Edico, edicis, edixi, edicere, edictum, is
proprely to proclayme, and perteynethe oonelye to princes, officers, and
renlers, and thereof hoc edictum, edicti, cto, is the commandment given or
proclaimed by any prince, ruler, or officer. But here edico, is taken for clare
dico, et clare proloquor. To speak or to pronounce out aloude without any fere
or dissimulation, for e, and ex, in composition have much that signification,
as eruo, is to get or to mine a thing out of the earth or any other place,
where it is hard to comme by. Egero, is to cast out, effero, is to bring out,
expello, is to thruste out. Eloquor is to speak out alowde, and so edico here
is to speak out alowde. And in the same signification did Terence use edico
before in the fyfte Scene of this fyfte act, in this same comedy in the
person of Parmeno, saying thus unto Pythias. Dico, edico uobis nostrum essem
illum herilem filium, I tell you, ye and I tell you plainly and boldly, that he
is my masters son: as who should say, I am not aferde to tell it you, but
would that you should well know it, and warn you that you do him no harm.

Si in platea hac te offendero post unquam, nihil est quod dicas mihi, alium
quaerebam iter hac habui, peristi. If it shall be my chance to find the in this
street at any time after this day, it shall nothing avail the to say unto
me, I sought for an other man, or my iourneye lay this way, for thou art but
a deed manne.

Eia haud sic decet. what soft, that is not semynge, or it should not be for
your honesty so to do. Eia is an aduerbe of correctynge.

Non cognosco uestrum tam superbu^. s in geniu^ uel genus, uel animu^, uel
more^, uel in stitutu^. I am not acqueinted with this proud and disdaynefull
facion of you, or I can no skylle of this your. etc. All be it Donate takes
these words, Vestrum tam superbu^, to be put absolutely, that is
substantifely, as uestrum tam superbum.i. uestram tanta^ superbiam. Donate
brings in for his authority a like manner of speaking out of Andria in the
syxte Scene of the fourth Act: Pol Crito antiquum obtines. Of which it is
there sufficiently noted.

Prius audite paucis, quod cum dixero, si placuerit, facitote. First here in
two or three words, and when I have said, if it shall like you, do it.

Tu concede paululu^ isthuc Thraso. Thraso go you and stand a little further
that way.

Ego uos credere hoc mihi uehementer ue lim. I would that you in any wise
believe me in this.

Si uobis prodest, uos non facere inscitia est. If it be for your profit, it
is a folyshenes for you not to do it.

Magis oportunus, nec magis ex usu tuo ne mo est. There is no man more mete for
it, nor no man more to your co^modite. Oportunus.i. idoneus, mete for the
purpose. Here note that three negacions do no more to the sense, nor have no
more signifycation nor strength, than two, as Laur. Vall. notith in the third
book of eleg. and the.27. chapter, as numquam mihi nec obfuisti, nec
profuifti, Thou never dyddest me neither harm, ne good. Cic. in tusc. quaest.
Nescirent nec ubi nec qualia essent, They knew not neither where, nor what
things they were. Ibid. Nihil nec disputare, nec scribere pretermisi, I
omytted nothing neither to dispute and reason, nor to write. Note also that
two or three negations do sometimes deny with more vebemencie. Plin. l. 18.
cap. 4. Oculorum uitia fieri negant, nec lippire eos, qui cum pedes lauant,
aqua inde ter oculos tangant. It is a saying, that they shall never have
diseases in their eyes, nor shall not be bleare eyed, which when they wasshe
their feet, touch their eyes thryse with the same water. Examples be
innumerable, and Budaeus has noted the same largely and copiously in his
annotacions upon the pandechtes of the civil law.

Et habet, quod det, et dat nemo largius. He both has ynowghe to give, and
does give no man more liberally.

Fatuus est, insulsus, tardus. He is a natural fool without any wisdom or good
facion, nor has any quyckenes or lustynes, nor actiuitie or spirit in him.
Fatuus (says Donat) inepta loquens, spekyng folyshe words. For fatuus (says
he) is said and derived a fando, of speaking. And thereof fauni, that is to
say rusticall the goddie of the woddes, which are called in greek Satyri,
were called in latyne fatui, i. (as Seruius upon Vergyll and Donate in this
place expounds it) multum fantes, hoc est multum loquentes, a fando et
uaticinando, So that after Donate fatui be they that are folyshe in their
words and sayenges, Insulsi are follysshe in the herfe, mind, and
intelligence, and after him they err and be deceived that think that fatuus
is animo and cord, and insulsus, in uerbis et dictis. But Laur. Vall. l. 4.
eleg. ca. 13. says in this wise, he is called in latin stultus, that lacks
experience of things, and knowledge of the world, nor has no foresight in
things to come, so that many being no unwise men otherwise, yet may at
sometime happly do stulte, that is foolysshely, or (more proprely to say)
unadvisedly. Fatuus, is he that is a very sole, and has no manner wisdom at
all. And it is by translation taken of the saueryenes of meats, for when
meats be all werysshe and unsavory, they be called in latyne fatui cibi. And
semblably a man that has no wisdom is called in latin fatuus. Martial. li.
12.

Vt sapiant fatuae fabrorum prandia betae,

O quam saepe petet uina piperque coquus.

O how often woll the cook ask and require wine and pepper for to make the
werysh beetes (that smythes and carpenters dyne withal) to be somewhat sauery.
And this seems to be a better reason for this vocable fatuus, than that other,
which certain writers do approve and allow, that is to say, that they are
called in latyne fatui, which being taken with a certain fury or madness
(such as Fatua the wise of king Faunus was much taken with all) do prophecy
things to come, like as she the said fatua did. Stolidus is he that is
folysshe and draws much nygh unto the nature and perceiving or
understanding of sheep or other folysshe brute beasts. Hactenus Valla.
Insulsus is be that has no wisdom, nor wit, nor no grace nor good facion
neither in words, nor gesture, nor otherwise in his behauoure. what sal and
sales signify, it is largely showed and declared in the third scene of the
second act of this same comedy. Of sal comes salsitudo or salsedo: Of
salsitudo or salcedo, is fourmed salsus a, um, a thing that is salt and by
translation, wyttie, sharp and pleasant and also bytynge in words or
otherwise. Contrary unto salsus is insulsus, a, um, without any wyttynes or
pleasant facion, and consequently very foolysshe and such as no man may have
any pleasure in.

Stertit noctesque et dies. He lies routynge and snortynge all day and all
night.

Facile pellas, ubi uelis. You may easilye thruste him out of doors when you
list.

Hoc ego uel primu^ puto. This do I think even principal and chief of al.
Vel.i. etiam.

Accipit hominem nemo melius prorsus, neque prolixius. He enterteineth a man,
no man in the world better, nor more sumptuously. Melius.i. lautius, more
deyntely, For it is referred to the provision and deyntines of cates, And
prolixius.i. largius, copiosius more abundantly and plentiously, for it is
referred to the abundance and plentee of all such things as are provided.

Vos oro, ut me in gregem uestrum recipiatis. I desire and pray you to receive
me into your flock, as who should say, that I may be admytted in to your
company as one of you. See chil. Eras. in the proverb, De grege illo est.

Satis diu hoc iam saxum uoluo. I have labored abonte this long enough now,
if that be good. It is a prouerbial? speaking alludynge unto the fable of
Sisyphus, which (as the poets feyne) was son unto Eolus, and a great thief
in Isthmoo, he used when any strangers arrived there to slay them with stones,
and to toumble them down into the water from the tops of the rocks, at
last he was slain by one Theseus, and when he came to Hell, this
punysshement was qyuen unto him, that he should bear up a great stone unto the
top of an hylle, and as often as it rolled down to fette it up again, but
he could never cause it to to lie, but that it rolled down to the hills
foot again immediately after that he had brought it to the top, and so his
labor is infinite, and of this fable such as have great and the same endless
peines with out any fruit or profit, are prouerbially said in latin saxum
uoluere, to tumble or to roll the stone. Read Eras. in chil.

Isti te ignorabant. These men did not know the.

Postquam eis mores ostendi tuos, et collaudaui secundum facta, et uirtutes
tuas, impetraui. As soon as I had enfourmed them of your conditions, and had
preysed you, according to your acts and virtues or good qualities, I opteyned.

Gratiam habeo maximam. I thank you with all my heart. Habere gratiam is
proprely in the heart when we here well in mind and remember such benefytes as
we have had or received, and have good mind and will to do semblable benifites
ageyn, in recompense of them. Read Laur. Vall. l. 5. eleg. cap. 4 1.

Numquam fui usquam, quin me omnes amarent plurimu^. I was never yet to in any
place, but that eueri bodi loved me very well.

Dixin' ego uobis ni hoc esse atticam eloquentiam? Did not I tell you, that
you should find in this man the most pure and high eloquence that is? Attica,
cae, or At, tes, is a region or countraye in Grecia situate and lyeng between
Achaia and Macedonia, in which countray or region stood the city of Athenes,
where was spoken the most pure and clean, and most eloquent greek, like as
in London is spoken the best and most pure and true english, and in Parise
the best french. etc. and by reason thereof Attica eloque^tia is used for the
most pure true and polyte eloquence, and that eloquence, which the best and
most cunning orators used: which best and chief orators for the same
cause were called in latyne Attici, as who should say, most eloquent, as
coming most nyghe unto the pure eloquence of the Attiques. Al be it here in
this place it is spoken ironice, much like as if Gnato should have said by
Thraso in english, Did not I tell you, that this gentle man rolieth in his
rhetorike as apes do in tails? For he said a little before, that Thraso was
fatuus, insulsus, et tardus.

Nihil praetermissum. s. est. There has nothing ben omytted or let pass.

Ite hac omnes uos. Goo all you thie way here.

FINIS EVNVCHI.
EX HEAVTONTIMORVMENO.
In the Prologue.
NE CVI SIT uestrum mirum. None of you marvel. Id primum dicam, deinde, quod
ueni, eloquar. I wolle first tell that, and afterward I woll show and declare
the cause of my hither coming, quod.i. propter quod, or cuius caussa, cuius
gratia.

Ni partem maximam existimarem scire uestrum, id dicerem. I would tell it, if
I thought not that the most part of you know it well enough all ready.

Nunc quam obrem has parteis didicerim, paucis dabo. Now wolle I show you in
two or three words, for what cause I learned to play this part.

Rumores distulerunt maleuoli. Ill wyllers, or maligners have spread abroad
naugh tie tales. Differo, differs, distuli, dilatum, has many significations,
to differ, to prolonge, or to delay. Lucanus. Nocuit differ pararis. It
has done harm unto many folks, after that they have been ready, to make
longer delay. Sometime to endure as Plin. in epist. did put differ sitim,
to endure thyrste, as who should say, to forbear to drink though one were
thyrstie. Sommetymes differ is to trouble a man, and with sum sodeyne fear
to bring him at his wits end, that he know not what first or best to do.
Plaut. in Pseud* Iam ego te differa^ dictis meis impudice, I woll rattell or
shake the up ere I goo that thou shalt not wot what to do, shameless fellow
that thou art. Id. est in Cistell. Miser, exanimor, feror, differor, distrahor,
diripior, ita nullam mentem animi habeo, I miserable body am almost deed, I
go like a mad manne, I wotte not whither, I am in such grefe that me
thinks I am pulled in pieces, I am haled in sondre, and am torn in pieces, I
have so lost all the reason and wit that was in my heed. So Terence before in
Andria. Orationem sperat inuenisse se, quo differat te, proin tu face ut apud
te sies. He hopethe that he has now found a tale against the, wherewith to
bring the at thy wits end, therefore see thou that thy wits be thine own.
And in this signification differor is much used among the latyne authors, as
differor amore uel cupiditate, I am in extreme peynes for love and desire:
Differor doloribus, I am in such extreme peynes, that I think my self to be
torn in pieces and may no longer endure for peyn: Differor laeticia, I am
rauyshed with ioyfulnes. Some times differo is to sow or to spread abroad in
diverse placis, as differ alicui famam, is to spread abroad a ma^s name in
diverse places. And differ rumorem de aliquo, is to sow and bring up and to
sprede abroad in diverse places a brute, or a noise, or tale of or by any body,
as in this place of Terence. Some times differe is to be unlike, or to be
diverse from an other thing. Rumor, oris, or fama, mae, is a fame, a brute, or
a noise brought up of any new thing, the author or brynger up thereof being
unknown, and whether the same brute or fame be good or ylle. what rumor and
fama signify, and of the use of them, see in Laur. Valla. lib. 4. eleg. c. 10.

Factu^ hic esse id non negar, et se deinde facturum autumat. He here says not
the contrary but that it was so done, and he thinks to do the same ageyn
hereafter.

Habet bonorum exemplum, quo exemplo, sibi licere id facere, quod illi
fecerunt, putat. He has to lay for him the example of good and honest
persons, by which example he thinks and reckeneth that he may lefully do
the same, that they have done before him. Exemplum is the thing that we
follow, or eschew, and exemplar is the thing in which exemplum is contained,
as here in this place of Terence, exemplum is in bonis, and ipsi boni, be
exemplar, or exemplaria. And semblably the eloquence of Cicero is exemplum for
us to follow, and Cicero self is exemplar, in which exemplum of eloquence is
contained, and semblably of all other things, as Laur. Vall. has copiously
and counnyngly annoted. li. 6. eleg. ca. 33.

Omnes uos oratos uolo. I would desire you all.

Ne ille pro se dictum existimet. Think he not that to be spoken in his favor.

Fecit seruo currenti in uia. He did hit unto the servant, as he ran by the
street.

Cur insano feruiat? why should he do service unto a mad man?

Finem maledictis facit. He leueth railing.

Adeste aequo animo. Stand still by, quietly and patiently.

Date potestatem mihi. Give me license.

Clamore summo, et labor maximo. with very high or loud cryeng out or
halowing, and with very great labor.

Caussam hanc iustam esse, animum inducite. Persuade your selfs and think in
your minds, that this cause or matter is good, just, and rightful.

Vt aliqua pars laboris minuatur mihi. That some part of my labor may be
cut of, or that I may have sommewhat the less labor and pain.

Ad me curritur. Every body comes ronnynge to me.

Experimini in utranque partem ingenium quid possit meum. Assay and prove ye
what my wit is able to do in or for both partis.

Nunquam auare pretium statui arti meae. I never set to high a price on my
craft^ or I was never covetous in setting price on my craft.

Eum esse quaestum in animum induxi maximum, quammaxime inseruire uestris
commodis. I have always been content to think and to reckon my most
winning, gaynes, and profit to be, to do all diligent service that I can for
your profits.

Exemplum statuite in me, ut adolescentuli uobis placere studeant. Show an
exaumple upon me, that other young men may be desirous and may labor to be in
favor with you, or to get your favor.

Out of the first Act in the first Scene.
Inter nos nupera dmodum noticia est. It is a very little while gone sithe we
have been acqueynted to gyther.

Agrum in proximo hic mercatus es. You have bought a plotte of ground here
next besides me.

Nec rei amplius quidquam fuit. s. inter nos. And there has been none other
thing at all between us.

Vel uirtus tua me, uel uicinitas, quod ego in propinqua part amicitiae puto,
facit, ut te audacter moneam, and familiariter. Both your virtue, goodness, or
honesty, and also that we are neighbors and dwell nyghe to gether (which
thing I reckon as one of the chief parts and greatteste causes of entiere
amity and frendshyp) causes me to speak unto you, and to show you my mind
boldly and familiarly like a friend. Monere or admonere is to tell a man of his
fault with a certain correction, rebukyng, or chydyng.

Mihi uidere praeter aetatem tuam facere, et praeter quam res te adhortatur
tua. Me thinks you do otherwise than is convenient for your age, and other
wise than is requisyte for a man of your substance.

Annos sexaginta natus es, aut plus eo, ut conijcio. You ar three score years
old, or above, as I suppose. Of amplius elegantlye and indifferently joined
with the nominative, accusative, or ablative case, see in Hadrian de sermon.
latino.

Agrum in his regionibus meliorem, neque pretij maioris, nemo habet. No man in
all these parties has a better plotte of ground nor better land, or more
worth.

Nunquam tam mane egredior, neque tam uesperi domum reuertor, quin te in fundo
conspicer fodere, aut arare, aut aliquid facere. I never go forth so erely in
the morning, nor come home again so late in the evening, but that I see the
abroad in thy ground either dyggynge, or ploughynge, or else some other work
doing.

Nullum remittis tempus, neque te respicis. You are no time unoccupied, nor
pass any thing on your self, or set any store by your self.

Haec non uoluptati tibi esse satis certo scio. That these things are no
pleasure unto you, I am very well assured.

At enim dices. But perauenture you wolle say unto me.

Me quantum hic operis fiat poenitet. Me thinks all the work that is done
here to little. Me poenitet.i. mihi parum uidetur. For poenitere is to be
sorry, to repent, or to forthynke, and because they that are soory, that their
matters or business goes not well forewarde, think all that is done to
little, therefore poenitere is sometimes eleganty taken for parum uideri, to
seem little. Verg. in bucol. aeg. l. 2. Nec te paeniteat calamo triuisse
labellum, And think it not a small thing to have learned to play on the pype
or the recorder. Cicero in prefatione libri primi officiorum. Quamobrem disces
tu quidem a principe huius aetatis philosophorum, et disces quam diu uoles, tam
diu autem uelle debebis, quoad te quantum proficias non paenitebit, Wherefore
thou shall learn and be scoler unto Cratippus, the best and chief of all
philosophers that are at this day, as long as thou shalt be willing thy
self, and so long thou ought to be willing, as thou shall not thynk thy
self to do little good, and to lose thy time, but to profit. So Terentius
before in Eunucho act. 5. sc. 7. An poenitebat flagitij, te authore, quod
fecisset adolescens, ni miserum insuper etiam patri iudicares? Did thou not
think that great offense, which the young man had done by thy counsel and
setting on, to be enough, but that thou must moreover also be the first that
should betray and appeche the poor soul unto his father?

Quod in opere faciu^do operae co^sumis tuae, si sumas in seruis exercendis,
plus agas. If you would bestow that labor in exercisyng you servants, and
setting them to work, and seenge them occupied, which you spend in werkynge
or labouryng your own body, you should do more good, or, you should have more
good done, or, you should have more profyt by it.

Tantum ne est ab re tua otij tibi, aliena ut cures, eaque quae nihil ad te
attinent? Have thou so much leisure and void time from thyn own busynes,
that thou may meddle in other men's matiers, and in such things as
apperteyne nothing unto the?

Homo sum, humani a me nihil alienum puto. I am a man, as other men be, and
think every thing that apperteyneth to any man, to apperteyne also unto me.

Tibi opus ut est facto, face, pro fac. Do thou as is expedient for the to do.

An cuiquam est usus homini se ut cruciet? Is it expedient for any man to
punysshe his own body, and to put him self to tomoche pain?

Ne lachruma. Weep not. Ne, when it is an aduerbe of forbyddyng, may be
indifferently joined with a verb of the subiu^ctiue mode, or else of the
imperative. But non may never be joined with the imperatyue, and with all
other modes hit may, as Linacre has noted.

Isthuc quicquid est, fac ut sciam. Let me know the matter what so ever it
be.

Ne retice. Hyde hit not, or keep hit not in. Donatus has noted, that
reticere, is to keep in and not to utter such things as we are soory and take
thought for. Obticere, is to keep in such things as we are asshamed of.
Terentius in Eunucho. Virgo conscissa ueste lachrumans obticet, The maiden
having her gown al to cut wepethe, and wolle speak never a word.

Tacere is to keep secret things of counsaylle, as before in Eunucho. Potin'
est hic tacere? Can this fellow concele any secretis? or keep any counsel?

Ne reuerere. Be not aferde.

Te aut consolando, aut consilio, aut reiuuero. I wolle surely help you
either by comfortynge you, or else with gyuynge you sum good counsaylle, or
else in very deed.

Hac equidem caussa, qua dixi tibi. In faith even for the same cause that I
have told you.

Istos rastros interea depone, ne labora. Lay down thy rake in the mean
season, and labor or work not.

Quam rem agis? What do you? or what entende you? or what go you about?

Sine me uacuum tempus ne quod them mihi laboris. Let me alone, that I may not
suffer my self to let any time pass void of labor.

Non aequum facis. You do not well or honestly, or as you should do.

Sic meritum est meum. So have I deserved.

Filium unum adolescentulu^ habeo. I have but one son, and he is a young
stryplynge.

Nunc habeam nec ne, incertum est. I am in doubt whether I have one now or not.

Quid ita isthuc. s. dicis? Why say thou that?

Est e Corintho hic aduena auus paupercula. There is here a poor old womanne,
a stranger that came out of Corinthe hither.

Eius filiam ille amare coepit perdite. Her daughter he began to love so
unthriftily that he had almooste cast away and undone him self.

Prope iam ut pro uxore haberet. So that he used her in manner as his wife.

Haec clam me omnia. s. fecit. All this he did unknowing to me.

Vbi rem resciui. Aftir that I had knowledge of the matter. Rescio, rescis,
resciui, rescitum, and a verb frequentatiue of the same Rescisco, resciscis,
resciui, resciscere, rescitum, after Aulus Gel. is to have some preuy knowledge,
and (as we say) an ynklynge of any thing done privily, or otherwise
dissembled and kept from our knowledge. But Valla checketh and reproves the
said Aul. Gel. for that saying, and shows that Rescisco is a plain verb,
and is proprely to have knowledge of a thing after that it is done, of which
thing thou were unknowing when it was done, specially if it apperteyne to
the, or to any of thyn.

Tibi ne haec licere speras facere, me uiuo? Doesth thou think that thou shall
be suffered to do such things as this, while I am alive?

Erras, si id credis, et me ignoras Clinia. Clinia thou art deceived, if thou
so think, and thou know me not.

Ego te meum esse dici tantisper uolo, dum quod te dignum est facies. I wolle
thou be called mine, so long as thou shall do as becomes the, and no longer.
For that is the use of tantisper joined with dum taken for quamdiu, as shows
Val. li. 2. eleg. ca. 48.

Si quod te dignum est, no^ facis, ego quod me in te sit facere dignum
inuenero. If thou do not as becomes the to do, I shall find the means to do
by the, as shall become me.

Nulla adeo ex re isthuc fit, nisi ex nimio otio. Surely this comes of none
other thing but of to much ese and ydelnes. Adeo.i. certe, profecto.

Ego isthuc aetatis non amori operam dabam. I when I was of the age that thou
art, did not bestow nor set my mind on wanton love. The latin authors
use with great grace and much elegancie Id temporis, per id tempus, id
aetatis, hoc aetatis, isthuc aetatis, quid aetatis, absolutely. Whereof read in
Hadrian de sermon. lat.

In Asiam hinc abij propter pauperiem. I was feign to go out of these parties
into the country of Asia for pouerte.

A dolescentulus saepe eadem et grauiter audiendo uictus est. The young man
with often and sore herynge one thing was even done, or was greatly dismayed.

Putauit me aetate plus scire. He thought that I knew more, by reason that I
was older, or, he thought that I could better skylle in things, or, was
better seen in experience of the world, because that I had moo years.

Putauit me beneuolentia plus prouidere, quam seipsum sibi. He thought that I
would provide for him, or foresee what was most expediente for his profit,
better thanne he could for him self, for thentiere and hearty love that I bare
him.

In Asiam ad regem militatum abijt. He is goone hens into the countreye of Asia
unto the king there, to be a soldier, and to goo on warrefare.

Clam me profectus, menseis tres abest. He went away and took his journey
unknowing to me, and has been away now a holle quarter of a year. In words
betokenynge space of time the use of the accusative signifies continuance of
time without intermission or cessynge, secuudum Seruium. Al be it we may use
also the ablative.

Ambo accusandi. s. estis. You are both to blame.

Illud in caeptum animi est pudentis signum. That beginning is a sign or
token of a shamefaste heart.

Vbi comperi, ex ijs, qui ei fuere conscij. When I knew of it by them that
were preuye to it, and of his counsaylle.

Domum reuertor moestus. Home I come again all sad.

Domum reuertor animo fere perturbato, atque incerto prae aegritudine. Home I
come again in manner out of my mind, and uncertain what to do for thought.
Cice. in tusc. quaest. shows, that aegritudo proprely is in the mind, and
aegrotatio in the body, and that animus est aeger, corpus aegrotum. Al be it
tho words are much confounded, that is to say the one used for the other, as
well in the said Cicero, as in Quintilian, Terence, and others.

Accurrunt serui, soccos detrahunt. My servants came rounnynge to me, and
poulled of my pynsons or stertuppes. Soccus, socci, socco, was a kind of
shoes, and hit is formed of saccus sacci, a bag, and had it name thereof
because that when it was fastened upon the foot, it bagged and lay full of
pleytes. And they were used in wearynge both of men and women, but most among
the nobles and rich folks.

Video alios festinare, lectos sternere, coena^ apparare. I see some others
make haste, to lay the table, and to make ready for suppar. Lectus, cti, is a
bed. Festus Pompeius de ryueth Lectum ab alliciendo, that when the body is
weary, it desires rest. And some deryue lectum of the grecke word lectron: All
be hit lectus, ut inquit Varro, dictus est, quod lectis herbis et frondibus
stramenta facerent, Lectus is so called (says Varro) because that in old time
they used to gather grass, and leaves or bowghes, and so to straw them on
the ground, and than to lie down on them. And because they used much to eat
in the same place (for than they had not yet found the use of meat tables)
therefore lectus was also taken for mensa, and is much used in that
signification in Plant. Terence, and all poetis and other writers, that be of
any antiquity, Horat, Saepe tribus lectis, uideas coenare quaternos, A man
shall see often times three tables at ones, and at every table four persons at
suppar, or eating mete.

Pro se quisque sedulo faciebat, quo illam mihi lenirent miseriam. Every body
for his part was as busy as they could be, to ease me of the care that I was
in.

Vbi uideo haec, caepi cogitare. When I saw all this, I began to cast and
think in my mind.

Tot mea solius soliciti sunt caussa, ut me unum expleant? Are so many persons
disesed and troubled for my cause and sake, only to satisfye the pleasure of me
alone? Of mea solius caussa, with others like, reed Laur. Vall. l. 2. eleg.
ca. 1. about the mids, where he does copiously and counnyngely treat
thereof. Vnu^.i. solum.

Sumptus domi tantos ego solus faciam? Should I alone, or being but one man,
spend so much money, or be at so greatte charges in my house?

Gnatum unicum pariter uti his decuit, aut etiam amplius. It was mete that my
only son should have had as much part of these things as I, or else
rather more.

Illa aetas magis ad haec utenda idonea est. That age is more mete to occupy
tho things. When there comith in any latyne clause a gerundiue of a verb
transitiue, that gouer with an accusatife case aftir him, the geru^diue may
very elegantly be changed into a nown participle of the later future tense, and
made to agree with the substantive in gender, nombre, and like case, as the
gerundiue was before it was changed, an example of the ablative case. Cic. in
prefat. li. 1. offic. Oratione^ latinam efficies profecto legendis nostris
plaeniorem, For legendo nostra, Thy latin tongue thou shalt make more full
and more copious by redynge my works, and such books as I have written. An
example of the accusative. Id. l. i. offic. Meminerimus autem et aduersus
infimos iusticiam esse seruanda^, For seruandum esse iusticiam, And we must
remember to keep Justice, that is, to dele justly and truly also with the
poorest and lowest persons that be. An example of the genitive case. Ibid. Vt
nec medici, nec imperatores, nec oratores, quamuis artis praecepta perceperint,
quicquam magna laud dignum, sine usu et exercitatione consequi possunt, sic
officij conseruandi praecepta traduntur illa qui them, ut faciamus ipsi, For
praecepa conseruandi officium, As neither physicians, nor capytaynes in war,
nor orators, though they have substancially well learned all the rules of their
faculte, yet may not possibly at teyne to do any thing worthy great preise and
commendacion, without much exercise and practisynge, right so there be
written and given unto us precepts of our office and duty how to behave our
selfs in our living toward all persons, but that is to the intent that we
must put them in ure and practice the same our selfs. I said a verb
transitiue, that governs an accusative case. For such mutacion or change may
not be done in any verbes gouernynge any other case and not an accusative. For
we may not say placendi praeceptoris studium, but praeceptori, nor in male
dicendo te, but tibi, nor in abstinendis uoluptatibus, but in abstinendo a
uoluptatibus, nor ad abundanda bona, but ad abundandu^ bonis. Therefore when we
say ad haec utenda, children shall note, that the latyne men of old time used
these verbes, utor, fungor, fruor, potior, and sometimes careo, gouernynge an
accusative case after them, examples be every where innumerable apud Plautu^,
Terentiu^, Ciceronem, iureconsultos, et alios.

Eum ego eieci hinc miseru^ iniusticia mea. I have expulsed and driven him from
hens poor soul by mine unreasonable facion or dealing.

Malo quidem me dignu^ quouis deputem, si id faciam. I might well think my
self worthy any mysauenture in the world, if I should do such a thing.

Vsque dum ille uitam illam incolit. pro colit, agit, degit. As long as he
lives such a life as that is, or, as long he lives after that sort, or
rate.

Illi de me supplicium dabo. I woll suffer him to punyshe me, or, I wol suffer
punishment for his sake. Dare paenas, dare supplicium, Luere poenas, luere
supplicium, pendere poenas, and pendere supplicium, is to suffer punyshement,
or to be punyshed. And they be construed with a nominatyue of the thing that
suffers the punysshement, and with a datyue case of the person that does
punysshe.

Nil relinquo in aedibus, nec uas, nec uestime^ tum. I left nothing in the
house, neither vessel, nor garment, nor nothing.

Corrasi omnia. I have scraped up and sold all to gether. Corrasi.i.
compilaui, uendidi. Donat.

Inscripsi aedes mercede. I gave up my house, and wrote upon the doors that
the house was to let, or to be sold. So Plaut. in Trinu^mo. Quia rure dum sum
ego unos sex dies, me absente, atque insciente, inconsultu meo, aedes uenales
hasce inscripsit literis, Because that while I have been in the country, but
one poor six days, he has written upon the door that this my house was to
selle, I being from home, absent or out of the way, and knowing nothing
thereof, and without any counsel asking of me.

Agrum hu^c mercatus sum. I have bought this plotte of ground. The difference
between ager, fundus, uilla, et praedium, read in Valla. li. 6. eleg. c. 41.

Hic me exerceo. Here am I occupied, or here do I exercise my self.

Non fas est ulla me uoluptate frui. I may not take any manner pleasure.

Ingenio te esse in liberos leui puto. I reken you to be of a gentle nature
and tender on your children.

Puto illum obsequentem, si quis recte aut commode tractaret. I reckon him
tractable or eath enough to be ruled, if a man did handle him well, or after a
good sort.

Neque tu illum satis noueras, nec te ille. Neither thou knew him very well,
nor he the: or, neither thou were very well acqueynted with his facion nor he
with thyn.

Nunquam ostendisti, quanti illum penderes. You never showed how much you
set by him.

Nec ille tibi est credere ausus. And he durst not trust the.

Ita res est. So goes the matter, or so hit is.

Illum saluum adfuturum esse confido propediem. I trust verily that he woll
right shortly be here in good health.

Vtinam dij ita faxint. God grannt hit may so be.

Si co^modum est, hodie apud me sis uolo. If you may conveniently, I would
desire you to make merry at my house to day.

Siccine est sententia? Think you so? or, is that your mind or opinion in
deed?

Quaeso tandem aliquantulum tibi parce. I pray you favor or spare your self
somewhat at last after so great labor.

Been uale. Fare ye well, or god be with you.

Lachrymas excussit mihi. He has caused me to weep.

Miseret me eius. I have pity on him.

Mouere oportet me hunc, ad caenam ut ueniat. I must put him in remembrance
to come to suppar.

Ibo ut uisam, si domi est. I woll go to see if he be at home. It is showed
before, that uiso, facesso, capesso, lacesso, with other like verbes in so, be
not desideratiues, as Priscian would have them, and that they signify and
betoken the mouyuge and act of th body: and not the affecte nor desire of the
mind. Yet somtymes they be used for their primitiues, as here uisam is put for
his primitive uideam. And so is hit to be taken as often as it is joined with
any of these words, ueino, eo, is, iui, gratia, caussa, studiu^, or any other
like voice.

Nihil opus fuit monitore, iamdudum domi praesto apud me esse aiunt. He had
no need of any manne to put him in remembrance. For they say that he is at
home at my house all ready, and has been a good while.

Egomet conuiuas moror. I my self cause my guestes to tarry. Moror te (as
testifies Donatus and also Valla) in orators is most taken for retineo te,
or in mora teneo, I cause the to tarry. Quintilian. Quid me ad huc pater
detines? quid moraris abeuntem? Father wherefore do you hold and keep me back
still? Why do you cause me to tarry that would be gone?

Quid crepuerunt fores? Why creeketh the door? or what crekynge makes the
door?

A me quisnam egreditur? Who comes forth of my house.

Huc concessero. I woll stand aside here. Concedo, dis, concessi, concessum,
sometime signifies to goo, or to depart out of one place into an other.

In the second Scene.
Nihil adhuc est, quod uereare. There is nothing yet why thou should be
aferde.

Illum simul cum nuncio tibi hic affuturum hodie scio. I know well that he wol
be here with you anon together with the messanger that went for him. Simul
cum nuncio, as who should say, as soon and with as great expedicion, as the
messanger that was sent for him.

Solicitudinem istam falsam, quae te excruciat, omittas. Let goo that false
care and thought, that vexes and freateth thy-hart.

Qui cum loquitur filius? With whom talks my son.

Opportune aduenis. You come very well.

Hunc Menedemum nostin' nostru^ uicinum? Do you know Menedemus here being our
neighbor?

Mihi magna cum eo iam inde usque a pueritia semper fuit familiaritas. I have
been very familyarly acqueynted with him evermore of a little child.

Voluptatem magnam nuncias. Thou show tidings that be very pleasant.

Quam uellem Menedemum inuitatum, ut nobiscum esset hodie. How glad would I
be that Menedemus had been desired to be in company with us, or at my house
to day.

Cave faxis, non est opus pater. Beware do it not, it is not so expedient
father. We may say Cave faxis, or, cave ne faxis: Cave cadas, or, cave ne
cadas: Fac scribas, or, fac ut scribas: Cura fiat, or, cura ut fiat, ex Valla,
And it is eclipsis coniunctionis.

Incertum est etiam quid se faciat. He can not yet tell what to do with him
self. Of such manner speakynges as this, Quid se faciat, with others like it
is noted before.

Modo uenit. He came but now.

Timet omnia. He fereth all things.

Misere amat. He is very deep in love.

Propter eam haec turba, atque abitio euenit. For her sake has chanced all
this business and going away.

Seruolum ad eam in urbem mifit. He has sent a servant into the city unto
him. Seruolum pro seruulum, per antithesin, which is when one lettre is put
for an other.

Quem minus miserum esse, credere est? Whom should a man think to be in
better case?

Quid reliqui est, quin habeat, quae quidem in homine dicuntur bona? parenteis,
patria^, amicos, genus, cognatos, diuitias. What fauteth, but that he has al
things, at leest wise that in a man are called and rekened good things, that
is to say his father and mother his country, his friends, his stock and
blood, kyne folks and riches?

Haec perinde sunt, ut illius animus, qui ea possidet. All these things be
such, as is the mind of him that has them in possession. Of the elegante
ioynynge of perinde, with ac, atque, ut, quam, quasi, acsi, atque si. etc. Read
Hadrian.

Qui uti scit, ei bona sunt, illi qui non utitur recte, mala. To him that
knows how to use them, they be good, and to him that can not use them as
he should do, they be ylle.

Ille fuit senex importunus semper. That old fool was ever more unreasonable.

Nihil magis uereor, quam ne quid in illum iratus plus satis faxit. I fear
nothing so much as this, leeste that he being moved and bearing him
displeasure, woll do sum thing unto him worse thanne he should do.

Reprimam me.i. tacebo. I would hold my peace.

In metu esse illi est utile. It is expedient for him to be kept in awe.

Quid tu tecum? s. loqueris? What say thou to thy self there?

Vtut erat, mansum tamen oportuit. How so ever hit was, yet he owghte to have
tarried at home, and not to have gone away. The voice of the participle of the
pretertense is much taken and used per enallagen partium, sieve per antimerian,
for the infinitive mode of the active voice, secundu^ Donatu^ or of the passive
voice, secundu^ Priscianum. Salust. Priusqua^ incipias consulto, et ubi
consulueris, mature opus est facto, Before that a man begin any thing, he
must first take good aduysement and deliberation, and when he has so
consulted and taken aduysemente, he owghte to do hit with all celerytie,
speed, and expedycion. Where Priscian interprets consulto and facto.i.
consuli et fieri. Ter en in Hecyra. In arcem transcurso opus est, It were
expedient to run and to make a step to the castle. Transcurso Donat
expouneth pro transcurrere. But in such speakynges as these, Volo datum,
factum oportuit, mansum oportuit. etc. is to be understand (says Linacre) the
infinitive mode, esse: So that hit be the preter tense of the infinitive mode
passive. For by them is vnderstonded not only the action or doing of a thing,
but also the perfection and end of a thing, already brought to pass and to a
fynall end or effect. etc.

Fortasse aliquanto iniquior fuit. Percase he was somewhat hard, streyghte, or
unreasonable.

Quem ferret, si parentem non ferret suu^? Whom should he have suffered or
forborne, if he should not have forborne his own father?

Hunccine erat aequum ex illius more, an illum ex huius uiuere? Was it mete
that this man should live, as he would have him, or else him to live, as
pleased this man?

Quod illum insimulat durum, id non est. Where as he accuses him, or lays to
his charge, that he is hard or streyte, that is not so. Insimulare is proprely
to lay to ones charge, a crime that is not true, but a forged matter.

Parentum iniuriae uniusmodi sunt ferme. The streytenes or hardenes of fathers
toward their children, is of one sort or after one rate for the most part.

Ea sunt ad uirtutem omnia. All tho things be to the furtherance of virtue.

Vbi animus semel se cupiditate deuinxit mala, necesse est consilia consequi
co^similia. When the mind has ones entangled and captiued it self with any
ylle desire or naughty appetite, hit can not be chosen but that semblable
counsayles and purposes must needs follow.

Hoc scitum est, periclum ex alijs facere, ti by quod ex usu siet. It is a
noble and goodly sentence or saying, every man to prove by others, what may
be most expedient and profitable for him self. Scitum. s. dictum.i. prae
clarum et doctrina plenum, quae confert ad uitam cu^ decoro inque officio
degendam. Periclum pro periculum, per syncopen. Siet pro sit, per epentesin.

Ibo hinc intro, ut uideam nobis quid coenae siet. I woll go hens in, that I
may see what we have to suppar.

Vide ne quo hinc abeas longius. See that thou goo not far out of the way.

Out of the second Act, in the first Scene.
Quam iniqui sunt patres in omnes adolescenteis iudices. How unequal judges be
fathers against all young men.

Aequum esse censent, nos iam a pueris ilico nasci senes. They think hit
reason that we even of little babes, should by and by become sage old men.

Ex sua libidine nos moderantur, quae nunc est, non quae olim fuit. They rule,
measure, and order us by their own wylfulle appetite, that they have now, and
not that they had many years gone, that is when them selfs were young men,
as we be now. Libi do libidinis is some time taken in bona^ partem, as Donat
notes. Sal. in Cat. Magisque in decoris armis et militaribus equis, quam in
scortis et conuiuijs libidine^ habebant, And they had pleasure and appetite in
goodly harnes and great horses for war, more tha^ in harlottes, and in
feastynge or bankettynge.

Mihi si unquam filius erit, nae ille facillime utatur patre. If ever I shall
have a son, in faith he shall have a very gentle father of me.

Et cognoscendi et ignoscendi dabitur peccati locus. He shall have time to
know his fault, and to have it pardoned or forgiven. Ignosco is sommetymes a
verb active and tra^sitiue, and governs an accusative and a datine, as
Forgive me this one faut, Ignoscas mihi hoc unum delictum. Of such verbes it
is noted before. Locus.i. tempus, spacium, sufficient time and space.

Mihi per alium ostendit suam sententiam. He declares me his own mind and
opinion under the name and color of an other person.

Adbibit plus paulo. He has dronken a litell to much, or, as (we use to say
in jesting) he has taken a pot of maius.

Sua narrat facinora. He tells of the prankes that he has played in his
days.

Periculum facito. Prove.

Nae ille haud scit quam mihi nunc surdo nar ret fabula^. In faith fulle
little wotteth he how deife I am, or how ill I can here now in this side, on
which he makes all this clatterynge unto me. Surdo narrare fabula^, to tell a
tale to a deife body, is a proverb to be said of them that labor in vain.
And it is the same that we use to speak prouerbially, when we here a thing that
lyketh us not, saying thus. I can not here in that side: which may be said
proprely in latin, Surdo narras fabulam, or, Surdo canis. Verg. No^ canimus
surdis, respo^dent omnia syluae.

Magis nunc me amicae dicta stimulant, da mihi, atque affer mihi. Now at this
time the sayings of my best beloue, go nere my stomach, give me this, and
bring me that. etc.

Quid respondeam nihil habeo. I have nothing in the world, what to make
answer. Quid, pro quod, as quid magnum, for aliquod magnum, or aliquid magni.
And all be it it appear contrary to the nature of quid, (taken and used most
commenly for a substantive, and gouernynge a genitive case after him) yet it is
an elegant manner of speaking, and much used in probate authors, as well
joined with adjectives put substantiuely, as also with substantives, and
agreinge with the same in case, gender, and number. Cato de liberis educandis.
Si quid his datum sit esculentum, If any thing be given them to eat. Cic. l.
2. epi. fam. Grave est homini pudenti petere aliquid magnum ab eo de quo se
benemeritum putet, ne id quod petat, exigere magis, quam rogare, et in mercedis
potius quam beneficij loco nume rare uideatur, It is a greatte pain or grefe
to a man that has any shame in him, to desire any great thing of him, unto
whom he thinks him self to have done and pleasure before, leest that he may
seem that thing which he desires, rather to exact and to require as due,
than to desire, and rather to reckon or account the same in place of a reward
or wages before deserved, than in place of a benefit. And so in other examples
innumerable, which for breuitee I omytte.

Neque me quisquam est miserior. Nor there is any manne living more miserable,
or in wors case, than I am.

Suarum rerum satagit. He has enough to do of his own, or, he has a busye
piece of work of his own to do. Satago, satagis, sataegi, satascum is to
have busynes or matters enough to do. And it is somtymes construed with a
genitive, as here, and somtymes it is put absolutely without any case joined
with it. And sometimes it signifies diligentem esse, seu festinare, To be
diligente or to make busy speed and haste.

Mea amica est potens, procax, magnifica, sumptuosa, nobilis. My best beloue
is a woman of good ability, and she is ever crauynge, magnificent or ladylike,
chargeable or costly, and a greatte gentyl woman. Procax, procacis,.i. petax,
ever askyng and crauynge, formed of proco, procas, which is poscere to desire,
and thereof wowers are called in latyne proci, as (who should say) poscentes
uxorem, desirynge and demaundyng or askyng the wise that they wowe.

Mihi religio est dicere. I dare not say it, or, I have a conscience to speak
hit. Religio.i. metus, Fear, and (as we say) scrupulo sitie of conscience, per
metaphoram. For religio proprely is the true serving and worshyppynge of god,
or of holy things, ex Cice, Read Thesaur. ling. lat.

Hoc ego mali non pridem inueni, neque etiamdum scit pater. It is not long
sith I have perceived this displesure or incommoditie, nor my father does not
yet know hit, inueni.i. intellexi, sensi.

In the second Scene.
Si mihi secundae res essent. If I were welthie, or in prosperity, or, if my
fortune were good.

Vereor ne me absente mulier corrupta sit. I fear leest that the woman has
been perverted or made naught, while I have been away.

Concurrunt multae opiniones, quae mihi animum exaugeant. There come many
opinions to gether, that cause my mind much the more to think. Exaugere is
to increase a thing, and to make it much more then it was, and thereof
exaugere animum is to increase the opinion of the mind, and to cause the mind
much the more to bow and to enclyne to that opinion that hit was in before,
per metaphoram.

Sub imperio cuius est? Under whose rule or ordering is he?

Illi nihil praeter precium dulce est. He thinks nothing sweet but money,
or there is nothing sweet unto him or in his opinion but money.

Hei misero mihi. Alas that ever I was borne. The interiections heu, and proh
govern a nominative case, as Heu pietas, heu prisca fides, proh Iuppiter, and
an accusative as Heu me miserum, proh deum atque hominum fidem. Hei gouernethe
evermore a datyue.

Etiam caves, ne uideat forte te a patre aliquis exiens? Wolte thou beware yet
again leest perchance some body coming forth from thy fathers espy the?

Nescio quid mihi animus praesagit mali. I can not tell how, my mind
gives me, that al is not well, or that I shall have sum ylle chance, or I
can not tell how my heart grudgeth again some ylle to comme. Praesagio,
sagis, praesagiui, is to perceive a thing, that is to comme, before it come.
Of prae, quod est, ante, before, et sagire, hoc est acute sentire, to be quick
of perceiving and smelling, and thereof dogs ar called sagaces, quycke of
smelling or sentynge.

Pergin' isthuc prius diiudicare, quam scis quid ueri siet? Do thou yet
still judge the matter, before thou know what the truth is?

Si nihil mali esset, iam hic adesset. If al had been well, he would have been
here ere now.

Non cogitas hinc longius abesse? Do thou not consider that it is a great
way he^s?

Nosti mores mulierum, dum moliuntur, dum comuntur, annus est. Thou knoweste
the guyse of women, while they set forward, and while they attire, pyke, and
trymme them selfs, it is a holle year, molior, moliris, molitus sum, is to
force a body self to do any thing. Como, mis compsi, comptum, est ornare, to
make gaye or to attire, or to deck the busshe: for in the trimmynge of the
hear consists a great part of the beauty of man or woman.

Respira. Take a good heart.

Eccum Dromonem cum Syro una adsunt tibi. Lo yond Dromo and Syrus, they are
both here together. Tibi est datiuus festiuitatis gratia adiunctus. Lau. Valla
has observed that ecce and en signify one thing, and be construed
indifferently with a nominative case or with an accusative. Al be it he
protestith, that he has not read at leest wise in any orator, ye and few
times in any poet, ecce, gouernynge an accusative, eccum, eccam, eccos, eccas,
ellum, ellam, ellos, ellas, the same Valla expou^dith not by pronownes
(contrary to Priscian and others) saying eccum.i. ecce eum, or ecce hunc, nor
ellum,.i. ecce illum, nor eccam.i. ecce eam, eccos.i. ecce eos, ellam.i. ecce
illa^, ellos.i. ecce illos, but by aduerbes thus, eccu^.i. ecce hic. s. eum.
etc. ellum.i. ecce illic eum. etc. illic. s. eu^. etc. and eccam.i. ecce hic.
s. eam. etc. eccos.i. ecce hic. s. eos. etc. ellam.i. ecce illic. s. eam. etc.
ellos.i. ecce illic. s. eos. etc. And therefore they be joined with an
accusative of the thing that we woll demonstrate or show. Terentius in Eun.
Eccum Parmenonem, eccum me.i. ecce hic Parmenonem, ecce hic me. And so here
Eccum Dromonem.i. ecce hic Dromonem. etc. So ellum Parmenonem.i. ecce illic
Parmenonem, uel potius ecce illic Parmeno secundum Vallam.

In the third Scene.
Ain tu? Say thou so in deed?

Sic est. It is even so.

Interea dum sermoues cedimus, illi sunt relicti. While we have kept and
prolonged communication they be left a great way behynd. Cedimus sermones.i.
miscemus, ex Nonio.

Minime miru^, adeo impediti sunt ancillaru^ grege^ ducunt secu^. It is no
meruaylle that they have been so long in coming, they be so pestred, for
they bring a holle flock with them. Minime mirum. s. est illos tam tarde
aduenisse.

Men' rogas? Do thou ask of me?

Non oportuit relictos, portant quid reru^. It was not well done to leave them
behind, for they bear sum things of charge about them.

Portant aurum, uestem, et uesperascit, and non nouerunt uiam. They have about
them both gold, and apparel, and it is almost dark night, and they know
not the way.

Factum a nobis stulte est. It was foolyshly or unwisely done of us.

Abidum illis obuiam propere. Goo and mete them apace. Abidum, abi, parelcon
est, ut quisnam, pro quis.

Vae misero mihi, quanta de spe decidi. Woe is me unfortunate body that I am,
out of how great hope am I fallen or brought.

Quae res te solicitat? What thing troubles your mind? Solicitare here
betokens inquietare, cura^ inijcere, to disquiet and to make pensife. So in
And. Cur ego meam senectutem huius solicito amentia? Why do I disquiete and
vex mine old age for his folly? Plaut. in Aul. Quia isthuc facinus quod tuu^
solicitat animum, id ego feci et fateor, For this deed that troubles and
vexes thy mind I did my self, and I confess it. Sometime solicitare to show
as well hope as fere. Plin. li. 1. epist. Nulla spe, nullo timore solicitor,
nullius rumoribus inquietor, I am not moved neither with any hope, nor with any
fere, nor I am not disquieted with the rumors or new tales of any man. Ter.
in An. Mis. Orare iussit, si se ames, hera, iam ut ad sese uenias, Videre ait
te cupere. Pamph. Vah perij, hoc malu^ integrascit. Siccine me, atque illam
opera tua nunc miseros solicitarier? Mis. My dame bead me prey you (if you love
her) to come to her by and by, for she says that she would very feign see
you. Pamph. Alas woe is me, this mischief is every day renewed worse and worse:
is it well done, that both I and she unhappy bodies, be thus brouzt in hope and
fere eueri day by the menes of the? Sometime it is to provoke or to lie
instantly upon and to entice one to any thing. Curtius lib. 2. Verumenimuero
cum modo milites meos literis ad proditionem, modo amicos ad perniciem pecunia
solicitet, ad internetionem mihi persequendus est, But yet for as much as he
does egg and entice somwhyle my souldiars by letters to betray me, and
somewhyle my friends with money and rewards to slay me, I must needs pursue
him to death. And solicito is to be written with one single. l. except in
poets which double the same l, per epenthesin metri causa, for it is fourmed
a solo. For what other thing is solicitare then solo citare.i. loco suo
mouere? to move or stere a thing out of the ground that it stood on, and out
of his place. For that solum does signify locum. Who doubts sins that the
etimologie of the latin word exules is, quod dicantur patriae suae solo
pulsi? And thereof solicitare is to turn up the ground in tyllynge or
ploughynge. Tibul. Et teneram ferro sollicitauit humum. And because that solum
is said also of the sea: Therefore solicitare is sometime taken for nauigare.
Claud. Et rudibus remis sollicitauit aquas. For solum is every thing that
bears up and stays any thing, as the water is solum to the ships and to
the fyshes. Verg. Subtrahiturque solu^. Et Ovid. Omne solum forti patria est,
ut piscibus aequor: Et uolucri uacuo quicquid in orb pater. In which verse
Ouide calls the air solum to the birds, and the sky is solum to the
sterres. Ovid* Astra tenent caeleste solum. etc.

Rogitas quid siet? Do thou ask what it is? or, what is the matter quod the?

Nunc demum intelligo. I perceive it now at last, and so I did not before.
Demum et denique idem significant quod tandem.i. post longam moram: after long
taryenge, as in example. When thou haste well and perfitely learned all things,
than at last and not before thou may teach others, Cum omnia perdidiceris,
tum demum, or denique, or tandem docere alios potes. Secondarilye demum is
taken for omnino uel solum, specially when it is joined with these pronownes
hic, iste, ille, is: or with these aduerbes ita or sic, so that demum
conteyneth and imports a certain exception of an other thing thus. Ea demum
est gloriosa laudatio, quae a laudatis uiris proficiscitur, That preyse and
none else is to a man's honor and good name, which comes and ryseth of honest
men and preyse worthy. Ita demum me abs te amari sentiam, si huic homini mea
caussa co^modaueris, I shall by this means and none other, perceive and think
that you love me heartily well, if you do pleasure unto this man for my sake. Tum
demu^ te amicum putabo, quum beneuolentiam erga me tuam re expertus fuero, I
woll then and never before think the my friend, when I have in deed had a
proffe of thy good will and hearty love toward me. etc.

Dij boni quid turbae est? Good lord what a rabble, or train, or company is
there of them? and sometimes it may be englyshed thus. Good lord what array,
or trouble, or business, or a do is there?

Aedes nostrae eos uix capient, scio. Our house will scasely hold them all, I
know well. or, our house will uneath be able to receive them all, I am very
sure.

Quid comedent? quid ebibent?.i. quantu^. How much woll they eat and drink?
or what meat and drink woll they spend?

Eccos quos uolebam. Lo here them that I would have. How eccos is to be
expouned, it is showed a little before.

O Iuppiter ubinam est fides? O Iuppiter, where is honesty or sure and
faithful kepinge of promyses becomme? For fides (as says Cicer. li. i. off.)
est dictorum conuentoru^que constantia et ueritas, ex quo credamus, quia fiat
quod dictum est, appellatam fidem, Fides is the substanciall keeping and the
true and sure agreinge of a man's sayenges and couenantes, and the performance
of the same. And therefore let us believe and think verily this to be the very
true etimologie, that is to say, the very true reason, diffinition, and
