interpretation of the word fides, quia fiat puod dictum est, that such
things be done in deed that are promised in word, and when the deeds are
answerable and agreing unto the words and sayings or promyses.

Tu interea loci collocupletasti te. Thou haste enryched thy self in the mean
season, or thou haste gotten greatte goods in the mean time.

Me in his deseruisti malis. Thou haste forsaken me in this adversity or
distress.

Propter quem in summa infamia sum, et meo patri minus sum obseque^s. For whose
cause I am in very great infamy, obloquie, or sklander, and am not ruled by my
father, as I should be, or do not follow my fathers mind as I ought to do.

Nemo est miserior me. There is no man more miserable or in wors case then I.

Hic de uerbis nostris errat, quae hic sumus locuti. This man is deceived by
reason of our words, that we have spoken here.

Aliter amorem tuum atque est accipis. You take your love otherwise then it is.
We say elega^tly aliter atque, aliter ac, or aliter quam, and aliter nisi,
when a negacion goes before. See examples in Hadrian.

Animus erga te idem est ac fuit. My mind toward you is the same that it has
been.

Quantum ex ipsa re coniecturam fecimus. As far as we could coniecte by or
of the thing self.

Nihil rerum omnium est, quod malim, quam me hoc falso suspicarier. There is
nothing in the world that I would be more glad of, then that I did suspecte,
surmyse, and mystrust this thing falsely or untruly.

Anus, quae est dicta matter esse ei ante hac, non fuit. The old womanne that
hitherto has been called his mother, was not his mother.

Ea obijt mortem. She is deed.

Hoc ipse in itinere alteri dum narrat, forte audiui. This did I here by
chance, as him self told it unto an other body by the way.

Mane, hoc quod coepi primum enarrem, post isthuc ueniam. Abide awhyle, let
me first of all tell forth this that I have begone, and than I will comme
to this afterward.

Vbi uentum ad aedeis est, Dromo pultat fores. Whenne we came to the house,
Dromo knocked at the door. Pulto, pultas is the same that pulso, pulsas, to
knock, or to beat, or to rappe: and it is formed of the supine pultum, which
supine the old writers did use, but now it is out of use.

Anus quaedam prodit. Forth comes a certain old woman.

Haec ubi aperuit ostium, continuo hic se co^iecit intro, ego consequor. As
soon as she had opened the door, he here whypte me in by and by, and I after
hard at the tail of him.

Foribus obdit pessulu^. He bolted the door.

Hinc sciri potuit, aut nus quam alibi, quo studio uitam suam te absente
exegerit. By this way, or else by no way at all it might be known how busily
or in what occupation he has passed and led his life while you have been
absent from home. Studio.i. labor, opere. Exigere aetatem or tempus, and agere
aetatem, is to pass and to spend out the years or time. Sometime exigere, is
for as abigere. to drive out of the doors. Plaut. Exegit omnes for as* He drove
every body out of the doors.

De improuiso est interuentum mulieri. We came upon the woman sodeinly or
unware.

Ea res dedit existimandi copiam cotidiane uitae consuetudinem. By that thing
we might eathely judge the daily conversation of his living.

Cotidianae uitae consuetudo cuiusque ingenium, ut sit, declarat maxume. The
daily conversation of living does very eathly and best of all things
show what the disposition of every body is. Maxume.i. facillime, optime, and
there is the figure antithesis, that is when one letter is put for an other. As
scribundis prologis, for scribendis, animum aduortite, for aduertite, so here
maxume for maxim.

Texentem telam studiose ipsam offendimus. We found her at the loome weauynge
full busily. Offendo, dis, offendi, offensum, is sometimes the same that
reperio, to find by chance. Of which verbes read Laur. Vall. li. 5. eleg.
ca. 2.

Offendimus eam mediocriter uestitam, ueste lugubri. We found her meanly or
coursly apparelled in a mourning gown.

Obsecto ne me in laeticia^ frustra conijcias. I pray the do not cast me in to
greatte gladness in vain.

Si haec sunt uera, ita uti credo, quis te est fortunatior? If these things be
true, as I thynk they be, what man is more fortunate, or who has better
fortune then thou?

Magnum hoc signu^ est eu^ esse extra noxa^. This is a great sign that he is
nothing culpable, or faulty. Noxa secundu^ festu^ Pompeium and Valla^ idem est
quod culpa. And thereof comith noxae dedere in the civil law,.i. poenae dare
seu tradere ob noxa^.i. culpa^, to deliver to be punyshed for some fault,
offense, or trespass done. As if a man's servant or bondma^ had done any trespass
against any person, the party pleintife took an action against the master of
such servant or bondsman, and such manner actions are callid in the civil law,
Noxales actiones, That if the master of such servant or bondsman (as aforesaid)
would not meintene him in the said trespass or offense done, he would and lefully
might yield his said servant or bondsman to suffer bodyly punyshement
according to the order of the law, ye and though it were to death, and that
was called noxae dedere. Extra noxam esse, ide^ est quod extra culpam esse, hoc
est, noxa aut culpa uacare, Not to be in fault, but to be gyltlesse or
blameless. Noxa is sommetymes taken for dampnum, hurt or harm. Ovid. li. 15.
Meth. speaking of the waters of a certain lake in the countreye of Arcadia,
which was called lacus phineus, Nocte nocent potae, sine noxa luce by buntur.
If one drink of them in the night they woll hurt him, but in the day time a
man may drink of them without any harm at all. Suet. in Iul. Caesar. ca. 81.
Spurinamque irridens, et ut falsum arguens, quod sine ulla sua noxa Idus
Martiae adessent, quanquam is uenisse quidem eas diceret, sed non praeternsse.
And mocking or scornynge Spurinam, and reprouyng him as foul dectiued, in
that the Ides of the month of March were comme without any his harm, not
withstanding that the said Spurina stylle said and affirmed, that the said
Ides of March in deed were now come, but that they were not yet paste nor
gone. For Spurina being a southsayer had warned Caesar before to beware of
the Ides of March, for he should be slain as that day, and so he was.

Disciplina est, eis them munerarier ancillas primum* ad dominas qui affectant
uiam. This is as a school, or this is the facion, manner, order, or train that
must be used, that is, what so ever person desires to have his way made unto
the mastresses, must first reward or brybe the maiden servants with sembleable
things, as he would give unto the dames selfs. Disciplina, nae, is that that
any disciple or scholar takes of his master, as any facion or sort of
living, or of doing, any manners or other doctrine. And thereof it is called a
schole, as in example. Christ did forbydde that we should put away violence
by violence, and bead us to do good for ylle, but now we have learned an other
schole, Christus uim ui repellere* phibuit, and bona pro malis reddere
praecepit, at nunc alia est disciplina. So disciplina militaris. is the facion
that young souldiars ar before exercysed in, and trained after, to be made
perfect good men of war. Disciplina gladiatoria, is the precepts and way of
traynynge men in the weapens, and the schools that masters of fense keep.
Disciplina scholastica, is the doctrine that scholars be trained in by their
masters. And thereof among the Philosophiers, the diverse sects are called
disciplinae, as disciplina Stoicorum, is the sect of the Stoiques, Disciplna
peripateticorum, is the sect of the Peripatesiques, and so of others, which
had every of them a diverse facion or train from an other. So disciplina
meretricia, is the school and doctrine that the light houswyues and
strumpettes have among them, and teach it to their disciples. Nam ea quoque,
si dijs placet, artis iam nomen obtinuit. Muneror, aris, has here the nature
and signification of a verb deponent. Plaut. and Macrobius used munero, ras,
neutrali significatione. For there be certain verbes, which be neutres in o,
and deponentes in or, under one signification, as adsentio, adsensi, and
adsentior, tiris, adsensus sum, to assente or to grant. Impertio, impertiui,
and impertior, tiris, impertitus sum, to give part. Comperio, and comperior,
comperi (for the preterperfect, compertus sum, the latin me^ have not said in
the actif signification) to have sure knowledge. Iurgo, as, and iurgor, aris, to
chyde. Fabrico, cas, in poets, and fabricor, caris, in orators, to imagine.
Populo, las, and populor, to distroye. Adulo, as, and adulor, aris, to flatter.
Luxurio, as, and luxurior, aris, to be rank, or to be riottous. Lachrymo, and
lachrymor to weep with others mo, which Nonius Marcellus rekeneth up.

Perge obsecro te. Goo forth in your tale I beseech you.

Cave ne falsam gratiam studeas inire. Beware that thou desire not to pyke or
to have a thank of me undeserved, or beware that thou goo not about to make
me thank the for nothing.

Quid ait, ubi me nominas? What would he say, when thou dyddest name me?

Dicimus redisse te, et rogare uti ueniret ad te. We told him that you were com
home agein, and that you desired him to com unto you.

Mulier lachrymis opplet os totu^ sibi. The woman all to washed all her face
with blabrynge and weeping

Prae gaudio, ita me dij ament, ubi sim nescio, ita timui. As god help me, I
can not tell where I am for joy, I was so aferd before.

At ego nihil esse sciebam. But I knew that there was no cause why at all.

Adducimus tuam Bacchidem. We bring here with us your sovereign lady Bacchis.

O hominis impudente^ audaciam. Oh the shameless boldness that is in this fellow.

Non fit sine periculo facinus magnum et memorabile. A great act and worthy
memory is never done without peril. Hic et hec memorabilis, et hoc memorabile,
A thing worthy to be had in memory. Plaut. in capt. At erit mihi hoc factum
mortuo memorabile, But this act shall be had in memory, when that I am deed
and rotten. Colu. in pref. Cu^ tot alios Romani generis intuear memo rabiles
duces. When I behold so many noble capitaynes and worthy memory of the blood of
the Romans. Cic. de amicitia. Cum accepissemus a patribus maxim memorabilem.
C. Laelij, et P. Scipionis familiaritatem, When we had herd of our fathers and
ancestors the familiarity of C. Laelius, and of P. Scipio most worthy
memory. etc. Read in Chiliad. Erasm. the proverb Difficilia quae pulchra, Unto
the which this sentence or clause of Terence alludeth.

Hoc uide, in mea uita tu tibi laude^ is quaesitum scelus. See I pray you,
thou go about to get unto thy self preyse, glory, or honor, by the
hasarde and danger of my life, thou naughty fellow. Is quaesitum.i. quaeris.
Hoc uide, is a manner of latin speaking much used in Terence and Plaut.
signifyenge the same that we say in english in indignation or anger, see I
pray you, as before in Eunucho. Illud uide, os ut sibi distor sit carnifex, See
I prey you, how the villein makes a wrye mouth. Id. in Adelp. Illuc uide, ut
in ipso articulo oppressit, Se I prey you, how he has come upon me, and taken
me sodaynely even at the very point of myschiefe. etc.

Si paululum modo quid te fugerit, ego perierim. If thou be ouerseen in any
thing be it never so little, I am utterly undone, or I may give my life for
an halfepeny. Of paululum quid, or paululum aliquid, pro paululum aliquod, with
others like it is showed largely before.

Si sinas, dicam. If you would let me alone, I would tell you.

Quas, malum, ambages mihi narrare occipit? What the dyuell, going about the
busshe begins he to use in telllynge his tale? Malum is sometimes an
interiection, or after Donate an aduerbe of anger and indignation, and is
swetely brought in (says Donate.) Cic. l. 2. off. Praeclare in epistola
quadam Alexandrum filium Philippus accusat, quod largitione beneuolentiam
Macedonu^ consectetur. Quae te, malum (inquit) ratio in istam spem induxit ut
eos tibi fideles putares fore, quos pecunia corrupisses? Philippus king of the
Macedonians did very nobly rebuke and blame his son Alexander, in a certain
epistle, that he wrote unto him, where he said thus? What (the devil) occasion
or consideration has brought the into this hope, that thou should think
that they would be, and continue faithfulle unto the, whom thou had
corrupt with money?

Ad rem redi. Return to the purpose or pythe of the matter.

Enimuero reticere nequeo. Truly I can not but speak.

Multis modis iniurius Clitipho est, neque ferri potis est. Clitipho is
unreasonable many ways, nor any man may suffer or abide his unreasonable
facion.

Audiendum hercle est tace. In faith you must needs here, or, you may not
chose but here, hold your peace.

Vis potiri, tuum esse in potiundo periclum non uis. You would feign have your
desire and pleasure, but you wol abide none adventure in labourynge for to
obtain it. We say potior, poteris, uel potiris, potitus sum, and in latyne
speaking it governs evermore the genitive plurell of this nowne res, when it
is set alone, and not joined with an adjective, and then it signifies to
bear a reule and to have in our dominion, or to govern the empire, as
Augusto rerum potito, totus quieuit orbis, When Augustus had or ruled the
empire, all the world was at rest and piece. And in that signification, that
is to sey, betokenynge to surmounte or to have in our dominion and under our
subjection, potior is read and joined also with certain other genitiues
besides rerum. Plaut. in Capt. Na^ postquam meus rex est potitus hostium, After
that the king my master had surmounted his enemies, and gotten the over hand
of them. Id. in Epidico. Atque hostium est potita, And she has subdued or
conquered her enemies. Sal. Cui fatum foret urbis potiri, Whose desteny it was
to win or to obtain the city, and to be lord of it. But of all other
casual words, ye and also of the said nowne, res, being joined with any
other adiectife then such as may appertain to the signification above noted,
Potior, governs an ablative case, and then it is to obtain or to get any
thing by labourynge, as potitus sum uictoria, I have gotten the victory. etc.
Potior, is also read gouernyng an accusative, as in old time it was used.
Plaut in Asinar. Fortiter malum qui patitur, idem post potitur bonum, He that
suffers, and manfully endures the sour, shall afterward have the sweet, or
he that does stoutly abide the pain for any thing, shall afterward have the
pleasure of the same. Ter. in Adel. Ille alter sine labor patria potitur
co^moda, That mine other brother Mitio with out any his pain or labor has all
pleasures and commodities belongyng to a father, or that a father should have.
Ibidem. Miseriam omne^ ego capio, hic potitur gaudia, I abide all the care,
sorrow, and myserie, and he has all the joy or pleasure. Cic. in Philipp.
Itaque si receptis illis possumus esse liberi, uincamus odium, pacemque
potiamur, Therefore if we may be free, and out of all subjection, when we have
received tho things, than let us overcome hatered or enmitee, and let us
have peace. Potior, is fourmed of potis, and therefore (says Valla) they be
both very like in signification. For we say in latyne Sum co^pos mentis, compos
animi, co^pos rationis, co^pos sanitatis, wha^ I have the things here
rehearsed. Also we say in latin sum compos uoti, co^pos uictoriae, compos
optati, When I have by my labor and industry gotten or obtained the said
things. Impos in signification is clean contrary unto compos.

Haud stulte sapis. You are no small fool. Aut haec cum illis sunt habenda,
aut illa cu^ his amittenda. Either thou must be contented to have these
things to gether with the other, or else to leave and let go the other
things together with these.

Harum duarum conditionum nunc utram malis uide. Now see whether of these two
conditions thou woldeste rather or leefer have.

Consilium quod coepi rectum esse et tutum scio. I know that the counsel or
deuyse that I have begone to take is good, and nothing dangerous.

Quod illi es pollicitus, eadem hac inueniam uia. That that thou haste promised
unto him, I woll find out and get by this self same way or mean.

Quod ut efficerem, orando surdas iam aures reddideras mihi. Which thing that
I would bring to pass for the, thou had all ready long before made mine
ears dulle and deyfe, with much desyring and praying.

Quid aliud tibi uis? What would you else have? or what would you more have?

Experiundo scies. By assayeng or prouyng thou shall know. Experiundo pro
experiendo per antithesin.

Age age, caedo isthuc tuum consilium, quid id est? Go to, come on, tell us
that thy deuyse, what is it?

Longum est, si tibi narrem, quamobrem id faciam. It would be a long tale to
tell, if I should show you, wherefore I wolle do hit.

Nihil satis firmi uideo, quamobrem accipere hunc mihi expediat metum. I se no
substanciall ^^^^ necesse. The matter is now come to that point, that I may
not chose but do it.

Merito te amo. My love is well bestowed on you.

Perdocta est probe. She can her lesson well enough, or, she is taught her
lesson in the best wise I warant you.

Hoc demiror, qui tam facile potueris persuadere illi. This I meruaylle
greattely at, how thou could so sone persuade him or bring him in mind.

In tempore ad eum ueni, quod reru^ omnium est primu^. I came unto him in seson,
which thing is the chief and principal of all things. Read the proverb
Nosce tempus, in chiliad. Erasm.

Art tractabat uirum.i. hominem. He handled the fellow craftily or subtly.

Vt esset apud te ob hoc quam gratissimus. That he might be in very good favor
with the for that same.

Sed heus tu, uide sis ne quid imprudens ruas. But serra, see that you play no
whylde touch ere you be ware, or forgetting your self. Vide sis, the
particle sis is as much as si uis, and is put for hit often times, as sodes
for si audes, or sultis for si uultis. And it is much used for an aduerbe of
exhorting, or else otherwhyles for a voice expletiue, so that it be
parelcon. Plaut. in Amph. Iam sequere sis, herum qui ludificas dictis
delyrantibus, Come after me at ones, thou which mockest me being thy master
with thy folish sayings. It is used sometime also in orators. Cic pro sexto
Roscio. Age nu^c refer animum sis, ad ueritatem, Well, go to now, return thy
mind to the very pleyn truth of the matter. imprude^s.i. incogita^s unware,
vnauised for fault of remembrance, and consideration. Ruas.i. immodeste a gas,
geras ue quasi temerarius.

Patrem nouisti, ad has res quam sit perspicax. Thou know thy father how
quick of sight he is in such things, and how sone he can epsy them.

Ego te noui, quam esse soleas impotens. I know the how wild thou art wont
to be, and unable to rule thy self. Impotens is he that can not master, rule,
refreine, nor measure the affections, passions, or desires of his mind, but
passes reason, and keeps no mesure or mean, whether it be in anger, joy,
sorrow, pleasure, orels what. Ter. in An. Adeo impotenti esse animo, ut praeter
ciuium morem atque legem, and sui uoluntatem patris, tamen hanc habete, studeat
cum summo probro? Should he be so far out of reason, beyond him self, or so
outrageous, that he should labor and goo a boute to have her to his wife,
ageynste the usage and custom of al honest men in the city, and against the
law, and contrary to the mind or will of his own father?

Inuersa uerba, euersas ceruices tuas, gemitus screatus, tusseis, risus,
abstine. Thy stumblynge or tryppynge in thy words, speking one thing for an
other, thy stretching or puttyng forth of thy neck, thy syghyng, spyttyng,
cowghyng, and laughyng or gyggelyng, forbere them. Inuerto, it is, inuerti,
inuersum, is to turn the contrary side outward, as of a furre, or of a
cappe, or of any other thing, and thereof inuertere uerba, est praepostere
aliquid efferre, to pronounce words, and bring them out, so that we speak
one thing for an other, as they use to do, whose tongues commenly speak that
thing, upon which their mind runs most.

Laudabis me. Thou shalt conne me good thank.

Quam cito nos consecutae sunt mulieres. How sone the women have overtaken us.

Nihilo magis. Nere a deal the rather.

Abeas si sapis. Get the he^s if thou wise be.

O hominem felicem. O happy man, that ever he was borne.

In the fourth Scene.
Edepol te laudo, et fortunatum iudico, cum studuisti, isti formae mores ut
consimiles forent. Trewely I commend the and think the fortunate or happy,
that thou haste so endeavored thy self, that thy conditions and behauour
might be according or answerable unto thy beauty.

Minime miror, si te sibi quisque expetit. I marvel nothing at all, that
every manne desires greatly to have you.

Mihi, quale ingenium haberes, indicio fuit oration. Your communication well
notified unto me, of what disposition you were.

Cum mecum in animo uitam tuam consydero. When I consider thy manner of living
in my mind.

Et uos esse istiusmodi, and nos non esse, haud mirabile est. That you be such
as you are, and we not, it is no marvel at all.

Nobis prospectum est. We be provided for.

Deserti uiuimus. We live as folks al forsaken.

Hoc beneficio utrique ab utrisque deuincimini, ut nun quam ulla amori uestro
incidere possit calamitas. By the good help of this thing you be in such wise
bounden either to other, that no mysse hap may at any time chance in your
love or frendeshyp, as who should say, that there can no mysfortune befalle
able to break and to undo the love that is between you. Laur. Vall. very well
shows, that Cicero nor Quintilian never used to say neuter neutri, and that
it is no latin speaking, but neuter alteri, nor uterque utrique, but uterque
alteri, as Cic. in prefatione officiorum. Eodemque modo de Aristotele and
Isocrate iudico, quoru^ uterque suo studio delectatus co^tempsit alteru^, And
the self same wise I judge of Aristotle and Isocrates, of whom either delityng
and taking singlar pleasure and felicity in his own study (that is to sey,
Aristotle in philosophy, and Isocrates in Rhetorike) despised the other.
Quintilan. Cum uterque alteri obijciat, palam est utrumque fecisse, Sins that
either of them lays it to the others charge, it is manifest and open, that
both the one and the other of them did it. Yet never the less Terence in
Phormione says. Quia uterque utrique est cordi, Because that both like either
other very well. And also in the tenthe book of the commentaries of the gestes
of Caesar (which book it is doubted whether Hircius or Oppius did write.)
Interim dissensione orta inter Achillem et Arsinoen, cum uterque utrique
insidiaretur, et summa^ imperij ipse obtinere uellet. etc. In the mean season
dissension, strife, and debate arysynge between Achilles and Arsinoen, when
either of them laid wait to distroye the other, and desired to get and have
the chief rule of the hole Empire him self. etc.

Nescio alios, me scio semper fecisse sedulo, ut ex illius commodo meum
compararem commodum. I know not other men, but my self I know, that I have
right diligently endeavored and applied my self to get and to esteem my
commodity, by his commodity, or in such wise that it might always stand
to gether with his commodytie.

Omnes mihi labores fuere, quos caepi, leaves. All the labors that I took, me
thought but light.

Vt patrem tuum uidi esse habitu^ diu, etiam duras dabit. As far as I have
seen the facion of thy father, or, as I have seen thy father disposed this
long while, he wolle yet deal hardly, or roughly with the. Duras. s. parts,
as before in Eunucho. Duras fratris parts praedicas, My brother is in hard
case by thy saying. Duras alicui parts dare, is to handle a body hardly: And
it is proprely said in latin. Quisnam hic est, qui intuetur nos? What fellow
is this same, that beholds us, or looks on us?

Amabo quid tibi est? I pray you, what eyleth you. Esi.i. accidit.

Quid stupes? Why art thou astonyshed?

Video ne Cliniam, an non? Have I espied, or do I se Clinia or not?

Quem uides? Whom see thou? or whom have thou espied?

Salve anime mi, ut uales? God save you mine own sweet heart, how fare you?

Saluum te uenisse gaudeo. I am right glad and joyous that you be come home
safe and in good health.

Teneo ne te, maxim animo, exoptate meo? O whom my heart does most desire,
have I, or do I hold you in mine arms? (as who should say) am I sure that I
touch you, and that you be here?

Ite intro, nam uos iamdudum expectat senex. Go ye in, for the old manne
looks for you, or tarieth on you, and has done a good while. Of dudum,
pridem, nuper, iamdudum, and iampridem, and the use of them in latin
speaking, it is showed before.

Out of the third Act, in the first Scene.
Lucescit. It dayeth, or it is almost broad day.

Cesso pulsare ostium. I am slack in knocking at the door.

Adolescentem hoc nolle intelligo. I perceive that the young manne would not
have this done, or, I perceive that this is against or contrary to the young
man's mind.

Cum uideam miserum hunc tam excruciarier eius abitu. For as much as I see
this poor soul to be in such great sorrow and heaviness or pain, because of
the going away of him.

Coelem ta^ inspaerata^ gaudiu^? Should I keep priuey or hyde from him this joy
so greatly rvnloked for? Caele^. s. eum, for caelo is one of the^ that
governs a double accusative case, albe it we may say in latin, Celauit me
hanc rem, or, Celauit me de hac re, or, Celauit hanc rem, Ye and Terentius in
Phormione joined a dative case with caelo, saying: Si hoc caeletur patri, in
metu sum, If this be kept or hydden from my father, I am in fear. And
Aemylius probus joined coelo with the same case, saying: Id Alcibiadi caelari
diutius non potuit, That thing might no longer be hydden from Alcibiades.

Haud faciam. I woll not do it, or I wolle not so do.

Quam potero adiutabo senem. I wolle help the old man as much as I shall be
able. Quam pro quantum.

Video filium meum amico, atque aequali suo inseruire, et so cium esse in
negotijs. I se that my son does gladly take pain for his friend and
companyon, and takes such part as he does in all his matters or business.
They be called in latyne aequales, which be of one age and time, and
especially which have been brought up to gether, as companyons and
playfeers. Inseruio, is, inseruiui, inseruitum, to do pleasure or service for a
man voluntarily or gladly.

Nos senes est aequum senibus obsequi. It is mete for us old folks to do
pleasure each for other, or reason would, that we old me^ help one an other.
Obsequi.i. inseruire.

Ego profecto egregie ad miseriam natus sum. Trewelye I am notablye borne to
myserye, wretchedness, mysfortune, or aduersytie.

Illud falsum est, quod uulgo audio dici, die^ adimere aegritudinem hominibus.
That is a false or untrue saying, which I here commenly spoken emonges men,
that long contynuaunce or process of time takes away care and thought from
men's hearts.

Nam mihi quidem cotidie augescit magis aegritudo. For unto me at leest wise my
sorrow care or grefe wexeth every day more and more. Augesco is not to begin
to wexe or to grow more, but stylle to be every day more and more. For verbes
in sco do not signifye beginning, nor should not be called inchoatiues (as
Priscianus and other grammarians would have them called) but rather
continuatiues, as the which betoken encreasement: as aegresco not to begin to
be sick* but to be sycker and syker. Vergi. l. 12. Aeneid. Haud quaquam
dictis, uiolentia Turniflectitur, exuperat magis, aegrescitque medendo, The
indignation or fiersnes of Turnus might in no wise be bowed, mitigated,
aswaged, or pacified with any the words or counsel of Latinus, but arose,
swelled and stylle wexed worse and worse, and the more phisike of good
counsel that Latinus bestowed on him, to pacify his said indignation or
fiersenesse, the more and more the same still wexed. Inualesco, to wax or to
grow stronger and stronger. Quintil. Tenuit con suetudo, quae cotidie
inualescit, A custom has been taken up and received, which wexeth stronger and
stronger every day. Lactant. Si nostra inualuerint, ut cotidie inualescunt.
etc. If our part get the better, as every day it waxes stronger and stronger.
Conualesco also to wexe stronger and stronger. Cic. Quanto plura ille miscebat,
tanto hic magis indies conualescebat, The mo medicines or drenches that that
other myngled, the stronger and stronger wexed this other. Crudesco is to wax
or to be rawer and rawer. Verg. Sin in processu caepit crudescere morbus. etc.
But if in the process of time the disease wexe rawer and rawer. etc. Sordesco is
to wexe more and more filthy. Consenesco to wexe older, and not to begin to
be old, and so of macesco, acesco, nigresco, albesco, tabesco, with other
verbes in sco: yet here it is to be noted, that many in sco have the
signyfication of their primytifes, and be used for them: as conticesco for
conticeo, adhaeresco for adhaereo, delitesco for deliteo, co^cupisco for
concupio, obdormisco for obdormio, with others mo, as shows Laur. Vall. l. 1.
eleg. c. 22.

Quanto diutius abest, tanto magis desydero. s. eum. The longer it is sith he
has been away, so much more am I desirous to see him.

Ipsum for as egressum uideo, adibo, alloquar. I see him self come forth, I
woll go unto him and speak to him.

Nuncium aporto tibi, cuius maxim te fieri participem cupis. I bring you that
tidinges, which you are most desirous to here of. Nuncius, cij, cio, in the
masculine gendre, signifies both the bringer of any message or tidings, and
also the message or tydyng^s that is brought. Plau. in Sticho. Praecucurri ut
nunciarem, nuncium exoptabilem: I ran to com before to show tidinges, which you
desire to here. Albe it some gra^marians say, that nunciu^, cij, cio, in the
neutre ge^dre, signifies the message or tidinges that is brought, which
vocable Laur. Vall. says that he never read ne found in no probate author.
And sometimes it is read nuncius, a, um, having the nature and place of an
adjective, as nuncia uerba. etc. There is also red haec nuncia, uunciae, in the
feminine gendre, for her that brings tidings.

Nunquidnam de gnato meo audisti? Have you hard any thing of my son?

Vbinam est, quaeso? Where is he I beseech you?

Est apud me domi. He is at home at my house.

Duc me ad illum obsecro. Bring me unto him I pray the for goddis sake.

Non uult te scire se redisse. He would not have you know, that he is comme
again.

Tuum conspectum fugitat propter peccatum. He absentethe or kepethe him self
out of your syghte for the fault that he has done.

Timet, ne tua duritia illa antiqua etiam adaucta sit. He fears leest the
same your old or wont hardness be now encresed or wexed more and worse then
ever it was.

Non tu ei dixisti, ut essem? Dyddeste not thou tell him how I was minded? Vt
essem.i. quomodo affectus essem, uel, quo animo essem, of what mind I was.

Pessime isthuc in te, atque in illum consulis. In this thing thou do very
ylle both for thy self, and for him, Or therein thou takeste a very ylle
way, both for thine own part and also for his. Consulis.i. statuis
decernis: and it is an elegant manner of speaking.

Te leui et uicto animo esse ostendis. Thou show thy self to be gentle or
tender hearted, and sone ouercomed.

Satis iam satis pater durus fui. I have been an hard, streyte, or heavy father
unto my son long enough now, There is understood diu.

Vehemens in utramque partem es nimis. Thou art to vehemente, to affectionate,
or to hot both in the one part and in the other.

In eandem fraudem ex hac re atque ex illa incides. Thou shalt fall in to one
and the same trappe both by this thing, and by that, or both by the one
thing and by the other. Of the sygnification of this nowne fraus it is showed
before.

Paululo tum erat contentus, ei quae erant grata omnia. He could then have
been contented with very little, and every thing was thankefully accepted or
welcome unto him.

Proterruisti hinc. s. illum. Thou have driven him away hens for fear.

Caepit uictum uulgo quaerere. He begone to get his living abroad, or here
and there.

Nunc quu^ sine magno detrimento non potest haberi, quiduis dare cupis. Now
that it can not be gotten or had without greatte loss and damage, thou
would fayn or with all thy heart give, thou careste not how much.

Vt scias quam ea instructa pulchre ad perniciem siet, for sit. That thou
may know how goodly she is appointed to undo, or to cast away any man, or
to bring a man to naught.

Ancillas secum adduxit plus december. She has brought with her at her tail ten
maidens and mo.

Satrapes si siet amator, nunquam sufferre eius sumptus queat. A greatte lord
if he were her lover might never be able to bear or to sustain the sumptuous
charges of her. Hic et haec Satrapes, huius Satrapae, or hic et haec Satrapa,
huius Satrapae is a vocable, which the latin men have taken of the greeks, And
the greeks out of the Persians, and it signifies a ruler, a captayn, or
leuetenaunt of any province, as the capteyne of Cales, or the capteyne of the
I Will of wight, or the leutenant of Irelande may be called in latyne Satrapes or
Satrapa. For satrapeia in greek is prouincia.

Ei unam caenam, atque eius comitibus dedi, quod si iterum mihi sit danda,
actu^ siet, for sit. I have given but only one suppar to him and to his train
or company, that if I should make one suppar more, I might be utterly undone by
it, or, it were inoughe utterly to undo me. Actum est is a prouerbial speking,
signifienge desperation of a thing, as being utterly paste cure and remedy.
Teren. in Andr. Actum est, siquide^ haec uera praedicat, The matter is utterly
paste cure and remedy, at leest wise if it be true that she here seythe. Read
in chil. Eras.

Vt alia omittam. To let pass all other things, or though I speak not of
other things. For Vt is here taken for quamuis or licet. Cic. in Oratore. Vt
quaeras omnia, quomodo Graeci ineptum appellent, non re peries, Though a man
seek through out all the vocables that be, yet he shall not find any vocable
that the greeks have countreuaylynge this latin word ineptus.i. inidoneus,
unapt or unmeet to any thing. Idem pro lege agraria. Vt circu^spiciamus
omnia, quae populo grata, atque incunda sunt, nil tam populare, quam pacem,
quam concordiam, quam ocium reperiemus, Though we consider al things that be
acceptable and pleasant unto the people, we shall find nothing so much to the
weithe or to the pay of the same, as peace, as concord, or unity, and as
living in ease and quiet.

Pitissando modo mihi quid uini absumpsit? What or how much wine has she
spente me by nothing but syppynge or tastynge?

Releui dolia omnia. I have set a broche all the vessels in my house.

Omnes sollicitos habui. I had al my meyny or householde as busye as they
could be to serve: Sollicitos.i. uarijs officijs et ministerijs distractos, ut
qui hinc et inde ad ministrandum destinabantur, et assignabantur.

Quid te futurum censes, quem assidue exedent? What shall become of the? or
what case shalt thou be in (think thou) whom they shall contynually eat
out of house and home?

Ita me dij amabunt, ut tuarum misertum est fortunarum. God love and help me,
as I have pity and compassion upon thy fortune or goods and substance. For
that signifies Fortunae, narum, in the plurel nombre, for the most part, and
that signification is most agreeable unto this place.

Faciat, quod libet. Let him do, what him lysteth.

Sumat, consumat, perdat, decretum est pati, dum illum modo habeam mecum. Let
him take, let him spend or waste, let him distroy and cast away what he
wolle, I have determined with my self to abide and endure it. so that I may
have him at home in my house and company. Dum illum modo, for dummodo illum.
etc. by the figure that is called tmesis, or else diacope, which is when a
vocable either simple or compounde, is divided, and one or two other words
set between, as Plinius. Haec ut res cunque se habeat, for utcunque haec res
se habeat, How so ever this matter standethe.

Si certum est tibi sic facere. If thou be utterly appointed or determined so
to do.

Id permagni refer arbitror. I judge this thing to make very great matter.

Nescientem sentiat te id sibi dare. Let him think that thou do give him
that thing unknowing, or, as though thou kneweste not that thou do give it
unto him.

Per alium quemuis des. Give it unto him, or let him have it by the hands of
any other body, who so ever it be, rather than by thyn own self.

Falli te sinas technis per seruulu^. Suffer thy self to be begyled or
deceived of some one of thy servants, by some subtyl craft or wyle.

Subsensi id quoque. I do half perceive that also.

Subsensi illos ibi esse, et id agere inter se clanculum. I have an ynclynge,
or, I have in manner espied or perceived, that they be and goo about such a
thing privily emonges them selfs.

Cum illo consusurrat. He whispereth with him.

Conferunt consilia. They ley their heedes to gether in counsel.

Tibi perdere talentum hoc pacto satius est, quam illo minam. Better thou were
to lose a hole talent this wey, than that other way a pound. Talentum, ti, is
the sum of fyfty pounds sterlynge.

Non nunc pecunia agitur. This ado is not now made for the money (as who should
say) for saving of money, but rather of thy son. Of this manner speaking by
this verb agitur, it is showed before in the fourth scene of the.ii. act of
this same comedy, in the vulgar, quasi isthic minor mea res agatur quam tua.

Illud agitur, quomodo minimo periculo demus pecuniam adolescentulo. This thing
do we speak of and most chefely regard or reckon upon, how we may let the
young man have money with leest peril of bringynge him to vnthryftynes.

Si semel animum tuum intellexerit. If he shall ones perceive thy mind.

Prodas prius pecunia^ omnem, quam abs te amittas filium. Depart rather with
all the money that thou haste, then let thy son go from the.

Quantam fenestram ad nequitiam patefeceris? How great a gappe shall thou break
open toward noughtynes? Fenestram aperire, or patefacere, to open a wyndoore,
aperire uiam, to open a way, praestruere uiam, to make way before, iacere
fundame^ta, to cast or lay a fundacion, aperire ianuam, to open a gate,
aperire repagula, to open the bars or rayles, be prouerbiall speakynges,
signifienge to give an occasion of any thing. Eras. in Chiliad.

Tibi ut non sit suaue uiuere. So that thou should have small joy of thy
life, or so that it should be but small pleasure to the to live.

Deteriores omnes sumus licentia. We be all the worse by having to much
liberty. Omnes. s. nos. Where note one of the figures of grammatical
construction, that is called in latin euocatio, which is as oftentimes as the
third person is immediately reduced unto the first person, or else to the
second: as Ego pauper laboro, cum tu dives ludas, I being poor do labor,
where as thou being rich do play. Where note that in euocation be four
things required. First persona euocans, which is evermore the first or the
second person, secundarylye persona euocata, which is always the third
person, thirdly that the said third person be joined unto the first or
second, (as aforesaid) immediately, that is to say, no manner conjunction
coming between. And fourthly that the verb be of the first person or the
second, according evermore Cum persona euocate, as in the example above
alleged, evidently it appears. And euocation is of two sorts, either
explicita, as when both persona euocans, and also persona euocata be
expressly set out, or else implicita, that is where persona euocans is
understood and not expressed. Verg. Coram, quem quaeritis, adsum Troius
Aeneas, I Aeneas of Troy, whom you seek, am here present. Ovid. Penelope
coniux sem per Ulysses ero, I Penelope woll ever during my life be the wife of
Ulysses only. In which both examples ego, being persona euocans is not
expressed but understood. Though Priscian think this oration Ego
Appollonius scribo, to be incongrue. Note further that in euocation persona
euocans, et persona euocata. All be it they be evermore of one case, yet
sometimes they be of diverse nombres, as when, persona euocata lacks the
singular nombre: as ego tuae deliciae isthuc ueniam, I thy golpoll, or, I thy
delight and pleasure wolle come thither where thou art: or else when persona
euocata is a nowne collectiue, that is to sey, when it signifies plurality,
or a multitude in the singlar number. Plin. in praefac. nar. hist. Magna pars
studiorum amoenitates quaerimus, A great part of us seek delectable and pleasant
studies. Also when persona euocata is a nowne distributive. Ovid. In magnis
laesi rebus uterque sumus, We be both offended, ill served, or violated in no
small things. Teren. in adelphis. Curemus aequam uterque partem, Let us both
care equally either for his own part. Where nos uterque sumus, and nos
uterque curemus, be euocation.

Quodcunque inciderit in mentem, uolet. What so ever shall fall in his mind, or
in his brain, he woll desire needs to have it.

Neque id putabit, prauumne an rectum sit, quod petet. Nor he woll not regard
this, whether it be ill or else good and honest that he shall desire.

Tu rem perire no^ poteris pati. Thou wolt never be able to abide to se thy
money, goods, or substance wasted or cast away.

Dare illi denegabis. s. pecunia^. Thou wolt say, that thou wolte give him
money.

Maxim apud te se ualere sentit. He perceyueth that he may do very much with
the.

Ibit ad illum, quo maxim apud te se ualere sentiet. He wolle go unto him, by
mean of whom he shall think that he may do most with the.

Abiturum se abs te esse ilico minabatur, He woll threten by and by, that he
woll goo his way from the, and forsake the.

Videre uerum, atque ita uti res est, dicere. Thou semest to say truth, and
even so as the matter is in deed.

Somnum hercle hac nocte oculis non uidi meis, dum id quaero, tibi qui filiu^
restituere^. In says I slept not one wynke this night, studyeng and deuysynge
how I might get bring home and restore thy son to the again. Qui.i.
quomodo. Joan. Calphurnius notes here a propre difference between redditur and
restituitur, saying: Redditur quis cupientibus, ut domino seruus, restituitur
cupiens, ut patriae ciuis: et redditur et restituitur cupiens cupientibus, ut
patri filius. And in these words Oculis non uidi, is a figure of speaking
called pleonasmus, which is when an oration has any superfluity of words
more then needs: as Verg. Vocemque his auribus hausi, I herd a voice with
these ears. Id. Et sic ore locuta est, And thus she spoke with her mouth. For
we do not here, but with our ears, nor speak, but with our mouths, nor see
but with our eyes.

Cedo dexteram. Give me thy hand.

Te oro, ut id facias. I pray the hartylye do hit.

Paratus sum. I am ready.

Scin' quid nunc te facere uolo? Can thou tell what I would have the to do
now?

Quod sensisti illos incipere, id ut mature^t, facito. That thing that thou
haste espied or perceived them to goo about, see, or find the means, or so
do, that they make haste with all, or do it spedily.

Cupio illi dare quod uult. I woll gladdely give him, what so ever he woll
have, Quod for quodcunque.

Cupio ipsum iam uidere. I desire to see him by and by, or I would feign see
him streyghte ways.

Operam dabo. I woll do my diligence.

Syrus est prehendendus, atque adhortandus mihi. I must have Syrus in hand, and
exhort him, or set him on.

A me nescio quis exit. One, I can not tell who, comes forth of my house, or
some man comes forth of my house, who so ever it is.

Concede hinc domum. Go hens home.

Ne nos inter nos congruere sentiant. Let them not perceive that we be agreed
between our selves.

Paululum negotij mihi obstat. I have a little busynes, that lets me.

Simus and Crito uicini nostri ambigunt de finibus. Sinius and Crito our
neighbors are at a little variaunce or controversy about their lands. De
finibus.i. de Limitibus, of the bounds or marks where their grounds do
part, and be divided the one man's land from thothers.

Me caepere arbitrum. They have made me vmpier and judge between them.
Coepere.i. fecere, elegere. Arbiter (says Donatus) dabatur ijs, qui de
finibus regendis ambigerent. Dicitur enim arbiter, iudex, quod totius rei
habeat arbitrium et facultatem, an vmpier or sticler.

Ibo, ac dicam, operam me hodie no^ posse hijs dare. I woll go and show them,
that I can not attend on them this day.

Continuo hic adero. I woll be here ageyn by and by.

Ita quaeso. So I pray you heartily.

Dij uostram fidem. Oh the faith of the goddis. Vostram for uestram per
antithesin, It is a manner of speaking, used in Poetis for an interiection of
meruaylynge: as proh Deum atque hominum fidem, Proh sum Iuppiter, with others
like.

Ita ne comparatam esse hominum naturam omnium, aliena melius ut uideant et
iudice^t, quam sua? Is it thus true, that it is naturally given unto all men to
see further, and better to judge in other men's matters, then in their own? or,
Is this so, that the nature of every man has this propretie, that they can see
or mark, and also judge other men's matters better than their own? Ita ne. s.
uerum est secundum Laur. Vall. And of such manner speakynges by the infinitive
mode absolutely put, it is in diverse places annoted before.

Eo fit. Thereby it is, it happens or chances, or thereof it comes.

In re nostra aut gaudio sumus praepediti nimio, aut aegritudine. In any matter
of our own we be let, that is to seye, blinded that we can not see or
judge, by reason either of to much ioyfulnes, or else of sorrow, wofulnes, and
disquiete of the mind. Praepediti. s. quo minus uidere et recte iudicare
ualeamus.

Hic mihi quanto nunc rectius sapit, quam egomet mihi. How much wiser is he
now in this case for my behofe and profit, than I am for mine own?

Dissolui me ocyus, operam tibi ut darem. I have dispatched my self hastily or
spedily to wait or attend on you.

In the second Scene.
Hac illac circumcursa. Run about this way and that way.

Inueniendum est argentum. I must find out or get some money.

Intendenda in senem fallacia. s. est. I must find some wyle to deceive the
old manne. It is a metaphore taken of the stretchynge of the synowes or of
strings in a bow, or lute, or other instrument. For Inte^do, dis, inte^di,
intentu^ is to stretche or to reche, as a man does streche the stryng of a bow,
or of a lute. And thereof by translation it is said in latin Intendere uocem to
strain the voice, that is, to speak as loud as a man may reche, and intentio
uocis is straynyng of the voice, Intendere animum aut ingenium, or intendere
neruos animi uel ingenij, is to strain the mind or wit, that is, to give very
exact advertisement of the mind, or of the wit, and to prove the same to the
uttermost. So here intendenda in senem fallacia is as much as if he should
have said in english: I must streyne a synowe or stretche a vein to beguyle
the old man. Of Intendo read L. Vall. li. 6. eleg. ca. 4.

Number me fefellit, host id struere? Was I deceived, when I seyde that these
fellows went about such a thing? Here is also a metaphore taken of buylders.
For struo, struis, struxi, structum is to buyld or to make a frame: and thereof
by translation struere ignem is to make a fire, struere fallaciam to ymagin a
wyle, struere milites, apud Titum Liuium is to set souldiars in array. etc.

Est ille tardiusculus. He is somewhat slow.

Huic nostro tradita est prouincia. The matter is committed unto this fellow of
mine, or to my man here to do. The Romans of old time called in latin
prouinciam, any out region or far countreye that they had subdued unto their
dominion, empire, and jurisdiction, and held in the same their jurisdiction by
a leuetenaunt, sent thither to govern and rule it. And because that tho
persons, which were admytted and sent by the Romans to rule in any province,
were sent thither as officers, and with commission, and with great charge,
therefore the very office self of rulynge in any province was also called in
latin prouincia, and thereof by a metaphore all the burden, labor, or business
of doing in any manner office, or thing to be done, is called in latin
speaking prouincia, as here. And also in Phormione. O Geta prouinciam cepisti
duram, O Geta thou have taken in hand, or take upon the, a great or hard
matter to do. etc.

Perij, numnam haec audiuit? Alas that ever I was borne, has he hard all this?

Quid tu istic. s. agis? Whatte makeste thou there? or what do thou there?
Note here the difference between these three pronownes hic, iste, and ille, with
their aduerbes derived of them, hic, haec, hoc, hic, hinc, huc, hac, horsum,
with all other deriuatiues and compoundes of the same, as hiccine. etc. in
latyne speaking be referred unto the first person, that is to say unto the
speaker. Iste, ista, istud, istic, istinc, istuc, isto, istorsum, istac. etc.
be referred unto the second person, that is, to the party that the speaker
speaks unto. Ille, la, lud, illic, illinc, llac, illuc, illorsum, illo, be
referred unto the third person, that is, neither unto the speker, nor unto
him that he speakethe unto, but to the third from them both, as I writing
from London to my friend being in Oxforde, that we might meet for to ride
together unto York, may write thus unto him. Iampride^ istuc profectus essem,
nisi me hic occupationes meae detinerent. Tu igitur matura istinc te mouere,
atque huc primo quoque tempore aduolare, quo possimus una illo, quo decreuimus,
proficisci, I had a greatte while sens commen thither (that is to Oxforde where
thou art) but that certain busynes that I have, keep and withholde me here
(in London.) Therefore hye thou, and spedily bestyrre the to comme away from
thense (that is from Oxforde before said) and in all haste possible to comme
hither (to London) to the end that we may both together take our iourneye
thither, as we appointed (that is to York.)

Equidem te demiror tam mane (.s. surrexisse, aut foris in publico conspici)
qui heri tantum biberis. I marvel greatly that thou art up, or that thou
come abroad so erely to day, which drankest so much or so depely yesterdaye.

Visa est, quod dici solet, aquilae senectus. Me thought I saw an old egle (as
the proverb says) Aquilae senectus, the old age of an egle, is a latin
proverb used to be spoken of old me^, or others that live more by drynk tha^
by mete. For Plin. li. 10. na. hist. ca. 3. of the nature of egles shows,
that egles dyen or peryshen neither for age, nor by reason of any sycknes, but
for hungre and lack of meat. For the upper part of their bylles or bebes
grows so much and so far over the nether part, that the aduncitie or
crokednesse thereof may not be opened, nor may not gape to receive sustinance of
meat, so that when they be old they live only by drynk, and by suckynge the
blood of such prays as they have killed, and not by eating. And semblably
aged folks for the most part drinken more then they eaten.

Mulier co^moda et faceta haec est. This woman is a gentyl companion, or a good
fellow, and a pleasant, or merry one.

Mulier forma luculenta. A very fair or beautiful woman. Luculenta.i.
egregia, prae clara, insigni, spectanda.

Mulier forma sane bona. A woman assuredly right well favored.

Ille hanc deperit. He is very far in love with her. or, he is nighe mad
for the love of her. For that is proprelye Deperire in latyne.

Habet patrem quendam auidum, miserum atque aridum. He has to his father a
certain fellow, gredy of money, a wretched fellow in his house, and a very
pynchepeny, as dry as a kixe.

Atque si is non diuitijs abu^det, gnatus eius profu^^^ inopia. His son is
run aweye, and has forsaken the country, as though he had no riches at
all, nor were worth a penye.

Scin esse factum, ut dico? Do you not know, that it is so as I say?

Homo pistrino dignus. A fellow worthy extreme punysshemente.

Tibi timui male. I was curstly or shrewdly aferde on thy behalf. Caueo with
an accusative is to avoid and eschew, as caueo te, I eschew the or thy
company. Caueo tibi with a dative, idem quod prospicio, I am ware and
provided, that thou have no harm.

Passus est id fieri. He suffered that to be done.

Garris.i. inepte loqueris, nugas loqueris. Thou ianglest, thou pratest, thou
speakeste foolysshely.

Haec facta ab illo oportebant. Thus ought he to have done. Verbes
impersonalles, as decet, delectat, oportet, iuuat, with others like be
sometimes changed into personals, especially in poetis.

Eho quaeso, laudas qui heroes fallunt? Ah sir, or, whatte I pray the heartily
do thou allow and commend such as deceive their masters? Elio somtymes
is an interiection of meruaylyng, somtymes of calling unto a body, as Ehodum ad
me: somtymes of asking a question, as here.

Recte sane. In good sothe well said, or in feythe gentylmanly spoken.

Magnarum saepe id remedium aegritudinum est. That is often times a remedy or
medicine for great soores or diseases.

Iocone an serio haec dicat, nescio. I can not tell, whether he say all
this in game, or in sadness, in iape, or in erneste.

Mihi quidem addit animum, quo libeat magis. At leest wise he gives me
courage, stomach, or boldness, that I have the better lust, or the more
mind, will, pleasure, or desire to do it.

At nunc, quid expectat? But now what does he look for?

Aliquam fabricam fingit. He gothe about some wyle. Fabricam.i. fallaciam.

Stolidus est. He is a very fool. Of the difference between these words,
Stolidus, fatuus, and stultus, it is showed before.

At te adiutare oportet adolescentuli causa. But thou must help thereto, for
the yo^ge man's sake.

Facile equidem facere possum, si iubes. I can do it quyckly, lightly, or
easily, if thou say the word.

Quo pacto id fieri soleat calleo. I know perfytely well how it is most
commonly used to be done.

Non est mentiri meum. It is not my propretye, condycion, or guyse to lie.

At heus tu, facito haec memineris. But how syrrha, see that thou remember all
this.

Si quid huius simile forte aliquando euene rit. If any such thing as this
is, shall perchance happen at any time.

Vt sunt humana. As the course of the world is, or as many things do chance
in the world among men.

Non usu ueniet, spaero. It shall not chance I hope.

Spaero hercle ego quoque. In good sothe I also hope the same.

Neque eo nunc dico quod quicquam senserim. And I say it not for that I have
perceived any such thing.

Quae sit eius aetas, uides. Thou see what age he is of, or what years he
has, thou see.

Nae ego te (si usus ueniat) magnifice tractare possim. In faith I could
handle the royally if need were, or, if any such occasion should chance.

De istoc, cum usus uenerit uidebimus quid opus sit. As concerning this, that
thou speastest of now, when time and occasion shall be, or when it shall come
in place or in ure, we shall see what is most expedient, necessary, or
behouable.

Nunc istuc age. As now, go about that thou haste in han de.

Nunquam commodius herum audiui loqui. I never hard a master speak more
commodiousely or more to the pay of his servant.

Quisnam a nobis egreditur for as? Who comes forth of our house?

In the third Scene.
Quid istuc, quaeso. s. est? What is that, I beseech the?

Qui istic mos est? What manner or guyse is that of thine?

Ita ne fieri oportet? Is hit honesty so to do?

Quid ego feci? What did I? or what have I done?

Vidin ego te modo manum in sinum huic meretrici inserere? Did I not see the
right now put thy hand in this drabbes bosom.

Acta res est. The matter is paste recure, or paste remedy. Acta res est, is
the same that actum est. of which it is said before.

Facis adeo indigne iniuriam illi, qui non abstineas manum. Truly thou do him
sham full wrong, that thou kepest not away, nor hold of thyn hands.

Istaec quidem contumelia est. In deed this is a great despite.

Hominem amicum recipe ad te. Receive or take in a friend of thine into thy
house.

Heri in conuiuio quam immodestus fuisti? Yesterday at the table how vnsobre,
wyld, unruly or unmannerly were thou?

Metui, quid futurum denique esset. I feared what should come of it at last? or
I was aferde what should be the end of it?

Noui ego amantium animos, aduertunt grauiter quae non censeas. I know well
enough the minds of them that be in love, They mark sore a thing, that a man
would not say nor judge that they do.

Fides mihi apud hunc est. I am in good credit with him.

Mihi fides apud hunc est, me nihil istius facturum. I am in such credit with
this man, that he mystrusteth not that I wolle do any such thing, or he has
very good trust in me that I wol not serve him any such touch, or play him
any such pranke.

Esto, at certe concedas aliquo ab eorum ore aliquantisper. Be it so, or, I
woll well that, yet in faith go into some place out of their sight, presence,
way, or company for a little while. Laur. Vall. has noted aduerbes compouned
with per, as parumper, paulisper, tantisper, aliquantisper, to be referred unto
brevity or shortness of time. Wherefore they erren and done amysse, that take
and use the said aduerbes for their symples, parum, paulum, tantum,
aliquantum. Esto is used for a voice or aduerbe of grantyng, ex Seruio.

Prohibet me facere tua praesentia. Thy presence, or thy being here lets me
to do it.

Ego de me facio coniecturam. I coniecte that by my self, or I take a
conjecture by the example of my own self.

Nemo est meorum amicorum hodie, apud que^ expromere omi^a mea occulta audeam.
There is never a friend that I have this day living, before whom, or unto
whom I dare be bold to open, utter, show, or disclose all my secrets, or
the bottom of my stomach.

Facti piget. s. me. I am sorye for that that I have done. These syx verbes
impersonals Poenitet, taedet, piget, pudet, miseret, miserescit be construed
with an accusative and a genitive: as Poenitet me dicti, I forthynke or repent
my saying. Taedet me uitae, I am werye of my life. Piget me laboris, I am loath
or unlusty to labor. In the stead of the genitive they may have joined with
them an infinitive mode: as Poenitet me dixisse, Taedet me uiuere, Piget me
laborare. Piget me here in this place of Terence is taken for dolet mihi uel
molestum est. Of the jignification of piget read more largely in Adelph. act.
3. scen. 4. in the vulgar Fratris me quidem pudet piget que.

Ne ineptus, ne proteruus uidear. That I seem not folyshe, nor saucy, or
malopert.

Nostrum est intelligere, utcunque, atque ubicunque opus sit obsequi. s. amico.
It is our part to mark and to perceive how so ever, and when so ever we
should do our friend pleasure in seruyng or following his appetite.

Haec ego praecipio tibi, hominis frugi and temperantis functo officium. This I
advise or counseyle the, doing therein the office or part of an honest or
friendly and of a sobre or chaste man. Frugi.i. utilis et necessarij, sumpta
metaphora a frugibus. Temperantis.i. sobrij casti. Te^pera^tia is diffined of
Tullie that it is rationis in libidinem, atque in alios non rectos impetus
animi firma et moderata dominatio, Temperancie is a firm and mode rate or
measurable domination and ruling of reason over all naughty and wanton
appetites and lusts of the body, and over al other violente affections of the
mind being wrong and out of course.

Tace sodes. Hold thy peace I pray the.

Pudet me, neque id iniuria. I am ashamid and that not without cause.

Pergin' hercle? s. loqui. Have thou not done yet? or, what yet more pratinge?

Dico quod uidetur. s. mihi. I sey as I think: or I sey as my heart gives me.
or, I speak as my mind is.

Non acceda^ ad illos? Shall not I com unto them? or shall I have no way to them?

Eho, quaeso una accedundi uia est? What I pray the heartily is there no mo but
one way to come to them? or (as we say prouerbially in english) been there no
mo ways to the wood but one?

Hic prius se indicarit, quam ego argentum effero. This fellow woll surely
betray his own counsel, before that I get any money at all. Indicarit pro
indicauerit, per syncopen. And it is as Donatus expounds the future of the
subiunctife set for the future of the indicative. After some grammarians it
is modus promissiuus, the mode or manner of promisinge, that a thing shall be.
Pomponius calls it futurum exactum, which the greeks expound by the
participle of the preter perfect tense joined with the future tense of the
verb substantife esomai. And the latin me^ in verbes deponent, as ero
locutus. And Linacer in his first book De emendata structura or de octo
partibus, makes mention, that Grocinus, which undoubtedly was a man of
most exquisite, exact, and precise both knowledge and judgment as well in
grammatical things, as also in all other kinds and sorts of learning,
divided the times of verbes in this wise, that he put in the verb three tenses,
that is to wit, the present tense, the pretertense, and the future tense. And
every of these he put to be of two sortis, that is to wit, the one imperfect
and the other perfyte. The present tense vnperfet, as Scribo, I write, or I am
in writing: so that the action of writynge is not yet accomplysshed nor
fynished. The present tense perfet, as scripsi, I have written, or I have done
writing: so that the said action of writing is finyshed and done. And in like
wise the preterte^se vnperfet, as scribebam, I did write, or I was in writing,
the action or doing thereof not yet paste. The pretertense perfet, as
scripseram, I had written, or I had done and ended writynge. Also the future
tense vnperfet, as scribam, I woll or shall write, the action of writynge not
yet begone. The future tense perfet, as scripsero, I shall have written, or I
shall have done writing, the action of writynge already begone and entered, but
not yet ended.

Vin tu homini stulto mihi auscultare? Wolt thou be ruled by the counseile of
me a folishe fellow? Vin pro uis ne.

Iube hunc abire hinc aliquo. Beade or command this fellow here to go or to
get him from hense some whither.

Quo ego hinc abeam? Whyther should I go from hens? Abeam.i. abire debeam. For
it is the potenciall mode, which may always be expowned by possum or debeo, or
some like verb: But of the potentiall mode, and of the use of the same in all
tenses, read examples in Linacer, in his first book, De emendata structura,
sieve de octo partibus.

Abi quo lubet Go whither the lusts.

Abi deambulatum. Go thy ways to walk. Deambulatum, the first supine, which
is used alweyes in thactife signification and is englished like the infinitive
mode of the active voice. And when so ever the english of the infinitive of
thactiue voice, comes after any verb or other word, betokeninge goinge or
mouynge to a place, hit shall be put in the first supine.

Abi istac, istorsum, quouis. Go this way, go that way, go whyther so ever
thou wolte.

Recte dicit, censeo. He says or speaks well, I say even the same.

Dij te eradice^t, qui me istinc extrudas. The goddis take a vengeance on the:
or, send the an evil end, which do thruste me out from your company.
Eradicare proprely is to pluck up by the roots, and by translation it is
referred to the utter destruction of any thing.

Tu tibi istas posthac comprimito manus. Hold in, keep down, or tame thou
those hands of thyn from hensforthe. Comprimito is the imperative mode and the
present te^se. For Linacer in the first book De emend. struct. very well
proves, that the imperative mode has no future tense. First for that the
greeks have no future tense in this mode: seco^darily for that the voices of
the imperatif mode, endinge in to, tote, and tor, may be joined with aduerbes
of the future time. Thirdely for that the same voices in to, tote, tor, ben
founden joined with other voices of the same imperative mode, which all
gra^marians confess and sey to be of the present time: as Propertius. Aut si
es dura, nega, sin es non dura, uenito. Ver. Tytere dum re deo, breuis est uia,
pasce capellas, Et potum pastas age Tytere, et inter agendu^ Occursa re capro,
cornu ferit ille, caueto.

Quid illu^ credis facturum, nisi eum, quantum dij dant tibi opis, seruas,
castigas, mones? What thynkest thou that he woll do, except thou awayte,
chastise, and rebuke him with all the help that the goddis may give the (as
who should say) with all the help that thou may have of the goddis?
Seruas.i. obseruas.

Ego istuc curabo. I wol se or look to that: or, I wol provide for that.

Hic tibi adseruandus est. Thou must lay good awayte on him here: or, thou
had need to take good head on this fellow here.

Mihi iam minus minusque obtemperat. He is now every day less and less ruled
by me.

Ecquid de illo, quod dudum tecum egi, egi sti? Haste thou done any thing in
the matter, for which I was in hand with the erewhile. Agere cum aliquo de re
aliqua, is to talk with a man, and (as we sey in english) to be in hand with
him for or concerning any thing to be done. Of ecquid hit is showed before.

Reperisti tibi quod placeat, an non dum etiam? Haste thou found out any
thing to thy mind, or not yet neither?

Frugi es. Thou art an honest fellow.

Aliud ex alio incidit. One thing arises of an other: or, one thing comes in,
or comes unto mind by reason of an other.

Pessima haec est meretrix. This is a perilous naughty queen.

Video quod inceptet facinus. I se what he begins to do, or I perceive
wheraboute he gothe, or I se what a pranke he is about to play. Io.
Calphurnius notes, that incipere and inceptare be referred to great bold and
hardy enterprises: as in Eunuch. Quid inceptas Thraso? What intend you to
enterprise now Thraso?

Huic drachmarum argenti mill dederat mutuum. He had lenned unto this man
syxtene pounds and one mark of ready money. Dram is a greek word, and it
was a certain coin of money in Athenes, and all that countreye of equal and
the same value, as was in Rome the quoyne of silver that was there called
Denarius, which after the supputacion and rekeninge of Budaeus, in that his
warke entitled De ass, is a grote sterlinge or some what more: so that
reckenyng drachmam at the value of a grote sterlyng, mill drachmarum a
thousand grotes sterlyng make the just su^me of.xvi. li. xiii. s. iiii. 8.

Reliquit filiam adolescentulam huic arraboni pro illo argento. He left a
daughter of his being a very young thing, for a pledge or gage unto this man
for that su^me of money. Arraboni.i. pignori here in this place, but arrabon,
nis, and arra, rae, proprely is the money that is given in earneste at any
bargayne making for thassaraunce and ratifienge of all couenantes and
conditions of the same.

Est nunc ad uxorem tuam. He is now with thy wife. ad for apud.

Dubium ne id est? Is that any mattier of doubt?

Ego sic putam. So thought I.

Quid nunc facere cogitas? What are you now advised and minded to do?

Dicam fi redimat magnum inesse in eo lucrum. I wol say unto him, that if he
should bye it, there were great gaynes or wynninge in it, or that greatte money
might be gotten by it.

Erras. Thou art deceived, or thou art out of the way.

Pro Menedemo nunc tibi ego respondeo. I woll now make answer unto the, on
the behalf, or in the name and person of Menedemus, or as though I were
Menedemus.

Optata loquere. Thou spekest joyful words, or as I would have the. Optata the
accusative plurell, neutre gendre, put substantiuely, or else understanding.

Non uerba opus est. It needs not, or it is not expedient.

Quid est, quod tam grauiter crepuerunt fores? What is the mattier, that the
doors have made such great creakyng?

Out of the fourth Act in the first Scene.
Nisi me animus fallit. If I be not deceived in mine own mind.

Quid uult sibi haec oration? Whatte mean these words?

Dixi equidem, ubi mihi ostendisti, illico eu^ esse. As soon as ever thou
shewedst him unto me, I said streyghte ways that it was even he.

At ut satis contemplatus modo sis. s. uide. But se that thou have well looked
upon him, or well marked him.

Abi iam nunc intro, atque illa si iam lauerit, mihi nuncia. Go in at ones, and
if she have already washed, bring me word.

Hic ego illum interea opperibor. I wolle tarry here for him there while.
Opperibor of opperior, riris. For in old time verbes of the fourth coniugacion
formed the future tenses of the indicative mode in bo and bor: saying scibo,
audibo, opperibor, with others. Examples be many apud Plautum. And some
grammarians note, that opperiri a verb deponent, when it betokens expectare
to tarry for, is written with double pp, for a difference to be had between
it, and operior the passyue of operio, which is to cover.

Te uult, uideas quid uelit. He seeks the, se what he would have.

Nescio quid tristis est. He is sad, what so ever the matter is.

Non temere est. It is not for naught.

Nae ista magno iam conatu, magnas nugas dixerit. In faith she with all her
great earnest facion woll anon say even very trifles, as who should say,
things of no weyghte nor ymportaunce, ne worth the heryng.

Teipsum quaero. I seek for you and no man else.

Loquere quid uelis. Say what thou would have.

Hoc te oro, ne quid credas, me aduersus edictum tuu^ facere esse ausum. This I
heartily pray you, not to think, that ever I was so bold, as to do any thing
contrary to your commandment.

Vis me isthuc tibi credere? Woldeste thou have me to believe the in that?

Nescio quid peccati portat haec purgatio. This purgation, or excuse making
imports, argues, or proves sum offense or fault done what so ever it is.

Scio quid feceris. I know what thou have done.

Sic factum est. It was even so.

Damno auctus est. He has o one shrewed turn more than he had.

Hic erat anus Corinthia, haud impura. Here was an old woman of Corinthe a
good honest creature.

O Iuppiter, tantam inesse animo inscitiam?. s. oportuit, uel decuit. O
Iuppiter should any body have been so folishe? or should so great folyshnes rest in
any bodies mind? or, should thou have had no more knowledge then so? For
inscitia proprely is lack or defaulte of knowdlege: as inscius is he, that
knows not a thing.

Si peccaui, insciens feci. If I did amysse, I did it unware or unknowing.

Id equidem, etiam si tu neges, certo scio. Verily that I know for a surety,
though thou would say nay to it.

Scio te inscientem, atque imprudentem dicere ac facere omnia. I know that
thou say and doeste all things vnwyttynge or unknowing, and unware, or
unadvised.

Multa peccata in hac re ostendis. Thou show many offenses in this one
mattier.

Si meum imperium exequi uoluisses, interempram oportuir. s. filiam. If thon
had been disposed, minded, or willing to have executed and done my
commandment, she must have been slain.

Id omitto. That I let pass.

Quam been abs te perspectum est? How well thou haste confydered the mattier
or seen unto it?

Quid uoluisti? What was thy mind? or, what dyddest thou entende?

Credo, id cogitasti? I believe thou thoughtest the same, or I think thou
dyddeste so entende.

Quid cum illis agas, qui neque ius, neque bonum atque aequu^ sciunt? What
should a body meddle with such, as know another right ne honest facion and reason,
equity, or good conscience.

Quid cu^ illis agas, qui melius, peius, pro sit, obsit, nihil uident, nisi
quod lubet? What should one meddle with such, as be it better or be it wors, may
it hurt a man or do him good, se nor regard nothing but what them self lift.

Te obsecro, quanto tuus est animus natu grauior, tanto sit ignoscentior. I
beseech you for goddis sake, that the more grave, sage, and discrete that your
wisdom is by reason of your aege, so much more redye the same may be to
forgive and pardon.

Meae stultiae iustitia tua sit aliquid praesidij. Let your goodness or
resonablenes be some refuge or succor unto my folyshnes.

Scilicet, equidem istuc factum ignoscam. Yes marye, I woll forgive or pardone
this deed of thine. For (as before is said) in the word istuc is proprely
included tuum.

Male docet te mea facilitas multa. My gentylnes, or my favorable facion
mystecheth the many things, as who should sey, gives the occasion to do ill in
many things.

Istuc, quicquid est, qua occeptum est causa, loquere. Show for what cause, or
of what occasion this thing began, what so ever it is.

Vt stultae et miserae omnes, sumus religiosae. As all we peuyshe and silie
poor women be full of superstition.

De digito annulum detraho. I pulled of a ring from my finger.

Ne expers partis esset de nostris bonis. That he should not be without some
part of our goods.

Istuc recte. s. factum est abs te, uel, fecisti. Therein thou dyddest well.

Conseruasti te atque illum. Thou haste saved both thy self and him to.

Vnde habes? How came thou by it, or, where have thou gotten it?

Lauatum dum it, seruandum mihi dedit an nulum. While he went to wasshe he gave
me his ring to keep.

Non aduorti primo, sed postquam aspexi, illico cognoui. I took no head to him,
or I marked him not at the first, but after that I looked upon him, or iyed him
better, I knew him anon.

Ad te exilui. I whypped me forth to the, or I came leaping or skyppynge
forth to the a great pace. For exilire here imports both ioyfulnes and
speed in coming forth.

Quid nunc suspicare, aut inuenis de illo? What do thou coniect orels find
of him? Suspicor, caris, is to deem, coniecte, surmise, or mystruste, and it is
used in latyne authors as well in the good part, as in the evil.

Si potest reperiri. If he may be sound.

Plus spei uideo quam uolo. I see more or better hope, than I would by my good
will.

Noster est, si ita est. He is of our side: or, he is our own man, if it be so.

Viuit ne ille, cui tu dederas? Is he he alive unto whom thou dyddest deliver
it?

Quid renu^ciauit fecisse? s. se. What brought he word again that he had done?

Renunciauit se fecisse quod iusseram. He brought word ageyn that he had done
that I commanded.

Nomen mulieris cedo quid sit, ut quaeratur. Show what is the name of the
womanne that she may be sought out.

Sequere me intro hac. Come in after me this way.

Vt praeter spem euenit. How much otherwise it is chanced then I looked, or
thought for. Vt, is here admirantis, as it is also showed before.

Quam timui male, ne nunc animo ita esses duro, ut olim? How shrewdely was I
aferde, lest that you would be as hard hearted now, as you were a great while
gone?

Non licet hominem esse saepe, ita ut uult, si res non sinit. A man often times
may not be as he would him self, if the thing wol not suffer him.

Nunc ita tempus est, ut cupiam. Such is the time now, that I would feign.

Olim nihil minus cupiebam. Ones I was as lyttelle feign or desirous of it as
of any thing.

In the second scene.
Haud multum a me aberit infortunium. I shall not be serre from a shrewde
turn, or I shall go nere to have a shrewde turn.

In angustum oppido nunc meae coguntur copiae. All the help that I have is now
driven to a very narowe streyte, as who should say, to an hard point of
shyfte. Oppido*.i. ualde. Copiae, copiarum, in the plurel nombre, proprely
signifies an host or an army, and by translation it is taken and used in
latyne speaking or writynge, for all the help or power that a man has.

Nisi aliquid uideo, ne resciscat senex. Except I see or find sum means,
that the old man may have no knowledge of it.

Quod sperem de argento, nihil est. As for me to trust or to hope to get any
money, it is in vain* or if may not avail.

Triumpho si licet me latere tecto abscedere. I am a conqueror if I may depart
or escape with a hole skin.

Crucior bolum tantum mihi ereptum tam subito e faucibus. It greueth me right
sore that such a good great morsel gobbette or pray is so suddenly snached out
of my mouth. Fauces proprely be the checkes. Bolus, li, is a piece or a gobbet
of any thing: as bolus terrae, is a clodde of earth, bolus argenti a wedge or
a piece of silver. Here it is taken for praeda. Read the prouer be Bolus e
faucibus ereptus, in chil. Eras.

Quid agam? aut quid co^miniscar? What may I do, or what may I deuyse and
ymagine? Agam and co^miniscar be of the potenciall mode, of the which mede
read Linacru^ li. 1. de emend. structura.

Ratio de integro ineunda est mihi. I must be feign to begin my reckenynge or
account al new again.

Nihil tam difficile, quin quaerendo inuestigari possit. There is nothing of
so greatte difficulty or so hard to be done, but by seeking it may be found
out. Of the signification of inuestigo and uestigo, it is showed before.

Quid si hoc sic incipiam nunc? What if I now begin the mattier in this
wise? or, what if I now begin to take this way in the mattier?

Si sic incipiam, nihil est: si sic, tantundem egero. If I begin thus, it is
to no purpose: or, it may not avail: if thus, I shall bring hit to like
effect, as who should say, all shall be one.

Euge, optumam habeo rationem. Well seid I have found a meruayllous or passing
good way.

Retraham hercle, opinor, ad me illud fugi tiuum argentum, tamen. In feyth (I
trow) I wol yet for all this pull back to me ageyn, or convey into my fyngers
again that slyp per money, that would so feign be gone.

In the third Scene.
Nulla mihi res posthac potest iam interuenire tanta, quae mihi aegritudinem
afferat. It is not possible for any thing to come in my way now from
henseforthe, so great, that may grene my heart, or cause me to be sad.

Tanta laetiria oborta est. s. mihi. So greatte joy and gladdenes is chanced
or comme unto me.

Dedo patri me nu^c, ut frugalior sim quam uult. I yield my self now unto my
father: or, I put my self now in my fathers hands to be more honest man, and
of better rule tha^ he would have me to be. Frugalior is the comparatiue, and
frugalissimus the superlatiue of frugi, and not frugalis, which is no pure
good nor vsitate latin word, as may be taken of Quintilian li. 1. instit.
orat. but in the stead or place of frugalis, the latin authors use frugi of al
gendres and vndeclyned. And frugi proprely signifies him, that is temperate
and measurable in his diete, or manner of living of his body, and somtymes in
apparel and other semblable things, as in plain contrary signification
luxuriosus is used apud Senecam, who says, Luxurioso frugalitas poena est,
Unto a ryottous person sobre living or good rule is a great pain. And apud
Quintil. who has a declamacion de duobus filijs. frugi, luxuriosoque, of two
sons, the one sobre and of good rule in living, and the other ryottous and of
ill rule. Frugalitas in greek is called sophrosyne: and frugi homines be called
in greek chresimi, that is utiles, sobrij, et necessarij, Profitable sobre and
necessary. Cic. li. 3. Tuscul. quaesti. Of the signifycation of these two
words frugalitas and frugi says thus: Sed quia nec qui propter metu^
praesidium relinquit, quod est ignauiae, nec qui propter auaricia^, clam
depositu^ non reddit, quod est iniustitiae, nec qui propter temeritatem male
rem gessit, quod est stultitiae, frugi appellari solet, eas tres uirtutes,
fortitudinem, iustitiam, and prudentiam, frugalitas complexa est. Ergo frugi
hominem, bonum et uirtute praeditum intelligimus. Cuius contrariu^ est nequam,
cuius significatio una est, quod sit libidinis inte^perans, altera, quod ad
nihil bonus sit, sed plane malus, But for because that neither he, which for
fear forsakes his garrison, which to do is a point of dastardnes or
cowardnes, nor he that for avarice or covetous desire and love of goods,
money, or any other thing, being privily and without any wytnes present, put
and laid in his custody to keep, doethe not restore, but withholde, when it is
required and asked again, which is untrue and vnrightwise dealing, ne yet he
that for temeritie or wylfull rasshnes and lack of discretion, has had ill
luck and mysauenture or mysfortune in his matters or affairs (specially in
battle) which is a token and the propretie of foolyshnes, because that no
such person is commonly or usually called in latin frugi: Therefore this
latin word frugalitas in signification conteyneth and betokens as much as
all these three virtues: that is to wit hardynes, rightousnes, or true and
upright dealing, and also prudence or much knowledge and experience of
things. Then it follows, that when we sey in latin frugi homo, we
understand thereby a good and honest man, and endued with manhood and virtue.
And the contrary of the same latin word frugi, is nequam: the which word
nequam has two significations: in the one signification it is a man that is
