lecherouse and of myslyuyng of his body: in the other signification nequam is
he that is good for nothing, but even a very naughty vnthyryfte. Columell.
lib. 1. Eiusdem agilitatis homo frugi, melius quam nequam faciet, If there be
two men of semblable and equal agilitie or weldynes and besterynge of them
selfs, an honest fellow shall do any thing that he is set unto better than
shall an unthrifty fellow or a naughty pack.

Nihil me fefellit. I was nothing deceived.

Quantum audio huius uerba. As fer as I here by his words here.

Istuc tibi ex sententia tua obtigisse laetor. I am glad that this has
chanced unto you after your own mind.

Audistin' obsecro? Haste thou herd it I pray the?

Quid ni, qui usque una affuerim. s* tecum. What else, that have continually ben
present to gether with the.

Cui aeque audisti co^modius quicquam eve nisse? Unto whom have thou herd any
thing to have chanced so greatly to his pay? Aeque commodius for aeque
commode, the comparatiue degree for the positife.

Ita me dij ament, ut ego nunc non tam me apt caussa laetor, quam huius. God
so love or help me, as I am glad and rejoice now, not so greattely for mine
own cause, as for his here.

Eum ego scio esse quouis honor dignum. I know that he is worthy to be had in all honor and price.

Da te mihi uicissim. Let me have thy help again.

Amici quoque res est uidenda, in tuto ut collocetur. A man must se unto the
matters and affairs of his friend, as well as him self, that it be set in
safety and in good case.

Siccine mihi interloqueris? Do thou in such wise break my tale? or, do
so interrupte my communication.

Deorum uitam adepti sumus. We be even in heaven, or (as we say in jesting) we
have apostles lives, or saints lives. etc.

Frusira operam hanc, opinor, sumo. I wene I spend this labor in vain.

Loquere, audio. Seye on, I here well enough: or, I harken to the.

Hoc non ages. Thou wolt not take heed to this, or (in an other signification
of ago, gis) thou shalt not do this thing.

Si nunc a nobis abis. If thou depart or go away from us now.

Coelabitur itidem, ut coelatum adhuc est. It shall be kept secret even so, as
it has been hitherto.

Isthoc nihil est meis nuptijs magis aduorsum. There is nothing that shall make
more against my marriage than this that thou faist. Aduorsum for aduersum.

Quo ore appellabo patre^? With what face or contenance shall I speak unto my
father?

Tenes quid dicam? Doeste thou perceive what I say?

Quid ni? s. teneam. What else?

Quid dicam? quam caussam afferam? What shall I say? and what excuse shall I
make? or what pretexte shall I lay?

Nolo mentiare. I woll not that thou make any lie. Nolo mentiare for ut
mentiare: it is eclipsis coniunctionis.

Aperte, ita ut res sese habet, narrato. Show the matter even plain so as it
is in deed.

Bonam atque iustam rem oppido imperas, et factu facile^. Thou beadest me to do
a good and a reasonable thing and easy to be done.

Satin' sanus es et sobrius? Art thou well in thy wit, and well auysed?

Tu quidem illum plane prodis. Thou do even plainly or utterly betray him.

Qui ille poterit esse in tuto, dic mihi. How may he possibly be in safety,
show me? Qui pro quomodo.

Huic equidem consilio palma^ do. In feith I give prick and price unto this
devise or counsel. Of the proverbs Palmam far, and palmam tribuere, it is
showed before.

Hic me magnifice effero, qui uim tantam in me, et potestatem habeam tantae
astutiae. In this I auaunce my self royally, that I have within me so great
might and ability to work so great a subtyltie.

Vera dicendo eos ambos fallam. I wol be guyle them both by telling the
troth.

At enim spem isthoc pacto rursum nuptiarum omnem eripis. Mary but by this
means thou take away again all hope of marriage.

Tu fortasse quid me fiat paruipendis, dum illi consulas. Thou percase do
little pass what become of me, so that thou may make some shyfte for him, or
help him. Of quid me fiat, with other like manner of spekinges, it is showed
before.

Tantum sat habes? Art thou contented or satisfied with that alone?

Quid tum quaeso? What then I pray you?

Si hoc pater resciuerit. If my father shall have knowledge of this.

Quid si redeo ad illos? What and if I goo back again to them?

Quid si coelum ruat? What and if the sky fall? Read of this proverb in chil.
Eras.

Metuo quid agam. I fear what I may do.

Metuis? quasi non ea potestas sit tua, quo uelis in tempore ut te exoluas.
Do thou fear? as though it lay not in thyn own power to deliver thy self
when thou be disposed.

Rem facias palam. Utter, break, open, or disclose the matter, or out with it.

In the fourth Scene.
Me promissa huc induxerunt. Fair words have persuaded me to this: or, fair
promises brought me in mind to come hither. For the latin words may be
taken in both tho senses.

Quod si is nunc me deceperit, saepe obsecrans me ut ueniam, frustra ueniet.
That if he shall now begyle me, many a time hereafter, though he pray me
never so fair to come hither, he shall come in vain.

Cum me uenturum dixero, et constituero, cum is certo renun ciarit. When I
shall have said that I woll come, and shall have appointed when, and when he
shall have brought sure word again.

Cum spe, pendebit animi, ac non ueniam. He being in hope shall hang
perplexe, and I wol not come at al.

Syrus mihi tergo poenas pendet. Syrus shall aby on the bare skin for my
cause, or I shall cause Syrus bare rybbes to smarte for hit.

Satis site promittit tibi. He makes the me tely propre promises.

Atqui tu hunc iocari credis? faciet, nisi caueo. But thynkest thou that he
speaks in bourde? nay, he wol do it in deed, except I take heed.

Dormiunt, ego polistos commouebo. These fellows sleep, but in feyth I wolle
reyse them.

Audistin' homo iste modo quam uillam de mo^strauit proximam esse huic fundo ad
dextram? Dyddest thou here when this fellow showed me erewhyle of a manner
place, that is adioynant and lies next unto this ground here, on the right
hand?

Currendo percurre. Run every foot a great pace.

Dic me hic oppido inuitum esse, atque seruari. Say that I am here much
ageynste my will, and that I am so kept and holden here.

Dic me aliquo pacto uerba his daturum esse, et uenturu^. Sey that I wol some
weye or other deceive these folks, and come.

Quo mittis istunc quaeso? iube maneat. Whyther art thou sendyng him here I
prey the? command him to tarry stylle.

Est paratum argentum, atque iam dabitur. s. tibi. The money is ready, and
shall be delivered unto you by and by.

Vt lubet number ego insto? As thou wolt, for do I cry on the for it? or, chose
the, do I call on the, or make any ado for it?

Transeundum est nunc tibi ad illum. Thou must now go over to him, or to his
house.

Tua pompa eo traducenda est. Thou must take, convey, or bring over thither
with the al thy trayn. Pompa. pae, is any manner of glorious or solemn
oftentation or show, and that as well in prosperity as in a triumph, as also
in adversity, as in funeralles. Laur. Vall. li. 4. eleg. ca. 39.

Quam rem agis scelus? What entendeste thou, or what thing go thou about,
thou naughty fellow?

Argentum cudo, quod tibi them. I am quoynynge money, that I may give unto you.

Dignum me putas, quem illudas? Do thou think me a mete man for the to mock?

Non est temere. It is not for naught.

Etiam ne tecum hic res mihi est? Have thou also any thing to do with me here?

Tuum tibi reddo. I give you that that is yours.

Quid est rei? What is the matter?

Omneis eos traduc ad uos propere, et ferant quae secu^ huc attulerunt. Take
and convey them all to your house at ones, and let them take, here, or carry all
such things as they brought hither with them.

Spaerabit senex sumptum sibi leuatum esse horum abitu. The old man shall be in
hope, that his charges shall be diminished, lessed, or made lyghter by the goinge
away of these folks.

Nae ille haud scit, hoc paulum lucri, quantu^ ei damni apportet. In feyth full
little woteth he, how great loss and hyndrance this little small gaynes or
saving shall cause him to have, or to sustain.

Tu nescis, quod scis, si sapies. Thou know not that, that thou knoweste, if
thou wolt play the wise man.

In the fyfte scene.
Menedemi uicem miseret me. I have pity on the ill chance of Menedemus. We seye
in the singular nombre uicem, and uice, and no more, in the plurell nombre it
has al cases. Some grammarins derive and form uices, of the word ui, which
in old time was much taken and yet is pro pugnis, for fightinge: as whenne we
say in latyne. Vim mihi intulit, He laid violente hands on me, and fought
with me. Vim sibi intulit, He kylde him self. In the same signification is
uices used in Plaut, where he says: Vices eius memorat, et cicatrices denudat,
He tells of the battayles that he has foughten, and discovers or shows
open the scarres or print of the wounds that he had. Some times uices is taken
pro poenis, et inco^modis, for punishment and mischances, displeasures, or
adversity. Horat. li. 1. carm. Vices que superbe te maneant ipsum. Sometimes
pro periculo, for danger and ieoperdie.

Verg. Nec tela, nec ullas uitauisse uices Danaum. Sometimes pro loco for in the
place or stead. Horat. Vtar uice cotis, acutum red dear quae ferrum ualet exors
ipsa secandi, I woll be in the stead of a wheatstone, which has power to make
knynes sharp, and yet it felfe has no such propretie that it can cut. Laur.
Val. in annotac. contra Raud. touches the difference between Dolere alicuius
casum, and dolere uicem.

Miseret me, ta^tum deuenisse ad eum mali. It pitieth me, that so greatte a
mysfortune has chanced unto him.

Ita magno desyderio fuit ei filius. He has longed so greatly for his son.
Desydero, ras, raui, is to long for, and thereof comes desyderium.

Hosce aliquot dies non sentiet. For these few days he shall not feel it.

Verum ubi uidebit tantos sibi sumptus domi cotidianos fieri, nec fieri modum,
optabit rursum ut abeat ab se filius. But when he shall perceive, that he shall
be daily at so great charges, and therein like to be no mesure nor end, he
woll wysshe that his son were gone from him again. Antonius Nebr. in that his
book, which he entitled Lexicon iuris ciuilis, shows and proves, that
cotidie and cotidianus should be written with, c, and not with q. Ab, in
apposition is read not only set before words beginning with vowels, but also
with words beginning with almost al consonantes: as apud Plautum, Terentium,
Liuium, and others plainly appears.

Syrum optime eccum. Lo here is Syrus meruaylous well, or as well as can be.

Cesso hunc adoriri? Am I slack, or am I not quycke to set on him? Adoriri
is to come upon a man sodeinly, as though a wayt leyde before.

Te mihi iamdudum exoptabam dari. I desired to have the mete with me a good
while gone: or, I would very feign have had the mete with me a good while sens.
Of the use of Iamdudum, it is showed before.

Videre egisse iam nescio quid cu^ illo. Me thinks thou have ben in hand
with him about some thing what so ever it is.

Dictum ac factum reddidi. I dispatched it with a word, or, in the turnynge of
an hand (as who should say) I did no rather move the matter, nor speak of it,
but it was done by and by. Al be it Eras. in chiliadibus interprets and
expounds this proverb to signify all manner diligence and labor necessary
to the doing or bringynge to pass of any thing. For he says, that dictum ac
factu^ is a prouerbiall speaking, by which is signified, that we have not
omytted or let pass any thing belonging to the furtherance or perfourmaunce
of any matter or business. And his example he brings in this place of
Terence, which in that sense may be englisshed thus: I have done as much as
is possible, or as may be done in the matter. Donatus says, dictum ac factum
to be a prouerbial spekyng, be tokeninge celeritie and speed in doing or
dispatchynge of a thing. Teren. in Andr. ac. 2. sc. 3. Haec sola est mulier,
dictu^ ac factum, inuenerit aliquam causam, quam obrem eijciat oppido, This
glycerie is alone woman, Simo wol not fail, but at ones with the turnynge of
an hand, to find some quarelle or other, to drive her out of the town. And
in the first scene of the.5. act, in the same comedy, Dictum factum huc
abijt Clitipho, By and by comes thither Cliti.

Bona ne fide? s. fecisti. Dydst thou it feithfully, substantially, trustily,
or ernestly?

Non possum pati, quin caput tibi demulceam. I can not forbear, but I must
needs stroke thy heed. The same self thing may be said also by facere thus.
Non possum facere, quin caput tibi demulceam: And with out either of both
thus. Non possum quin caput tibi demulceam.

Faciam boni tibi aliquid pro ista re, ac lubens. I woll do the some good
turn for this that thou haste done, and that with all my heart.

Si scias quam site in mentem uenerit. If thou knew how proprely it came
in my mind.

Vah, gloriare euenisse ex sententia? Auant, do glory, crake, or make thine
auaunte, that it has chanced as thou would?

Non hercle uero, uerum dico. Nay in feith I sey troth. Hercle uero be
aduerbes of co^firmynge and auouchynge.

Ausculta quod superest fallaciae. Here that is behynd more of this subtilte or
falsheed.

Sese ipse dicet tuam uidisse filiam, eius sibi complacita^ forma^ postquam
aspexerit. Him self woll show you, that he has seen your daughter, and that
her beauty or favor lyked him well, when he saw her.

Dicet se filiam tuam cupere uxore^. He wol show you, that he would feign have
your daughter to his wife.

Equidem prorsus nihil intelligo. In feith I understand or perceive nothing at
al.

Vah, tardus es. Tushe thou art a dull fellow to understand or to perceine a
thing.

Argentum dabitur ei ad nuptias, aurum, atque uestem qui comparet. He shall
have money given him to his marriage, wherewith to bye clothe of gold and other
apparel. Aurum here is taken for clothe of gold, and so it is taken in diverse
places of Titus Liuius, and namely in the concions of Cato, and Lucius Valerius
li. 32. ab urbe condita. and Ioachimus Perionius notes the same.

Non ego perpetuo dicebam, ut illi dares, sed ut simulares. s. te daturum. I
seid not that thou should give it unto him for ever, but that thou should
feyne, show a countenance, or make as though thou would give it him.

Non mea est simulatio. I can no skyl of such feynynge, or, I can not make nor
show no such con^tenances.

Ita tu istaec tua misceto, ne me admisceas. Bring in or meddle of thin own
matters in such wise, that thou bring not me in among it.

Egon' cui daturus no^ sim, ut ei despondea^?. s. filiam. Should I betrouth or
make sure my daughter unto him, whom I woll not let or suffer to have her?
There is eclipsis of opor tet, decet, or conuenit.

Site poterat fieri. It might so have benne done proprely.

Ego hoc, quia dudum tu ta^topere iusseras, eo coepi. I began that for
because that thou had beaden me so greatly or so instantlye erewhyle.

Equidem isthuc aequi bonique facio. Truly I am nothing discontented therewith.
Aequi bonique facere, aequi bonique consulere, aequi consulere, and boni
consulere, been elegant manner of latin speakynges betokenyng the same, that we
say in english, to take well in worth, and in good gree. Examples been every
where innumerable.

Maxim uolo te dare operam ut fiat, uerum in alia uia. I woll in any wise that
thou do thy labor and diligence that it may be done, but yet after an other
way.

Illud quod tibi dixi de argento, quod iste debet Bacchidi. That that I spoke
to you of the money, which this man owes to Bacchis.

Argentum reddendum est illi. He must have his money again, or his money must be
paid unto him ayene.

Neque tu scilicet eo nunc confugies, quid mea?. s. refert. And in feithe thou
shalt not now have any refuge to that saying, (which some men use) what have
I to do therewith, or what perteyneth the matter to me?

Number mihi datum est argentum? Was the money delivered unto me?

Number ille oppiguerare filiam meam me inuito potuit? Might he lay my daughter
in pledge, or to gage, whether I would or not?

Verum illud dicunt. That is a true saying of men. Verum is a nown here in
this place.

Ius summum saepe summainiuria est. The rigor or the extremity of the law,
or of a man's right, is often times the greatest injury and wrong that may be.

Haud faciam. I wol not do it.

Alijs si licet, tibi non licet. Though other men may, yet thou may not:
or, though it be leful for others, yet it is not leful for the. Si for etsi.

Omnes te in lauta, er been aucta part putant. Al men think you to be one of
them, that live welthyly, and ben of great substance.

Quin egomet iam ad eum deferam. s. arge^tum. Marye I my self woll go bear it
unto him by and by.

Imo, filium iube potius. s. defer arge^tum. Nay, rather beade your son to
bear it.

Quia enim in hunc translata est suspicio. Mary because that the suspicion of
the matter is laid to him.

Videbitur magis uerisimile esse. It shall appear or seem to be more true like.

Facilius conficiam quod uolo. I shall the sooner and with more ease bring to
pass that that I would.

Ipse adeo adest, abi, effer argentum. He is even here now him self, go thy
ways, and bring forth the money.

In the sixte Scene.
Nulla est tam facilis res, quin difficilis sit, quam inuitus facias. There is
nothing so easy or light to be done, but that it is hard, if a man do it by
co^pulsion, or against his will

Vel haec deambulatio quam non laboriosa, me ad langorem dedit. Even the same
little walking, being nothing peynfull, or being a thing of no labor, yet
has made me all weary and faint. Vel in this place, is not a conjunction
disjunctive, but taken for etiam per enallagen partium. Ter. in Eu. Vel rex
mihi semper agebat gratias, Even the king him self did ever give me thanks.

Nec quicquam magis nunc metuo, quam ne denuo miser aliquo extrudar hinc. And I
fere nothing more now, then yt I shall be shifted or driven from hens some else
whither.

Vt te omnes dij cum istoc inuento, cumque incepto perdiunt. All the gods
take a vengeance upon the with this thy devise, and beginning. Perdiunt pro
perdant, with others like much used in old poets. Vt pro utinam, whereof it
is largely showed before.

Huiusmodi mihi res semper co^miniscere, ubi me excarnifices. Thou do always
deuyse and invent such thnges for me, to tourment and vex me withal. Vbi.i.
in quibus.

I tu hinc quo dignus es. Get the hense to the deuylle. The words sound
thus. Goo hens whyther thou art worthy to goo, (as who should sey) whyther
thou have deserved to go, and because they be used and spoken always in
indignation, they may be aptely and well englyshed, as before, for that is our
most used manner of speaking in english.

Quam pene tua me perdidit proteruitas. How nere thy saucynes had undone me,
or, how thy malopertnes had almooste utterly cast me awey. Of proteruus,
proteruitas, or proteruia, procax, procacitas, petulans, and petulantia read L.
Val. li. 4. eleg. ca. 105.

Vellem hercle factum. In feyth I would it had been so.

Ne me istuc ex te prius audisse gaudeo, quam argentum haberes, quod daturus
iam fui. In feyth I am glad that I have herd that word of thy mouth,
before thou had received the money, which I was ready and about to deliver
now by and by. It may be said ex te audisse, a te audisse, and de te audisse,
as shows Lau. Vall. li. z. eleg. ca. 66.

Iam non sum iratus. Now I am not angry.

Scin' ubi nunc sit tibi tua Bacchis? Do thou know or can thou tell where
now to find thy best beloue Bacchis?

Bono animo es. Be of good chere.

Ludis fortasse me. Percase thou mockest or bourdest with me.

Ipsa re experibere. Thou shalt prove or se it in very deed. It is alredye
showed that in old time verbes of the fourth coniugacion fourmed their future
tenses of the indicatife mode in bo, and bor.

Ne ego fortunatus homo sum. Truly I am a fortunate fellow, or truly I have
good chance and fortune.

Deamo te Sire O Sire I love the with all mi heart. Deamo.i. ualde amo. For the
preposition de, in composition somtymes betokens encreasynge, al be it most
times it signifies diminution or privation.

Obsecundato in loco. Be ruled or follow the appetite of a man at some time.
For locus in such manner speakinges is taken and used also pro tempore, et pro
re. Obsecundare est obtemperare, obsequi et omnia ad alterius uelut nutum
facere.

Cave quidquam admiratus sies. Beware that thou make no maruailing at the
matter.

Quod imperabit, facito. What he shall bead or command the to do, do it.

Loquitor paucula. Say lyttel, or speak but few words.

In the seventh Scene.
Vbi Clitipho nunc est? Where is Clitipho now?

Eccum me, inque. Here I am sir, say thou.

Quid rei esset, dixti huic? Have thou showed him here what the matter is?

Dixi plaeraque omnia. s. ei. I have told him the most part of al.

Cape hoc argentum, ac defer. s. ad eum. Have here this money, and goo bear
hit unto him.

Hei quid stas lapis? Alas why stand thou deed stone.

Quin accipis? s. argentum. Why do thou not take it? Quin, pro quid non.i.
cur non.

Sequere hac me ocyus. Com after me this way at ones, apace, or quyckly.

Tu hic nos, du^ eximus, interea operibere. Thou shalt tarry here for us in
the mean while, till we come forth.

Nihil est illic quod moremur diutius. There is no cause why for us to tarry
there any long space.

Minas quidem iam december habet a me filia. My daughter has there now alredye
of me ten pounds sterlyng. Mina in Athenes and the countreye of Greece
theraboute was as much in su^me as libra in the city of Rome, and Libra
romana was equalle with our pound sterlynge.

December minas pro alimentis esse nunc duco datas. Ten pounds I reckon now as
given or paid for her table.

Hasce pro ornamentis consequentur alterae. s. december minae. After them must go
other ten pounds for her apparel.

Porro haec talenta dotis apposcent duo. And these two wolle require besides
and above that one hundrede pounds to her marriage. Dos dotis proprely is the
money that is given with any woman, or that she brings with her to her
marriage, and by translation it is taken for any gift of nature or other wise:
as prudence, justice, and fortitude, with other virtues be called dotes animi:
beauty, strength, stature, ben dotes corporis: so dotes ingenij. etc. Of
talentum it is showed before. Note that here is set haec for hae plurally, and
the neutre gendre for the feminine. For next before went alterae december minae.
So Terence before in Enucho, speaking de ancillis Thaidis. Continuo haec
adornant, ut lauet, pro hae adornant. Anon the maidens made al things ready,
that she might wash. Idem in Phorm. Haec illae erant itiones. pro hae illae
erant itiones, This was all the goinges in and out. And the same noten both
Donatus and Io. Calphurnius.

Mihi nunc. relictis omnibus, inueniendus est aliquis, labor inue^ta, mea cui
them bona. I must be feign now, all other things laid aside, to find some
man, unto whom to give my goods, gotten with great labor. Labor inuenta.i.
parta, uel parata.

Multo omnium nunc me fortunatissimum factum puto esse gnate, cum te intelligo
resipisse. pro resipuisse. I think my self becomed the most fortunate and
happy of all men, now that I see the my son returned and come again to
goodness.

Vt errat. How greatly he is deceived, or how far he is out of the way.

Teipsum quaerebam Chreme. I sought for you, and no man else Chremes.

Cedo, quid uis? Tell me, what would thou have? or, show me what is thy will?

Quid tu hominis es? What a fellow art thou? Of such manner latyne speakynges
it is noted before.

Iam ne oblitus es, inter nos quid sit dictu^? Haste thou now sens forgotten
what was said between us?

Ea res nunc agitur ipsa. Even very that, we go about now.

Erraui. I was beguiled, or deceived.

Sic res acta est. It was even right so.

Quanta spe decidi? Out of how greatte hope am I fallen?

Id est profecto. It is even that surely.

Frustra gauisus sum miser. I was glad for nothing unhappy body that I am.

Quiduis iam malo, quam hunc amittere. I woll now suffer any mysaduenture in
the world, rather then lose him here, or, have him go from me. Quiduis iam
malo. s. facere ac pati.

Quid nunc renunciem abs te responsum? What shall I bear word ayene now,
that thou haste answered?

Ne sentiat me sensisse, atque aegre ferat. Lest that he epsy me to have
perceived the matter, and so be discontented.

Nimium isti indulges. You make to much of him, or you cocker him to much, or
you suffer him to much to take his pleasure, and to have his own mind.

Inceptum est, perfice hoc mihi perpetuo. The matter is already ones entered and
begone, go me through with it still.

Dic egisse te de nuptijs. s. mecum. Sey that you have been in hand with me for
or about the marriage.

Dica^, quid deinde? I wol, what then after?

Dic me facturum esse omnia. Seye that I wol do al things.

Dic generum placere. s. mihi. Sey that my son in law lyketh me well.

Isthuc uolueram. That was my desire.

Quam ocyssime ut des. That thou may give it very hastily.

Nae tu propediem istius obsaturabere. In feyth thou shalt soon or shortly
have thy belly full of that gere.

Haec cautim et paulatim dabis si sapies. Thou shalt give these things
warely, and by lyttel and little, if thou wolte play the wise manne.

Abi intro, uide quid postulent. Go thy way in, and se what they lack, or what
they would have. Postulo. las, is proprely to ask or to require.

Ego domi ero, si quid me uoles. I wol be at home, if thou wolt any thing with
me.

Te sciente faciam, quicquid egero. Thou shall be of couunsayle, what so ever I
shall do, or I wol do nothing, but the being of counsel. For Te sciente is
the ablative case absolute.

Out of the fyfte Act in the first Scene.
Ego me non tam astutum, neque ita perspicacem esse scio. I know my self not
to be so crafty, or wily, ne so quyck in perceiuyng or espienge of things.

Hic adiutor meus, et monitor, et praemonstrator, hoc, mihi praestat. This mine
helper, and counseylour, and leader or poynter of the way is above me, or
better than I in this point. Praemonstrator, toris, is he that goes before a man to guide him, and to show him the way, and as who should say, to lead
him by the hand.

In me quiduis harum rerum conuenit, quae sunt dicta in stultum, caudex,
stipes, asinus, plumbeus. All those things may well be said unto me, that be
commonly said against a fool, as to be called a block, or a stumpe, a stock,
an ass, an heuyheed: as who should say, I may well be called a block, a
stock, an ass, a dulheed, with all other such names as be given to foolyshe
and dulle fellows, in despite or in derision. Caudex is the foot and lowest
part of the stock of a tree next unto the roots. Stipes, stipitis, is a
logge, that is set fast in the ground. Plumbum, by, is latin for leed:
thereof plumbeus, bea, beum, a thing made of the metal of lead: and by
translation a man, or any other thing of the propretie and condition of lead,
that is to wit, heavy, blunt, and dulle: whereof is taken a latin proverb:
Plumbeo iugulare gladio, to cut ones throat with a sword of leed, that is to
sey, to go about and to labor to overcome or to convince a man with a vain,
light, and slender reason or argument. The proverb Cicero does use.

In illum nil potest. s. dici. Nothing can be said against him.

Exuperat eius stultitia haec omnia. The folyshnes of him posseth all this.

Desine deos gratulando obrundere. Leave or forbear to dulle the goddis with
geuyng thanks to them for thy good chance or fortune. Gratulor, laris, has
two significations, the one is to make joy, and to say or to testefie in
words, that thou art glad of the good fortune or happy chance, either of an
other man, orels of thyn own self. And in this signification Gratulor wol
have after him a dative case of the party, for whose cause such ioyinge or
testification of gladness is made, whether it be thy self, or else any other
body: as Gratulor tibi, quod tam gratiosus sis apud principem, I am glad,
that you be in such high favor with your prince. Gratulor mihi cui hoc
saeculo tam li terato nasci contigerit, I am glad on mine own behalf, that it
was my chance to be borne in this time, when letters so well flouryshen. Where
note, that gratulor, besides the said dative case, may have after him also, of
the thing that you allege, wherefore you sey that you be glad, an accusative
without a preposition, or an accusative with the preposition, ob, orels an
ablative with the preposition de: as I am glad that you have that office,
Gratulor tibi istum magistratum, or, Gratulor tibi ob istum magistratum, or else
Gratulor tibi de isto magistrato. So Gratulor tibi nouum istum honorem, or ob
nouum istum honorem, or de nouo isto honor, I am glad on your behalf, of
this your new promocion. Al be it the poets sometimes leave out the dative
case, especially when it is a pronown: as Ovid. in ep. Gratulor Oechalia^
titulis accedere nostris. Where the dative is understood, which may be tibi,
mihi, or nobis. Id. de art am. Prisca iuuent alios, ego me nu^c denique natum
Gratulor: Where is understood mihi. Some times also in orators, the dative
is not expressed, but omitted and understood. Quin til. in pasc. cadauer.
Gratulemur iam, quod nulla ciuitas fame laboret, where is understood nobis.
And by reason of such omission of the dative case some grammarians have thought
gratulari idem esse quod gaudere, to joy, to rejoice, or to he glad, alleging
for their authority the examples above written: But Laur. Vall. li. 5. eleg.
cap. 42. does not allow them, well considering, that gratulation may be,
where not only no joy or gladness is, but also entier and hearty sorrow, as a
man may say or testify that he is glad of the promocion of an other man,
(which is in latin, Gratulari alicui noua^ dignitatem) and yet in his heart
be right sorye for the same. Gratulor is also taken sometime for gratias agere,
to give thanks, but that is in manner never, but to the gods immortal. So
that in this signification it is the same that supplicare, to pray and to do
our deuocions to the gods. Titus Liuius. Triumphantes in capitolium
ascendebant, Ioui optimo Maximo, caeterisque dijs gratulatum, Such persons as
triumphed in old time in the cite of Rome used to ascend or to go up into the
Capitole castle, or tower of the city, to give devout thanks unto the most
good, and most mighty Jupiter, and unto the other gods. So that Gratulari Deo
et superis, is to give devout thanks unto god and to his saynctes for any
benefyte, felicity, or happy chance received. And so is gratulando taken here
in this place of Terence, and there is understood eis nempe dijs.

Desine obtundere, nisi illos, ex tuo ingenio iudicas, ut nihil credas
intelligere, nisi idem dictum est centies. Leave dullyng them, except thou
esteem and judge them of thine own propretie, that is, that thou thynkeste
them to perceive or understand nothing, but if the same have been spoken,
repeted, or rehearsed an hundred times.

Quid risisti? Wherefore dyddest thou laugh?

Serui uenere in mentem Syri calliditates. I remembered, or I thought upon the
subtyle or wily deuyses of my servant Syrus.

Itane? Yea, or in deed.

Vultus quoque homninu^ fingit scelus. The ungracious harlotte can facion or
transpose also the countenances and lokes of men. Scelus for scelestus per
emphasin, aut potius auresin, as is showed before. Io. Rinius in his
castigations upon Terence notes these words in this place, and exponneth the
same in such wise that he takes the sense and meaning of them to be this.
Vultus quoque hominum fingit scelus, The ungracious fellow can also transpose or
facion the countenances of men (that is) can make or cause men to look and to
have such countenances as him lysteth, meaning by Syrus, that he had in such
wise taught and enstructed Clinia, that he could facion his look and
countenance at his pleasure, and countrefeate any facion of countenance that
he would him self. And in this sense it best agreth with the words next
following, which ben these. Gnatus quod se adsimulat laetum, id dicis, That my
son makes a countenance as he were glad speak you of that? And fingere
uultum, in the self same sense uses Caesar. l. i. bell. Gall. where he says
thus. Hi neque uultu^ fingere, neque interdum lacrumas tenere poterant, These
men could neither feyne or make a good countenance, nor yet sometimes hold or
forbear weeping (as who should say) they could not hyde nor dissemble their
fere, but that there appeared in their faces and cou^tenances evident notes and
tokens* that in their minds and hearts they were soore troubled and vexed, and
aferde of punysshement. And of the same comes a prouerbiall spekyng: Fortuna^
sibi quisque fingit, Every man makes (as who should say) is causer of his own
fortune be it good or bad, as who should say, they that be good or virtuous or
learned, or have any honest craft or occupation, shall have good fortune, and
shall be sustained thereby, and live well, and contrary wise they that be idle
fellows, nor have any learning, nor occupation, but be flagicious and full of
myschefe, shall not live well, but in misery, whereof they may always thank
them selfs, as causers of their own fortune.

Idem istuc mihi uenit in mentem. I remembered, or I thought upon even the self
same thing.

Magis, si magis noris, putes ita rem esse. Thou would much more think it
to be so, if thou dyddest better know the matter.

Ain tu? Say thou so?

Hoc prius scire expeto. This I desire to knew first.

Mira narras. Thou tellist a merueilus tale. Mira the acusatife plurel, neutre
gendre, put substantiuely.

Quid est, quod amplius simuletur? What is there that is feyned more than this?

Est mihi retro ultimus in aedibus conclaue quoddam. I hane a certain parlour
behind, or in the back side in the furmooste part of all my house.

Lectus uestimentis stratus est. There be clothes laid upon the bed.

Quid post quam hoc est factu^? What, when that was done?

Huc abijt Clitipho, Bacchis consecuta est illico. Thither goes Clitipho, and
after goes Bacchis at the hard heles of him. Consequi is to follow, as we use
to say on english, cheek by cheke, or at the hard heels. etc.

Vbi abiere intro, operuere ostiu^. As soon as they were gotten in, they
closed or shut to the door.

Clinia haec fieri uidebat? Did Clinia se all this done?

Quid ni? mecum una simul. What else? even to gither with me. Quid ni. s.
uiderit. Vna simul it is the figure plonasmus, of which it is showed before.

December dierum uix mihi est familia. My houwseholde may scacely endure or hold
ten days longer, (as who should say) I shall not be able to continue
householdyng or keeping of an house ten days longer.

Ille operam amico dat suo. He atte^deth upon his friend.

An dubium id est tibi? Haste thou any dout of that?

Quenquam animo tam comi esse, aut leni putas? Thynkest thou any man to be of
so familiar, or so gentyl in heart? Hic haec comis et hoc come, is he that is
gentyl and familiar and nothing statelyke nor high minded or strange. Hic
haec lenis, hoc lene, is he that is not rough nor sharp, nor soon moved to
anger, but is gentyl and sone contented or pleased, and is full of sufferance.

Quo uerba facilius dentur mihi. That I may the sooner be deceived.

Merito mihi nunc ego succenseo. I am angry with my self now, not without a
cause.

Quot res dedere, ubi possim percentiscere, nisi essem lapis. How many things
have they done, whereby I might have perceived it, if I had not ben a very stone.

Anne illud inultum fere^t? Shall they escape with it vpnunished?

Non tu te cohibes? Wolt thou not refreine or keep thy self in?

Non tu te respicis? Do thou nothing regard thy self?

Non ego tibi exempli satis sum? Am not I example good enough for the?

Prae iracundia non sum apud me. I am out of my wit for anger.

Te ne isthuc loqui?. s. decet, conuenit, uel oportet. Such a word to come
out of thy mouth?

Nonne id flagitium est, te alijs consilium dare, foris sapere, tibi no^ posse
auxiliarier? Is not this a very noughtye thing, that thou canste give
counsaylle unto others, and to be wise in other men's mattiers, and not to be
able to help or ease thine own self?

Fac te esse patrem sentiat. So do that he may perceive and feel the to be his
father.

Fac ut audeat tibi credere oi^a. So do that he may commit and show v^nto the
al things. Credere alicui in this significacion is to commytte unto any man al
secrets, and to keep nor hyde nothing from him.

Fac ne quam aliam quaerat copiam. So do that he may not seek for any other
help.

Fac ne te deserat. So do that he may not forsake the.

Imo abeat potius multo, quouis gentium, quam hic per flagitium, ad inopiam
redigat patrem. Nay mary let him go from hens to the worlds end, much
rather then he should here through his michiefe and ungracious facion of
living, undo his father, and bring him to poverty and need or beggary. Of this
word Gentium how it is added unto diverse aduerbes, hit is largely declared
before.

Si illi perga^ suppeditare sump tibus, mihi il laec uere ad rastros res redit.
If I should give him asmoche money, as he would spend, that would surely bring
me to the rake and the spade: as who should say, it would undo me, and make me
a very beggar in deed: and it is elegantly said in latin, Res mihi ad rastros
redit, I must be feign to take a rake and a spade in my hand, (as who should
sey) to dygge and delfe for my living. Suppeditare illi sumptibus, may
otherwise also be said in latyne, Suppeditare illi sumptus, and this later is
more used.

Quot inco^moditates in hac re accipies, nisi caves? How many incommodities or
displeasures shall thou have in this thing, except thou be ware or take heed?
All be it Io. Riuius in his castigations redeth, Quot incommoda tibi in hac re
capies. etc.

Difficilem ostendis te esse, ignosces tamen post. Thou show thy self
hard, sore, or heavy toward him, yet thou wolt forgive or pardone him at
last. Post.i. postea, and it is here an aduerbe and not a preposition, for it
governs no case, whereof also it is annoted before.

Nescis quam, doleam. Thou know not how sorry I am.

Quid obticuisti? Why do thou not speak? Of the propre signification of
Obticeo, it is showed before.

Ita dico. So I say.

Ne quid uereare. Be not aferde, or fear nothing.

Nil dos nos mouet. We care nothing for any money to her marriage.

Duo talenta pro re nostra ego esse decreuisatis. I have determined or judged
that one hundred pounds is enough for one of my substance.

Ita dictu opus est, si me uis saluum esse, et rem, et filium. Thou must needs
so say, if thou wolte have my life saved, and my goods and my son. Here is
the figure of construction called zeugma, which is when a verb or an
adjective is reduced and referred unto diverse a nominative cases or
substantives, that is to wit, unto the nominative or substantive that is
next unto it expressly, and unto all the others, by understanding or
repetyng: as Ego et tu uiuis, I and thou live. Where uiuis is reduced and
referred unto two nominative cases, that is to wit ego and tu. And with the
next, that is tu, it accords in number and person expressly, and with the
further that is ego, by understanding thus. Ego. s. uiuo, et tu uiuis. So
coeli mouendi sunt, et terra. Where mouendi and sunt accorden with the next,
that is coeli expressly, that is to say, the verb in noumbre and person,
and the adjective in case, gendre, and number. And with the further terra, they
both accorden not, but by understanding thus, Coeli mouendi sunt, et terra.
s. mouenda est. So Virgil. Hic illius arma, hic currus fuit. Where the sense
and oration is thus to be made perfect. Hic illius arma. s. fuerunt: Hic currus
fuit. etc. Again Ego et uxor mea est docta. The perfect speking is this, Ego.
s. sum doctus, et uxor mea est docta, or else Ego sum doctus, et uxor. s. est
docta. Yet this not withstanding, if there be any comparatio^, the verb or
the adjective may accord with the further. As in example we say not Ego melius
quam tu scribis, but Ego melius quam tu, scribo, I write better then thou. Like
wise if there be any similitude, the verb or adjective shall accord with the
further nominative or substantive: as Ego sicut tu sentio, and not ego sicut tu
sentis, I think as thou do, or I am of the mind that thou art. And in the
psalm Ego sicut foenum arui, and not ego sicut foenum aruit. Also by the
conjunction nisi, thus. Talem filium nulla nisi tu pareret, and not pareres:
but by understanding. And here note, that zeugma, may be three manier ways.
First in person, as Ego et tu uiuis: Secondarily in gendre, as Rex et regina
est irata: Thirdely in number, as Cic. Nihil te hic munitissimus habendi
senatus locus, nihil horum ora, uultus que mouerunt? Has this most strong
place, that the parlyament is kept in, and the faces or countenances or lokes
of these men, being present and beholdynge the, moved the nothing at all? Note
furth^r, that there be three kinds of zeugma. One is called in greek
protozeugma, that is when the verb or the adjective is set in the
beginning: as Dormio ego et tu. An other is called mesozeugma, which is when
the verb or the adjective is set in the mids: as Ego dormio et tu. The
third is called hypozeugma, and that is where the verb is put in the end: as
Ego et tu dormis. But when a verb or an adjective is reduced unto diverse
nominative cases or substantives, and agrees with them both, the grammarians
callen it zeugma locutionis, of speaking, non constructionis, and not of
construction. As in the example of Cicero before, Nihil horum ora uultusque
mouerunt. Ageyn. Ioannes est eruditus et petrus. Zeugma requires four
things: First diverse substantives, as Rex et regina: Secondarily a
conjunction, as et. Thirdely a verb or an adjective, as irata est. Fourthly
that the same verb or adiectife do agree expressly with the next noiatiue or
substa^tiue, as Rex et regina est irata. Where note that the conjunction may be
either a coniunctife, a disiunctife, or else an expletiue, it may be also an
aduerbe instead of a conjunction: as Cubas ubi ego, Thou lie where I do.
Coenas quando nos, Thou suppest when we do. Haec fere de zeugmate Gerardus
Listrius.

Id mirari te simulato, et illum rogato simul quamobrem id faciam. Make as
though thou dyddest marvel thereat, and together there withal demand of
him, wherefore I do it.

Ego uero, quamobrem id facias, nescio. And in deed to, I know not wherefore
thou do hit.

Ego id facio, ut eius animu^ qui nunc luxuria et lasciuia diffluit retundam. I
do it that I may clean dasshe his heart or mind, which now runs at
rouers in riot and wantonness. Diffluere proprely is to ru^ne abroad in diverse
places, as water does, and by translation it is to be ryottous and wild,
rounnynge at large, nothing refreynynge him self. Semblably retundere
proprely is to make dulle or blunt as in a knyfe, when the point or edge of it
is turned. And the same by translation is to abate, to bring down, and (as
we use much to say now) to dasshe, to blanke, and to appall. Luxuria, riae,
and luxus, xus, xui, is latin for riot, and not for lechery. Libido, bidinis,
is latin for lechery, and lasciuia perteyneth to the same. For lasciuus
propreli is he that is lecherous both in living and also in words. Ex Laur.
Vall.

Redigam eum, ut quo se uertat nesciat. I wolle bring him to that point, that
he shall not wotte whyther to turn him. A child may marvel why Terence
says not nesciet, rather then nesciat, saying that our manner of speaking in
english so sowneth as though it should be the future tense of the indicative
mode. But it is to be noted, that this particle ut, is never joined with verbes
of the indicative mode. For ut nesciat in this manner speaking, is the same
that barbarous, rude, and unlearned persons say thus, quod nesciet, but the
latin phrase is otherwise. Therefore in this manner speaking with others like it
is necessary to put the present tense of the subiunctiue mode instead of
the future of the indicative mode. And this is also noted in the little book
De corrupti sermonis emendatione. And this example of Terence is there brought
in, Quo se uertat,.i. quo se uertere debeat, it is the potencial mode.

Mitte me. Let me go, or let me alone.

Sine me in hac re gerere mihi more^. Suffer me in this thing to follow mine own
appetite, or to have mine own mind and pleasure.

Ita ne uis? Wolte thou so? or is that thy mind?

Vxorem accersat. Let him send for his wife.

Dictis confutabitur. He shall be convinced or blanked with words or reasons.
Confuto, tas, is to avoid a man in such wise, that he can nothing more
allege nor say for him self.

Eum si uiuo, adeo exornatum dabo, adeo depexum, ut, dum uiuat, meminerit
semper mei. If I live, I woll so beraye him, and so deck or trymme him, that
as long as he lives, he shall ever remember me, or thynk on me. Depexus, xa,
xum, comes of depecto, which is diligenter pecto, to kembe diligently. Ovid.
l. z. fast. Interea liber depexos crinibus indos Vicit, et eoo dives ab orb
uenit. Per metaphora^, uel potius ironeiam, it is taken and used in the ill
part. So that de pexus, which is proprely well kembed, and having a bushe well
decked, is taken for the contrary, that is ill arayde and ill handled.

Sibi me pro ridiculo, ac delectamento putat. He reputeth me as his laughing
stock, and as one to make him sport and pasty me.

Non auderet haec facere uiduae mulieri, quae in me fecit. He durst not have
done unto a wydowe, or a lone womanne, that he has done ageynste me. Vidua,
duae, is a lone woman and a wydowe, whose husband is decesed: and because
women (especially such as have nohusbandes to help and defend them, from
injuries and wrongs) for the most part be nothing set by, but had in
contempt, and reputed as abiectes and vile persons, which no man cares for,
nor fereth to delude and mock, therefore he uses here that comparison and
example.

In the second Scene.
Omnem de me eiecit animum. He has cast all his mind away from me.

Quodnam ob facinus? For what act, deed, or trespass?

Quid ego tantum sceleris admisi miser? What so great and heinous trespace have
I perpetrate or done unhappy bodi that I am?

Vulgo id faciunt. They do it commonly, (as who should say) every body uses to
do this thing that I have done.

Scio tibi esse hoc grauius multo, ac durius. I know that this thing is to
the much more grievous and hard.

Ego haud minus aegre patior. I take the matter even as grievously.

Nescio, nec rationem capio. I know it not, nor I perceive nor understand not
the reason of it.

Tibi been ex animo uolo. I would the well with all my heart.

Hic patrem astare aiebas? Stood my father here, saydest thou?

Quid me incusas? Why blamest thou me? or why layeste thou fault or blame in
me?

Quicquid ego huius feci, tibi prospexi, et stultitiae tuae. What so ever I
have done in this matter, I did it to help the and thy folyshenes. Prospicere
is to look or to se unto, and to provide or shyft for.

Te uidi animo esse omisso. I saw or marked the to be of a negligente or
rechelesse mind. Omisso.i. negligenti. So in Adelphis. At enim metuas, ne ab
re sint omissiores paulo, Marye but percase thou wolte fear and cast peryls
that they should be negligent about their own profyt, and about getting
money to sustain them or to live by.

Vidi te, suauia in praesentia quae essent, prima habere, neque consulere in
longitudinem. I sal^e or marked the to set most by tho things which be
sweet, pleasant, and delectable for the while, and not to provide for any
space or long time to come.

Coepi rationem, ut neque egeres, neque haec posses perdere. I begone to take
such a way, that neither thou might be brought to poverty, need, or
penury, nor yet myghtest lose this that thou haste.

Eis commisi et credidi. I have committed and put all the matter to them.

Ibi tuae stultitiae semper crit praesidium. There shall be always a refuge and
place of succor for thy folyshenes.

Disperij, scelestus quantas turbas conciui insciens? I am undone ungracious
fellow that I am? how great troublous busynes have I procured vnwittynge?

Emori cupio. I would feign dye, or I would I were out of the world, or I would
gladly be out of my life.

Prius, queso, disce quid sit uiuere, ubi scies, si displicebit uita, tum
isthoc utitor. I pray the, first learn, what it is to live in deed, and after
that thou shalt know it, then if good life shall myslyke the, then use this
facion, that thou do now. Viuere, is here taken for secundum uirtutem
uiuere, to live after the rule of virtue. And uita here is taken for actus et
mores, as who should say, the life actife, moral, and virtuous. So in Phorm.
Quem ego in uita uidi optimum, Whom I have seen meruaylous good, honest, and
virtuous of living.

Quae ista est prauitas, quae ue amentia? What naughty facion is this, or what
madness?

Quod peccaui ego, id ob est huic. That that I have offended, hurts him here.

Ne te admisce. Meddle not thou in the matter. Nae an aduerbe of forbyddynge may
be joined with verbes of the imperatife mode, or of the subiunctife mode
indifferentely. Non in the same signification is joined with verbes of the
subiunctife mode only, and not of the imperative mode.

Nemo te accusat. No man accuses, blames, or appecheth the, or, no man
compleyneth on the.

Nec tu aram, tibi neque precatorem pararis. Neither take seynctuarie for the
matter, ne yet provide get or make any spokesman, peticioner, or intercessor
for the. Pararis for paraueris, per syncopen. And hit is the future tense of
the subiunctiue mode set for the imperative mode.

Nihil succenseo tibi. I am nothing angry with the.

Rogasse uellem unde mihi petere^ cibum. I would I had asked of him, where I
should require my meat and drink.

Nos abalienauit. He has given us over, or, he has cast us of, or, he has
forbeaden us his house.

Irrides in re tanta, neque me quicquam co^silio adiuuas? Do thou mock or
jest in so great a matter, nor helpest me nothing at all with thy counsel?

Ego dicam quod mihi in mentem, tu diiudica. I woll say that is in my mind,
and than judge thou. In mentem. s. uenir, or else in mentem. s. est for in mente
est, per antiptosin, albeit in the mergyn of Basyl imprintynge it is noted (as
founden read in some exempleries) not in mente^, but in mente est.

Inuenta est causa, qua te expellerent. There was a quarrel found, whereby they
might drive or thrust the out.

Est uerisimile. It is like so.

An tu ob hoc peccatum, esse illum iratum putas? Do thou suppose or think
that he is angry for this offense?

Non arbitror. I think not.

Nunc aliud specta. Now mark an other thing, or yet more over consider an
other thing. For I think nunc here to be taken for iam, which very often and
many times is taken for praeterea, insuper, ad haec, Furthermore besides all this. etc.

Matres omnes filijs in peccato adiutrices solent esse. Al mothers vsen or ben
wont to help their sons, when they done any faut.

Matres filiis auxilio in paterna iniuria solent esse. Mothers are wont to
help their children, when their fathers do the^ wrong, or be unreasonable.

Id non fit. That is not done.

Suspitionem istam ex illis quere. Dema^de of them and enquire the cause of
this suspicion.

Rem profer palam. Utter the mattier plainly.

Si non est uerum, ad misericordiam ambos adduces cito. If it be not true, thou
shalt anon move and bring them both to pity and compassion.

Recte suades. Thou gyuest me good counsaylle.

Sat recte hoc mihi in mentem uenit. This camme very well unto my mind or
remembrance.

Eum precatorem mihi paro. Him woll I get to be spokesman, peticioner, or
intercessour for me.

Seni nostro fidei nihil habeo. I trust our old man nere a deal, or I have
no trust or affiance at al in the old man my master.

In the third Scene.
Profecto, nisi caves tu homo, aliquid conficies mali. Undoubtedly, except
thou beware thou fellow, thou shalt work or bring up some myschife.

Miror quomodo tam ineptum quidquam potuerit tibi uenire in mentem. I marvel
how any so folyshe a thing could come unto thy mind.

Pergin' mulier esse? Wolt thou be a woman still? as who should say. Wolte
thou never leave thy woman's conditions?

Vllam ne ego rem unquam uolui, quin tu in ea re mihi aduersator fueris? Was I
ever minded or wyllyng to have done any thing, but that thou haste resisted and
been ageynste me therein? Terence in the text, speaking in the person of
Chremes unto Softrata being a woman, says aduersatrix.

Si rogitem iam, quid est quod peccem, aut quamobrem hoc faciam, nescias. If I
would now ask of the, what it is wherein I do amysse, or wherefore I do this
same, thou coudest not tell.

In qua re nu^c tam confidenter restas stulte? In what thing do thou now
withstand me, or strive, and strogle against me, so boldly, or so malopertly
folyshe fellow?

Iniquus es, qui me tacere de re tanta postules. Thou art unjust or unreasonable
that do require, or that would have me to hold my peace and say nothing,
in so great a matter.

Nihilo minus ego hoc faciam tamen. Yet nevertheless I will do this same.

Non uides quantum mali ex ea re excites? Do thou not se how much myschiefe
thou areasest thereby?

Subditum se suspicatur. He mystrusteth him self that he was some
chaungelynge. Subditum.i. subdititium uel suppositum. Suppositus, ta, tum, a
chaungelynge, or a child, that was changed in the cradle, and laid there for
the very true child. Iuuenalis. Transeo suppositos, I pass over and speak
nothing of chaungelynges, or such as were changed in their cradels, as we
say in englysh. Of the same signification is subdititius and here in this
place subditus.

Isthuc inimicis siet. Such chance come to our enemies.

Ego ne confitear meum non esse filium, qui sit meus? Should I confess, or say,
that he is not my son, which in deed is mine?

Quid metuis? What fear thou? or whereof art thou aferde?

Conuinces facile ex te natum, nam tui similis est probe. Thou shalt sone or
eathly prove him to be of thyn own body borne, for he is very like unto the of
conditions. Diverse grammaryans have noted, that similis when it governs a
datyfe case, betokens like in favor, and when it governs a genetyue, it
betokens like of manners and co^dicions. Which observation here in this and
other places (for the more part) is true, but yet not every where.

Illi nihil uitij est relictum, quin id itidem sit tibi. He has no fault or
vice left, but that thou haste even the same also.

Talem nulla nisi tu pareret filium. No woman alive could have brought forth
such a son, but thou. Here is the figure zeugma, of which it is diligently
noted a little before, and this same clause of Terence there recyted.

In the fourth Scene.
Si unquam ullum fuit tempus, cum ego uoluptati tibi fuerim, obsecro, eius ut
memineris. If ever there was any time, that you had any delight or pleasure in
me, I beseech you for goddis sake to call it to mind and remembrance.

Inopis te miserescat mei. Have thou pity or compassion upon me, being a pore
fellow, and without any manner help. For that signifies proprely inops.

Peto parents meos ut commonstres mihi. I desire you that you woll show unto
me my parents, or my father and mother.

Ne isthuc animum inducas tuum. Never think that.

Hoccine quesisti obsecro? Haste thou asked such a question of me I pray the?

Cave posthac, si me amas, unquam isthuc uerbum ex te audiam. Beware if thou
love me, that I never here that word of thy mouth again.

Cave mores posthac in te esse istos sentia^. Beware that I never se or
perceive to be in the again from henseforth such manners or behauour as thou
have used.

Si scire uis, ego dicam. If thou be willing or desirous to know, I wol tell
the.

Nostrum te esse credito. Think thy self to be our own son. So hit is
taken here in this place of Terence, but it may be englished in an other
sense, thus: Think thy self to be our own (as who should say) our friend and in
our good favor.

Non sunt haec parentis dicta. These be not mete words of a father to his
son.

Non, si ex capite sis meo natus itidem ut aiunt Mineruam esse ex Jove, ea
causa magis patiar flagitiis tuis me infamen fieri. Although thou were borne
out of my very heed even so, as they say that Minerua was born out of the heed
and brayn of Iuppiter, yet I wol not, any thing the rather for that cause,
suffer my self to be in infamy and obloquie of men, or to incur and run
in sclaunder of men, by reason of thy abhomynable vices or naughtye facions.
Flagitijs, is ablatiuus causae. Minerua is the goddess of wisdom, of artis
and sciences, and wars, and it is metaphoricalli taken sometimes for nature,
for wit, or for any art or science. Which things because they be high gifts
and only given by god, the poetis feign, that Minerua was the daughter of
Jupiter, and delivered or borne out of his own heed or brain. Whereof Lucianus
the greek poet has a very pretty dialoge in dialogis deorum superu^.

Dij isthaec prohibeant. The gods forbid or defend those things.

Ego, quoth potero, euitar sedulo. I wol do my busy labor as much as I shall be
able to do.

Quaeris id, quod habes, parents, quod abest, non queris, patri quomodo
obsequare, et ut serves, quod labor inuenerit. Thou sekest that, that thou
haste, that is to say, parents, or father and mother, but that that thou haste
not, but lackest, thou sekest not, that is to say, how thou may please thy
father, and how thou may keep that he has gotten with his labor.
Inuenerit.i. parauerit.

Pudet dicere hoc praesente. I am ashamed to speak it in the presence of this
man.

At te illud nullo modo facere puduit. But thou were never a whytte at all
asshamed to do that.

Eheu quam ego nunc totus displiceo mihi. Alas how angry I am with my self, or
how sorry I am. Displiceo mihi in latin speaking is sometimes used in a much
like sense, as we use to sey in english, I am half naught, as thus. I was
weary of trauailyng, or after my journey, and was half naught and more, De uia
fessus era^, mihique totus displiceba^.

Quam pudet. s. me. How greattely ashamed I am.

In the fyft Scene.
Enimuero nimis grauiter cruciat adolescentulum, minusque inhumane. Truly he
vexes the poor young man to soore, and to unkindly or vngentylly.

Exeo ut pacem conciliem. s. inter eos. I comme forth to make peace or
attonemente between them.

Cur non accersi iubes filiam. s. meam. Why do thou not beade or co^mande my
daughter to be sent for?

Mi uir te obsecro ne facias. Sweet husband I pray you for gods sake do not
so.

Pater obsecro mihi ignoscas. Father I beseech you for gods sake, forgive or
pardone me.

Da ueniam. s. mihi. Forgive or pardon me.

Sine te exorem. Let me entrete you. Exorare, est orando impetrare, to
obtain with prayinge.

Sciens no^ facia^. I wol not do it wyttingly.

Id nos no^ sinemus. I wol not suffer it. Nos for ego is much used modestiae
causa, but uos for tu may not be used, ne honoris quidem gratiae.

Si me uiuum uis pater, ignosce. s. mihi. Fader if you love my life, forgive or
perdon me.

Ne tam offirma te. Be not so styffe, or be not so cursed hearted. For that is
offirmare any mu^, albeit it is used sometimes in the good pert.

Ealege hoc faciam. I woll do the thing upon this condition.

Omnia faciam, impera. I woll do al things that you shall think good,
command me. Omnia faciam. s. quae tu censes aequu^ esse ut faciam, for that
went next before.

Ad me recipio. I undertake it.

Haec dum incipias grauia sunt, dumque ignores, ubi cognoris, facilia. Al these
things be hard, till a man has begone or is entered in them, and as long as he
knows them not, but after that he is ones acqueynted with them, they be light.

Rufam ne illam uirginem, caesiam, sparso ore, adunco naso, ducam? Shall I
marye that reed heeded, gray eyed, platerfaced, and hawkenosed wenche? Caesius,
a, um, and glaucus, ca, cum, is blew or gray, as the sky is when it has little
spleckes of gray clouds in a fair day, as it were a plumbet color. Of the
names of colors read Aul. Gel. l. 2. noct. attic. ca, 26. Sparso ore.i. largo,
lato, amplo, large, broad, and (as we use to say in scorn or derision)
platerfaced. Adunco naso.i. in curuo, crokynge or bowynge inward like as the
bil of an egle or of an hauk, and such we call in scorn or derision hawknoses.

Eia ut elegans est. Eih what a minion it is.

Quando quidem ducenda est, egomet habeo propemodum, quam uolo. Sens that I
must needs marye one, I my self have in manner found her that I woll have.

Nunc laudo te gnate. Now I began the thank son.

Perplacet. s. mihi. I am very well contented, or it pleases me very well.

Hoc nunc restat. This now remains.

Syro ignoscas uolo, quae mea caussa fecit. I would have you to perdon Syrus,
of tho things that he has done for my cause, or for my sake. Syro ignoscas.
s. ea quae, etc.

Finis Heauton.
LONDINI IN AEDIBVS THO. BERTHELETI. M.D.XXXIII.

CVM PRIVILEGIO.
